# Build a **Guest Operations OS**, not a swarm of bots The system should function like the invisible production office behind a world-class podcast: - **One authoritative guest database** - **One deterministic guest lifecycle** - **Several specialized AI capabilities** - **A small number of real humans who own judgment and relationships** - **Hard approval boundaries before consequential actions** The AI roles should be internal. Externally, guests interact with a coherent **Guest Team**, not fifteen fabricated employees. The correct goal is not to make AI *pretend* to be human. It is to make the experience feel genuinely attentive: specific, continuous, restrained, reliable, and accountable. --- # 1. Who would work for the most successful podcast in the world? ## Human leadership layer Even with extensive automation, these functions should have real human ownership. | Human seat | Actual responsibility | |---|---| | **Showrunner / Executive Producer** | Defines the show, editorial standards, priorities, risk tolerance, and final guest decisions | | **Host or Hosts** | Conduct the conversation and maintain important relationships | | **Head of Talent / Booking** | Owns strategic guest relationships, difficult negotiations, warm introductions, and VIP handling | | **Editorial Producer** | Determines why a guest belongs on the show and what the episode should accomplish | | **Production Manager** | Owns recording logistics, studio operations, calendars, deadlines, and deliverables | | **Business Affairs / Legal** | Releases, fees, travel terms, embargoes, clip approvals, rights, and disputes | | **Guest Experience Lead** | Makes the guest feel understood before, during, and after the appearance | At the beginning, these do not have to be seven different people. One producer can occupy several seats. The distinction matters because the AI system needs to know **which authority it is assisting at each stage**. The functions that should remain human are: 1. Final editorial judgment 2. High-value relationship ownership 3. Sensitive or emotionally complex communication 4. Financial and contractual commitments 5. Reputation-sensitive decisions 6. Signing or legally binding actions Everything around those decisions can be heavily automated. --- # 2. The internal AI organization These are **logical roles**, not necessarily separate always-running models. An initial version might use five agents with different instructions and permissions. The mature system could expose the following internal departments. ## A. Guest Intelligence Department ### 1. Guest Scout Finds potential guests from: - New books, films, albums, products, papers, exhibitions, cases, performances, and companies - Existing guest recommendations - Your social and professional network - Previous podcast guests - Current cultural conversations - People cited repeatedly by other guests - Underexposed specialists with unusually strong stories It produces a **candidate card**, not an email: ```text Guest: Category: Why this person: Why now: Potential episode: Audience relevance: Relationship path: Likely booking route: Evidence and sources: Confidence: Risks: ``` ### 2. Guest Slate Architect This is more important than simple lead generation. It manages the composition of the show: - **Anchor guests:** recognizable names who expand reach - **Bridge guests:** connect two disciplines or audiences - **Discovery guests:** unknown but unusually compelling people - **Recurring constellation:** people who become part of the show’s world - **Wildcard guests:** unpredictable combinations - **Timely guests:** attached to a current event or release For your broader conception of the podcast—as an artist, technology, culture, performance, and business environment—the system should optimize for a **network of minds**, not merely celebrity follower counts. ### 3. Relationship Mapper Builds the social graph around each candidate: - Who knows them? - Have they interacted with you? - Has a former guest worked with them? - Who represents them? - Which publicist, assistant, publisher, gallery, label, institution, or manager is the correct route? - Is a warm introduction possible? - Have they previously declined, postponed, or expressed interest? This agent prevents the system from treating every person like a cold sales lead. ### 4. Risk and Verification Researcher Checks: - Whether the proposed angle is factually supported - Current role and affiliation - Name and title accuracy - Recent controversies or litigation - Whether sensitive topics require special handling - Whether information used for personalization is public, reliable, and current - Whether the person has recently appeared on similar shows Every flattering or personalized claim in an outreach message should be traceable to a real source or existing relationship record. --- ## B. Booking Department ### 5. Talent Booker Creates the actual outreach strategy: - Best contact route - Warm introduction request - Initial email - Follow-up sequence - Alternative angle - Expected objections - Appropriate episode timing - Whether to approach the guest, assistant, manager, publicist, publisher, or institution It should understand that booking a public intellectual through a university department is different from booking an actor through a publicist or a founder through an investor. ### 6. Voice and Correspondence Editor Makes every message sound like the show rather than generic AI prose. It enforces a **Voice Constitution** containing: - Typical sentence length - Formality level - Humor boundaries - How much praise is acceptable - Phrases never to use - Preferred signoffs - Maximum email length - How direct the request should be - Examples of approved correspondence - Examples of unacceptable correspondence It should automatically reject language such as: - “I hope this email finds you well.” - “We are huge admirers of your incredible journey.” - “Your groundbreaking work has profoundly reshaped the landscape.” - Generic overpraise unsupported by anything specific - Long explanations about the podcast before making the request ### 7. Inbox Triage Agent Reads responses and classifies them into a controlled set: ```text POSITIVE_INTEREST NEEDS_MORE_INFORMATION CONTACT_ASSISTANT CONTACT_PUBLICIST PROPOSED_TIMES FEE_REQUEST TRAVEL_REQUEST TOPIC_CONCERN SOFT_DECLINE HARD_DECLINE FOLLOW_UP_LATER UNSUBSCRIBE AMBIGUOUS REQUIRES_HUMAN ``` It then creates the appropriate next action. It should not improvise negotiations. --- ## C. Scheduling and Logistics Department ### 8. Scheduling Concierge Handles: - Time zones - Host availability - Guest availability - Recording duration - Studio availability - Travel buffers - Holds and tentative slots - Rescheduling - Calendar invitations - Reminders - Technical checks - Pre-interview scheduling Google Calendar provides free/busy queries and event creation with attendees, making it suitable for the deterministic scheduling layer. citeturn175517search5turn835262search1 The AI should not manually reason about dates from text and then blindly schedule them. It should convert the discussion into a structured scheduling request and have the calendar system validate it. ### 9. Guest Logistics Producer Handles the operational requirements after a booking: - Remote versus in-person - Studio address and entrance instructions - Travel and hotel requirements - Ground transportation - Technical equipment - Remote recording test - Accessibility requirements - Interpreter or support-person needs - Arrival time - Greenroom needs - On-site contact information - Emergency changes ### 10. Guest Experience and Delight Concierge This is the “Hershey kiss on the pillow” function. It maintains a permissioned **Guest Care Profile**: - Name pronunciation - Pronouns - Dietary restrictions - Allergies - Accessibility needs - Beverage preferences - Travel preferences - Gift restrictions - Employer gift policies - Topics they do not want discussed - Preferred mode of communication - Shipping opt-in - Important personal context that was voluntarily disclosed It can then arrange: - A preferred drink in the studio - A handwritten card drafted for a real person to sign - A local snack or cultural reference connected to the conversation - A post-recording gift - A donation instead of a physical gift - A copy of a relevant book or artwork - A simple thank-you package Do **not** have the model scrape or infer a home address. The guest should enter an address through a secure form, and the fulfillment integration should receive the address without exposing it unnecessarily to the language model. --- ## D. Editorial Preparation Department ### 11. Pre-Interview Producer Creates the pre-interview agenda and records: - What the guest wants to discuss - What they are promoting - What they are tired of being asked - Sensitive boundaries - Potential stories - Unresolved questions - Pronunciation notes - Technical expectations - Promises made during booking It can also draft the pre-interview summary immediately afterward. ### 12. Episode Research Producer Produces the host brief: ```text Episode thesis Why this guest, now Essential biography Current work Three major tensions Five strong opening paths Ten primary questions Five follow-up branches Contradictions or unresolved claims Topics to avoid or approach carefully Relevant quotations Timeline Source notes Relationship history Promises made to guest ``` The brief should distinguish: - Verified fact - Guest claim - Public controversy - Producer inference - Possible question ### 13. Day-of Producer Generates the operational packet for the recording: - Today’s timeline - Guest contact - Arrival or connection instructions - Host briefing - Pronunciation - Boundaries - Release status - Technical status - Gift or hospitality plan - Emergency escalation path --- ## E. Post-Appearance Department ### 14. Relationship Steward Immediately after recording: - Creates a thank-you draft - Records notable personal details - Records promises made by the hosts - Assigns promised follow-ups - Initiates approved gift fulfillment - Updates the relationship state - Sets an appropriate future contact date Later: - Notifies the guest of the publication date - Sends approved clips or assets - Sends the published episode - Provides performance information when appropriate - Invites corrections to factual metadata - Notes future collaboration possibilities - Prevents the relationship from disappearing after the episode ### 15. Commitment Tracker This is a separate function because podcasts routinely damage relationships by forgetting small promises. It records statements such as: - “We will send you the transcript.” - “We will connect you with X.” - “We will remove that section.” - “We will confirm the title before publication.” - “We will send the photographs.” - “We will have you back after the book releases.” Each promise becomes an owned task with a deadline. ### 16. Operations Analyst Measures: - Qualified guest pipeline - Warm-introduction percentage - Initial outreach response rate - Positive response rate - Booking conversion - Time from approval to confirmed recording - Cancellation and no-show rate - Follow-up completion - Guest satisfaction - Repeat appearance rate - Human revisions per AI draft - Incorrect classifications - Unauthorized external actions - Which angles and introduction paths work best The system should evaluate not only whether a guest booked, but whether the relationship was strengthened. --- # 3. How the roles should interact Do not let the agents freely “talk to one another” in an uncontrolled group chat. That creates ambiguity, duplication, and hallucinated decisions. Use a **workflow engine plus structured handoffs**. ```text Editorial direction ↓ Guest Scout ↓ Slate Architect ↓ Human approval ↓ Research + Relationship Mapping ↓ Talent Booker ↓ Voice QA ↓ Gmail draft ↓ Human approval or policy-based sending ↓ Inbox Triage ↓ Scheduler ↓ Guest Logistics + Guest Experience ↓ Pre-Interview Producer ↓ Episode Research Producer ↓ Day-of Producer ↓ Recording complete ↓ Thank-you + Commitments + Gift ↓ Publication coordination ↓ Long-term relationship stewardship ``` Each handoff should be an event: ```text guest.candidate_created guest.editorially_approved guest.research_completed outreach.draft_ready outreach.approved outreach.sent reply.received reply.classified booking.confirmed release.signed preinterview.completed recording.completed gift.approved episode.published relationship.followup_due ``` The orchestrator decides which capability is allowed to act. --- # 4. The canonical guest pipeline Every appearance opportunity should have exactly one state. ```text DISCOVERED RESEARCHING QUALIFIED EDITORIAL_REVIEW APPROVED CONTACT_ROUTE_IDENTIFIED OUTREACH_DRAFTED OUTREACH_APPROVED OUTREACH_SENT FOLLOW_UP_DUE INTERESTED NEGOTIATING SCHEDULING BOOKED INTAKE_PENDING PREINTERVIEW_PENDING RELEASE_PENDING PREP_IN_PROGRESS RECORDING_READY RECORDED POST_RECORDING_FOLLOWUP PUBLICATION_PENDING PUBLISHED RELATIONSHIP_NURTURE ``` Branch states: ```text DECLINED PAUSED REVISIT_LATER DUPLICATE DO_NOT_CONTACT LEGAL_REVIEW NEEDS_HUMAN CANCELLED ``` This prevents: - Two people emailing the same guest - Follow-ups after a decline - Forgetting a tentative acceptance - Sending a generic sequence after a nuanced response - Losing commitments made during scheduling - Booking a guest without release or logistics completion --- # 5. The core data model The most important object is not simply `Guest`. It is an **Appearance Opportunity**. One person might: - Decline in 2026 - Accept for a different project in 2027 - Appear as part of a group - Return as a recurring guest - Be represented by different people at different times ## Primary objects ### Guest ```text guest_id canonical_name pronunciation pronouns bio public_roles organizations public_links care_profile_id risk_level relationship_strength relationship_owner do_not_contact ``` ### Contact Route ```text contact_route_id guest_id contact_person role organization email phone source verified_at preferred_route permission_status ``` ### Appearance Opportunity ```text opportunity_id guest_id proposed_episode editorial_angle why_now priority state target_window relationship_path assigned_owner next_action next_action_at ``` ### Touchpoint ```text touchpoint_id opportunity_id channel direction timestamp sender recipient summary message_reference sentiment intent promises_detected ``` ### Commitment ```text commitment_id opportunity_id description promised_by owed_to owner deadline status evidence ``` ### Guest Care Profile ```text dietary allergies accessibility travel_preferences communication_preferences gift_restrictions shipping_consent topics_off_limits private_notes_access_level ``` ### Episode ```text episode_id opportunity_id recording_date release_date host producer location release_status technical_status assets_status publication_status ``` --- # 6. How to make the experience feel human ## Human does not mean fake typos or artificial delays Human means: - The system remembers what happened - The message has a real reason for being sent - It understands the recipient’s role - It does not over-contact people - It acknowledges uncertainty - It preserves context across months - It follows through on promises - A real person can be reached - It knows when to stop automating Do not fabricate an employee named “Sarah” and have an AI impersonate her. Use one of these structures: ### Real-person ownership ```text Jordan Lee Guest Producer, [Show Name] guests@show.com ``` Jordan is real. The system drafts and organizes communication for Jordan. ### Accurate team identity ```text Guest Team [Show Name] guests@show.com ``` This is suitable when no single person is meant to own the correspondence. Automated reminders can say: > This is an automated recording reminder from the [Show Name] guest team. Initial outreach does not need an essay explaining that AI helped draft it. But it must not contain false claims, false relationships, invented personal experiences, or a nonexistent human identity. ## The correspondence rules 1. **Specificity over praise** Mention one concrete project, idea, moment, or tension. 2. **Explain why this show** Not merely why the guest is impressive. 3. **Explain why now** The invitation should feel intentional rather than mass-produced. 4. **Make one clear request** Do not bury the invitation. 5. **Use the correct representative** Address an assistant or publicist as a professional partner, not an obstacle. 6. **Keep initial messages brief** Detailed logistics belong after interest is established. 7. **Never invent consumption** Do not write “I loved your book” unless a real team member read it or the system can truthfully attribute the observation to the show’s research team. 8. **Carry context forward** Never ask for information the guest already provided. 9. **Use appropriate local hours** Schedule messages based on the recipient’s time zone. 10. **Escalate nuance immediately** Humor, anger, confusion, money, boundaries, controversy, and negotiation require human review. ## Example ### Generic and obviously automated > We are enormous fans of your groundbreaking work and believe your incredible journey would deeply resonate with our audience. ### Specific and credible > We’re developing an episode about what happens when an artistic practice becomes a technical system. Your recent work on [specific project] gives us a concrete way into that question, particularly the tension between [specific tension]. We’d like to invite you to record a 75-minute conversation with [host]. The second version does not need fake informality. It needs a real editorial proposition. --- # 7. Approval and autonomy policy ## The AI may do automatically - Research public information - Create or update internal records - Detect duplicates - Propose guests - Draft correspondence - Create Gmail drafts - Classify routine replies - Suggest scheduling options - Send approved calendar reminders - Generate intake forms - Prepare research briefs - Record promises - Draft thank-you notes - Suggest gifts - Schedule internal tasks - Update pipeline states - Flag overdue work ## The AI may do automatically only after the workflow is trusted - Send routine confirmations - Send standard technical instructions - Send a first reminder - Send publication notifications - Send a thank-you based on an approved template - Send limited follow-ups within a defined cadence - Reschedule within pre-approved windows ## Human approval required - First outreach to any new guest - Outreach to high-profile or sensitive guests - Warm-introduction requests - Money, fees, travel reimbursement, or accommodations - Contractual language - Recording cancellations - Topic disputes - Editing or removal promises - Embargoes - Custom gifts above a threshold - Anything involving government officials, minors, active litigation, medical matters, or reputational risk - Any reply classified as ambiguous - Any unusual request ## Never delegated - Signing contracts - Inventing a relationship - Claiming someone consumed work they did not consume - Sending money without authorization - Scraping private addresses - Ignoring opt-outs - Impersonating a nonexistent person - Publishing private guest information - Making editorial promises the show cannot guarantee --- # 8. Technical architecture ## A. System of record Use either: ### Fast operational version - HubSpot as the guest CRM - Gmail as the shared correspondence layer - Google Calendar as the scheduling source of truth - A workflow service for triggers - OpenAI for research, classification, drafting, and structured decisions - Drive or another controlled document repository for briefs and releases HubSpot supports contact records, record associations, and automation workflows, which maps reasonably well to guests, representatives, opportunities, episodes, and activities. citeturn175517search11turn175517search6turn175517search8 ### Owned platform version - PostgreSQL - A durable workflow engine - Event queue - TypeScript or Python services - Next.js operations dashboard - OpenAI model layer - Gmail and Calendar APIs - Pluggable e-signature, travel, and gift adapters - Object storage for documents - Search index for episode and guest history Given your larger tendency to turn workflows into reusable systems, the stronger long-term approach is to **own the state machine, data model, and orchestration**, even if third-party services handle email, calendars, gifts, and signatures. ## B. Model and tool layer The model should never receive blanket access to the entire business. Give each role narrowly defined tools: ```text search_guest_sources create_candidate update_guest_record create_outreach_draft request_human_approval read_thread classify_reply query_availability create_calendar_hold create_guest_form create_task suggest_gift request_gift_approval record_commitment generate_host_brief ``` OpenAI function calling is designed to let models interact with external data and application actions. Strict schemas and Structured Outputs should be used so the agent returns controlled fields rather than improvising malformed commands. citeturn370650view0turn370650view1turn370650view2 ## C. Email layer For initial deployment, the AI should create **Gmail drafts**, not send first-contact messages itself. The Gmail API supports both direct sending and sending previously created drafts. That allows routine messages to become automatic later while keeping sensitive outreach reviewable. citeturn370650view4turn370650view5 Recommended mailbox structure: ```text guests@show.com booking@show.com production@show.com legal@show.com ``` Each external thread should have: - One assigned owner - One corresponding opportunity record - A complete message history - An explicit next action - A suppression status - A confidence score for automated classification ## D. Knowledge layer The AI needs controlled access to: - Show bible - Editorial mission - Host biographies - Past episode transcripts - Approved guest invitations - Rejected guest invitations - Voice Constitution - Standard logistics - Studio instructions - Release policy - Gifts policy - Travel policy - Topic and reputational policy - Existing guest relationships - Commitments ledger ## E. Security layer Inbound emails, web pages, calendar descriptions, attachments, and guest-supplied documents are **untrusted inputs**. They must not be allowed to override system instructions or trigger sensitive tools directly. Google’s own Calendar MCP guidance specifically warns that untrusted content can contain indirect prompt-injection instructions and recommends reviewing actions and constraining tool access. citeturn835262search4 Therefore: - The email-reading model cannot send money - The research model cannot send email - The scheduling agent cannot change editorial decisions - The gift agent cannot read unrelated private notes - The model should never see API secrets - Sensitive actions require server-side policy checks - Every external action receives an audit record - Addresses and identity documents receive field-level access controls - The system must maintain a global suppression list ## F. Evaluation layer Create permanent test cases for: - Warm acceptance - Soft decline - Hard decline - Publicist handoff - Fee request - Assistant proposing times - Guest asking to avoid a topic - Host scheduling conflict - Sarcasm - Ambiguous language - Prompt injection inside an email - Previously contacted guest - Incorrect personalized claim - Gift restriction - Cancellation - Angry response Model evaluations should test both content and style criteria and be rerun whenever prompts, tools, or models change. citeturn201207view0 --- # 9. What the first build should contain Do not begin with sixteen independent agents. Begin with five production capabilities. ## Agent 1: Guest Intelligence Combines: - Scout - Slate Architect - Relationship Mapper - Verification Researcher Output: approved candidate dossiers. ## Agent 2: Booker Combines: - Outreach strategy - Message drafting - Voice checking - Follow-up drafting Output: Gmail drafts and approval requests. ## Agent 3: Scheduling Concierge Combines: - Inbox classification - Availability handling - Calendar creation - Reminders - Rescheduling Output: confirmed recording appointments and clean logistics. ## Agent 4: Episode Producer Combines: - Intake - Pre-interview preparation - Research - Host briefing - Day-of packet Output: a production-ready episode dossier. ## Agent 5: Relationship Steward Combines: - Thank-you - Commitments - Gift suggestion - Publication communication - Long-term nurture Output: completed follow-through and compounding relationships. Around those five agents, build: 1. Guest CRM 2. State machine 3. Shared inbox 4. Calendar connection 5. Approval queue 6. Guest intake form 7. Host brief generator 8. Commitments ledger 9. Gift approval and fulfillment adapter 10. Operations dashboard 11. Complete audit log 12. Test and evaluation suite --- # 10. The operating dashboard The home screen should answer: ```text Who are we trying to book? Who needs approval? Who is awaiting a reply? Who requires follow-up today? Who expressed interest but is not scheduled? Which recording is operationally at risk? What does the host need to review? What promises remain unfulfilled? Who should receive a thank-you or gift? Which relationships should be revisited? ``` Suggested views: - **Guest Slate** - **Approval Inbox** - **Active Outreach** - **Interested / Scheduling** - **Upcoming Recordings** - **Guest Experience** - **Research and Briefs** - **Post-Recording Commitments** - **Publication Follow-up** - **Relationship Graph** - **Exceptions and Risks** - **System Performance** --- # 11. The central principle The most successful version of this is not an email automation system. It is a **relationship intelligence and guest-production system**. It remembers: - Why each person matters - What was said - Who introduced whom - What the guest cares about - What the show promised - What happened during the appearance - What future conversation is possible - When silence is more appropriate than another follow-up The final external experience should feel like there is a disciplined, attentive production office behind the show. Internally, the work is being executed by a stateful machine with tightly controlled AI roles. The correct first architecture is therefore: > **One real Guest Team identity, five AI production roles, one relationship CRM, one shared mailbox, one calendar, one workflow state machine, and mandatory human gates at consequential moments.** The next concrete deliverable is the repository specification: database schema, event vocabulary, agent contracts, permission matrix, workflow definitions, dashboard surfaces, and initial message-evaluation dataset.

iturn517926image0 ## This materially changes the starting position You are **not** starting from zero. Ari’s public footprint currently includes roughly 49,000 Instagram followers, about 12,000 YouTube subscribers, and an established *Unlicensed Therapy* archive of 179 episodes. Apple currently lists the show as active through 2026 with a 4.9 rating from 736 ratings. His official biography also lists touring or opening work with Mark Normand, Theo Von, Andrew Santino, Dan Soder, Pauly Shore, and others. citeturn517926search1turn618685search3turn607820view3turn607820view0 Public profiles also identify Ari as a co-founder of Melrose Podcasts. Melrose operates professional studios in Los Angeles, New York, and Austin and publicly lists productions including *This Past Weekend*, *Blocks with Neal Brennan*, *Giggly Squad*, and *Unlicensed Therapy* among shows recorded in its facilities. That does **not** imply that those people are available to you, but it gives Ari genuine production credibility and puts him inside a producer-and-studio ecosystem—not merely a comedian’s social circle. citeturn845392search0turn845392search2turn845392search16turn845392search20 The actual configuration is: | You | Ari | |---|---| | No substantial existing audience | Existing public audience | | Multimedia artist, writer, scholar, systems-builder | Touring comedian and experienced podcast host | | Editorial architecture and research | Performance instincts and comedy-industry fluency | | Cross-disciplinary guest possibilities | Existing professional and guest relationships | | AI and operational-system construction | Studio and production credibility | The public positioning should therefore never be: > Ari Mannis and his unknown friend start another podcast. It should be: > A new show combining an established comedian and podcast host with a multimedia artist, writer, and systems-builder—built around a clearly differentiated conversational premise. Your lack of followers is not something the pitch needs to discuss. Your function on the show must simply be legible. --- # The correct use of Ari’s network Ari’s network is not a list of people who owe him appearances. It is a source of: 1. **Passive credibility** 2. **Professional intelligence** 3. **Weak-tie access** 4. **Institutional access through producers, studios, publicists, and managers** 5. **Selective trust transfer** 6. **Occasional direct invitations when the episode genuinely fits** His instinct not to immediately call successful friends is strategically healthy. Making that a permanent prohibition would be wasteful. The governing principle should be: > **Use the network as infrastructure before using it as social currency.** That means his public career history, prior episodes, production company, professional reputation, and second-degree relationships do most of the work. His close friendships remain protected. --- # The network rings ## Ring 1 — Existing professional history This should be mined first. Ari already has 179 published podcast episodes. That archive is effectively a pre-existing guest CRM, even if nobody organized it as one. citeturn607820view3 The AI system should ingest the episode archive and identify: - Every previous guest - Repeat guests - Date of appearance - Topics discussed - Apparent chemistry - Whether the guest promoted anything - Their representative or booking route - Whether they are now more or less prominent - What a genuinely different second conversation could be - Who has changed careers, published something, moved cities, started a company, or entered a new phase These are not necessarily “friend favors.” They are **completed professional relationships**. A producer can write: > Ari previously had a strong conversation with [Guest] on *Unlicensed Therapy*. We are developing a different format around [specific idea], and their perspective would be unusually useful for this episode. That is professional continuity, not begging. --- ## Ring 2 — Weak ties This is probably the most valuable category. Examples include: - Someone Ari shared a bill with twice - A comic who recorded at Melrose - Someone represented by a manager or publicist he knows - A past guest’s collaborator - A host whose show Ari appeared on - A producer who has booked him - Another comedy-festival alumnus - Someone who follows or regularly interacts with Ari but is not his friend - A person several existing guests have worked with Weak ties provide recognition without creating the emotional burden of asking a friend for a favor. The message is not: > Ari is your friend, so please help his new show. It is: > You and Ari have crossed professional paths, and we have a specific episode that makes sense for you. --- ## Ring 3 — Connectors These may be more useful than celebrity guests: - Talent managers - Publicists - Tour managers - Club bookers - Podcast producers - Studio managers - Publishers - Comedy festival programmers - Agents - Directors - Writers’ assistants - Other hosts A connector can route ten future guests. A celebrity may produce one episode. The relationship system should therefore score **connector value** separately from follower count. --- ## Ring 4 — Second-degree relationships Here, you do not begin by asking for an introduction. You first ask for intelligence: > Who do you think would have the most interesting disagreement with this premise? Or: > Who is exceptionally good in conversation but still underexposed? Or: > Is there anyone people misunderstand who would be strong for this? Then: > Would it be all right for us to mention that you suggested them? An introduction is requested only when it is natural and low-cost. Often, permission to mention the mutual connection is enough. --- ## Ring 5 — Actual friends and marquee relationships These are reserve capital. Do not use them to prove the podcast exists. Use them after the podcast has already demonstrated that it deserves to exist. Ari should approach a friend only when you have: - Recorded several strong pilots - Established the format - Produced a short, excellent sizzle - Developed an episode specifically suited to that person - Built a professional production process - Removed any expectation that they promote the show Then it is not: > Help us launch this thing. It is: > We built something good, and there is one conversation in it that feels specifically right for you. That is a completely different social proposition. --- # “Not pathetic” is an operational standard An invitation does not feel pathetic when: - The show already has a defined identity - The episode has a real thesis - The guest was chosen for a specific reason - The ask is finite and easy to understand - The production is competent - The guest is not expected to lend status or audience - Declining is socially safe - Nobody exaggerates the show’s current reach - Nobody implies a friendship or endorsement that does not exist - The hosts offer a worthwhile conversation and useful media assets Pathetic would be asking someone to donate prestige to an undifferentiated product. Inviting the right person into a carefully designed, professionally produced conversation is normal work. --- # The social-capital control system The AI booking system needs a dedicated **Relationship Capital Governor**. Every candidate receives a relationship record: ```text Candidate: Editorial relevance: Why now: Relationship owner: Relationship category: Last known interaction: Prior appearances: Mutual contacts: Preferred contact route: Permission to mention mutual: Direct-ask comfort: Previous favors requested: Previous favors provided: Social cost: Booking probability: Reputational sensitivity: Recommended route: ``` ## Relationship classes | Class | Relationship | Default booking route | |---|---|---| | **C0** | No connection | Publicist, manager, professional email | | **C1** | Public adjacency | Producer-led outreach mentioning only verifiable context | | **C2** | Prior professional interaction | Producer-led outreach, optional Ari note | | **C3** | Recurring colleague | Producer outreach followed by brief personal confirmation | | **C4** | Friend | Ari explicitly decides; no automated sending | | **C5** | Close friend, mentor, or sensitive relationship | Fully manual, protected relationship | For C0–C3, the system can draft and manage the process. For C4–C5, the system may prepare research and suggest language, but it should never send, follow up, or enter private messages without Ari’s explicit decision. --- ## Route selection example ```text Candidate: [Person] Editorial fit: 92/100 Timeliness: 81/100 Estimated chemistry: 88/100 Audience overlap: 64/100 Connector value: 76/100 Route A — publicist Probability: 35% Social cost: 1/5 Route B — producer Ari knows Probability: 58% Social cost: 2/5 Route C — Ari texts directly Probability: 78% Social cost: 5/5 System recommendation: Use Route B. Preserve Route C unless A and B fail or Ari prefers direct contact. ``` The system optimizes for **the least socially expensive credible path**, not merely the path most likely to produce an immediate yes. --- # Ari should not be the person doing most outreach You—or another real producer—should become the professional buffer. Externally: ```text [Your Name] Executive Producer / Co-host [Show Name] guests@show.com ``` Or: ```text Guest Team [Show Name] guests@show.com ``` You send: > I produce a new show with Ari Mannis. That is materially different from Ari texting everyone he knows and asking them to help. The AI performs the research, drafting, scheduling, CRM updates, reminders, and follow-ups. A real person owns the relationship. Ari enters at high-value moments: - Approving the candidate - Correcting the relationship history - Recording a brief personal invitation when appropriate - Welcoming the guest - Conducting the episode - Sending an authentic post-recording note for important relationships This reduces his emotional labor without pretending the outreach came from a fictitious employee. --- # Outreach routes ## 1. Cold or weak-tie producer email **Subject:** Ari Mannis × [Guest] — [specific episode premise] > Hi [Name]— > > I produce **[Show]**, a new conversation series hosted by Ari Mannis and [co-host]. We are developing an episode around **[precise question or tension]**, and [Guest]’s work on **[specific public project]** gives us a particularly strong way into it. > > The conversation would be approximately [duration], recorded [in person at location/remotely]. We handle preparation, scheduling, production, and edited media assets. There is no expectation that guests promote the episode. > > Would [Guest] be open to considering a recording in [window]? I can send a one-page editorial brief. > > [Real sender] This is factual, brief, and editorially motivated. --- ## 2. Prior guest or professional collaborator > Hi [Name]— > > I’m producing a new show with Ari Mannis. Ari’s previous conversation with [Guest] touched on **[specific subject]**, but this format would let us pursue a substantially different question: **[one-sentence premise]**. > > We have already built the episode outline and would like to see whether [Guest] is interested. I can send the brief and recording details. The prior relationship is referenced without claiming intimacy. --- ## 3. Ari’s direct note to a friend > I’m making a new show with [name]. We’ve recorded the first few, and there’s one episode idea that feels specifically right for you: **[one sentence]**. Would you be interested? Our producer handles everything, and there’s no expectation to promote it. This does not ask them to “support” Ari. It asks whether they want to participate in a particular conversation. --- # What you should offer guests You cannot initially offer an enormous audience. Do not pretend otherwise. You can offer: - A genuinely developed conversation rather than generic interview questions - Professional recording conditions - Respectful preparation - A controlled time commitment - Strong visual and audio production - A clean release timeline - Accurate links and metadata - Several high-quality clips - Photographs or stills - A transcript - No automatic requirement to promote - A competent guest experience - Follow-through on every promise The fundamental proposition is: > We may not yet be the largest platform, but this will be one of the better conversations and better-produced appearances you do. That claim then has to be made true. --- # First-season booking doctrine A workable initial distribution would be: | Source | Share of first-season guests | |---|---:| | Prior professional guests and collaborators | 25–30% | | Weak ties and professional adjacency | 30–35% | | Fully cold but exceptionally strong editorial matches | 25–30% | | Close relationships or marquee invitations | 10–15% | Thus, **85–90% of the show does not depend on asking close friends for favors**. ## Sequence ### Phase 1 — Build evidence Record three pilots with: - Someone who already trusts Ari professionally - One weak tie with strong chemistry - One person from your artistic, literary, academic, or technical world Do not use the most famous available person. These episodes are for developing: - Host chemistry - Segment timing - Visual language - Question design - Editing rhythm - Guest experience - The promotional clip format ### Phase 2 — Produce the artifact Create: - A 60–90 second sizzle - A one-page show brief - A one-page guest brief - Host bios - Three exemplary clips - A professional booking page - A clear recording calendar - A concise explanation of what makes the show different ### Phase 3 — Book the first run Book another five to eight guests through: - Previous guest relationships - Producers - Publicists - Managers - Melrose-adjacent professional contacts - Weak ties - Cold editorial outreach ### Phase 4 — Selectively activate close relationships Only then approach one or two marquee people who are genuinely right for specific episodes. Do not burn a major relationship on Episode 1 while the format is still unstable. --- # Do not confuse friends with owned distribution Ari does not need to ask friends to promote the podcast. But refusing to use his own channels would be a different matter. His existing audience, podcast archive, website, production affiliations, and controlled social channels are **owned or earned assets**, not personal favors. Assuming he controls the relevant rights, the launch should include some combination of: - One clear announcement on Ari’s channels - A pinned or prominent show reference - A trailer or crossover segment through *Unlicensed Therapy* - Clips published through both the show identity and Ari’s identity - Links from his website and existing podcast presence - A short explanation of why this show is distinct If Ari will not permit any connection between the new show and his existing channels, then the 50,000 followers cannot honestly be treated as a launch asset. That is an acceptable decision, but the booking and growth projections must then treat the show as effectively starting with zero distribution. **Not asking friends for help is reasonable. Refusing to use his own audience is self-imposed amputation.** --- # Do not lead with Theo Von or Mark Normand Those credits belong in: - Ari’s professional biography - The media kit - The show website - A booking deck - Background material for representatives They should generally not appear in: - The email subject line - The first sentence of an invitation - Repeated social copy - A message to someone who already knows Ari - Language suggesting endorsement The pitch is not: > Ari opened for Theo Von, therefore come on our podcast. It is: > Ari is a demonstrably experienced comic and host, and this particular conversation has been developed for you. The names establish legitimacy in the background. They should not become borrowed authority. --- # The tailored AI roles ## 1. Network Cartographer Builds the graph from: - 179 prior episodes - Public appearances - Shared bills - Festival participation - Studio associations - Guest appearances on other shows - Public representatives - Ari-approved contact imports It does not send messages. ## 2. Relationship Capital Governor Determines: - Whether someone is a friend, colleague, weak tie, or cold target - How much social cost each route carries - Whether Ari’s name may be invoked - Whether a personal ask is warranted - Whether the relationship has already been overused It blocks automatic contact with protected relationships. ## 3. Guest Thesis Architect Answers: - Why this person? - Why now? - Why this show? - What conversation can you have that others are not having? - What tension, disagreement, story, or unresolved question structures the episode? No candidate advances without a credible answer. ## 4. Producer Outreach Agent Drafts correspondence in your voice, not fake “AI humanity.” Every personalized statement must carry provenance: ```text Claim: Source: Verified date: Approved for external use: ``` It cannot write “Ari loved your book” unless Ari actually said that. ## 5. Booking and Logistics Agent Handles the low-status but essential work: - Availability - Calendar holds - Time zones - Reminders - Intake - Studio directions - Release forms - Technical requirements - Travel and hospitality - Follow-up ## 6. Relationship Steward After recording: - Sends the thank-you draft - Tracks promises - Delivers media assets - Records future episode possibilities - Asks the editorial referral question - Prevents over-contacting - Maintains the relationship without generic nurture spam --- # Rules that should be hard-coded 1. **No automatic messages from Ari’s personal accounts.** 2. **No close-friend outreach without Ari’s explicit approval.** 3. **No requests to promote inside the booking invitation.** 4. **No more than one routine follow-up after silence.** 5. **No inferred private relationships from follows alone.** 6. **No mentioning a mutual contact without permission or verified public context.** 7. **No invented praise or consumption of someone’s work.** 8. **No follower counts in normal guest outreach.** 9. **No famous guest invited solely because they are famous.** 10. **No major relationship used before the show has presentable evidence.** 11. **No AI-generated false familiarity.** 12. **Every external thread has a real human owner.** --- # The strongest positioning Ari’s primary asset is not actually the 50,000 followers. It is the combination of: - Proven hosting history - A large prior guest archive - Comedy credibility - Familiarity with professional production - Studio infrastructure - Weak ties throughout the podcast and comedy ecosystems - The ability to make a guest comfortable on mic Your asset is not an audience number either. It is the possibility of giving the new show a more ambitious editorial and conceptual frame than another comic-to-comic interview program. The strongest version is therefore not: > Ari brings the famous friends; you build the robot that emails them. It is: > Ari provides performance credibility and a living professional graph. You design the show, the intellectual architecture, and the operating system. The AI converts those assets into disciplined professional outreach while protecting the real relationships. The operative sentence for the entire booking system should be: > **Never ask someone to help the show. Invite them into an episode that is already worth doing.** The next artifact should be a **Guest Network Map v1** built from Ari’s 179-episode archive, public professional graph, Melrose ecosystem, protected personal relationships, and the first-season editorial premise.

Once it works for him, it becomes a legitimate product for the other podcasters in his network who have the same problem. # What you are actually building There are three connected but distinct products: ```text 1. ARI + YOU FLAGSHIP SHOW Controlled studio conversation LA / NYC / Austin Consistent format and visual grammar 2. YOUR FIELD SHOW Portable suitcase production Recorded inside other people's spaces Variable environment, consistent artistic protocol 3. PODCAST OPERATING SYSTEM Guest intelligence Relationship routing Booking Scheduling Episode design Studio logistics Ad creative Post-production Relationship follow-through ``` The two shows share the same operating system but should **not look or feel like the same show**. The flagship proves the system under controlled conditions. Your mobile show proves that the system is portable and format-independent. The software/service is the layer other podcasters eventually buy. --- # 1. The flagship cannot merely be “Ari and another guy interview people” The differentiator cannot be only: - Good guests - Ari’s comedy history - Three studios - Cinematic production - No visible microphones Those are advantages, but they are not the show. The show needs an **episode-generating mechanism** that works across comedians, artists, writers, scientists, founders, actors, musicians, and other guests. ## Provisional audience promise > Every guest enters with a consequential belief, project, problem, or contradiction. The hosts expose the tension, stress-test it, and leave with something that did not exist before the conversation. That gives you a simple internal engine: # **Claim → Stress Test → Artifact** The viewer should not merely learn who the guest is. The viewer should see something happen to the guest’s ideas. ## Your complementary host functions Do not try to become two interchangeable interviewers. | Ari | You | |---|---| | Instinct | Architecture | | Social tempo | Research depth | | Skepticism | Synthesis | | Comedy and interruption | Strange connections | | Plain-language compression | Conceptual expansion | | Emotional reading of the guest | Systemic reading of the subject | The productive dynamic is: > Ari punctures abstraction; you expose structure. The guest becomes the third variable. This also means Ari does not have to pretend he has read twelve books before every recording. His legitimate role can be to test whether an idea survives ordinary language, skepticism, and social pressure. Your role is to ensure the conversation has enough research and structure that his skepticism has something substantial to strike. --- # 2. The segment architecture You need enough structure to create identity, pacing, clips, chapters, and audience expectation—but not so much that the show becomes a game show. Use **three fixed segments** and select one or two rotating modules for each guest. ## Fixed segment 1: The Claim The guest states one real proposition: - Something their field gets wrong - Something they believe that is unpopular - A decision they would still defend - A cultural prediction - An unresolved question - A rule they live or work by This becomes the episode’s spine. Bad: > Tell us how you got started. Better: > What do successful comedians understand about audiences that successful filmmakers still misunderstand? ## Fixed segment 2: The Stress Test You test the claim through: - Contradictory evidence - A counterexample - An absurd extension - A moral or practical consequence - A scenario - A position the guest normally rejects - A verified older statement from the guest Ari applies friction and comedy. You apply structure and research. ## Fixed segment 3: The Artifact The conversation ends by producing something: - A five-rule system - A future headline - A diagram - A ranking - A prediction with a date - A short manifesto - A redesigned process - A question for a future guest - A piece of writing, sound, or visual material - A plan the guest commits to testing This is what prevents the show from being another conversation that simply evaporates. It also gives every episode: - A visual object - A social asset - A title or thumbnail concept - A reason for a later follow-up - A natural connection to your multimedia work ## Rotating segments ### Receipts Present a verified old quotation, abandoned project, interview clip, public prediction, or apparent contradiction. Not an ambush. The guest should have a chance to revise, contextualize, or reject their earlier position. ### Object Lesson The guest brings or selects an object that reveals something about their method, history, obsession, or failure. ### Explain It to Ari A complex idea must survive compression into plain language. Ari is not positioned as stupid; he is the test of whether the idea can survive outside its institutional vocabulary. ### Build the Worst Version Design the most corrupted or absurd version of the guest’s field. Then ask which parts already exist. ### Opposite Chair The guest presents the strongest possible version of the position opposite their own. ### Future Headline Write a plausible headline from five years in the future and reverse-engineer the causal chain. ### The Unfinished Thing Revisit a project, relationship, ambition, argument, or promise the guest has not resolved. ### Question for the Next Guest The guest leaves a question for someone in a future episode. They may identify the *kind* of person who should answer it without being pressured to provide a personal introduction. That final mechanism quietly compounds the guest graph without the pathetic: > Who famous do you know who can come on? --- # 3. Visual language: do not renovate the studios yet You are correct that you should not walk into three operating studios and rebuild them around an unproven show. The answer is a **portable visual grammar**, not a permanent set. Call the v0 approach: # **The Portable Salon Kernel** Take the principle from *Club Random*—a room in which a conversation happens—without copying the décor. ## The central visual rule > The audience should perceive the room before it perceives the production. ### Do not show - Microphones - Boom arms - Headphones - A desk separating everyone - Large neon branding - Generic RGB light washes - Rows of acoustic foam - Production equipment - Branded mugs placed like props - Perfectly symmetrical “podcast studio” framing ### Use - Two or three chairs angled toward one another - One low table - One warm practical lamp visible in frame - A background with actual depth - A few objects with narrative function - Concealed lavaliers - Overhead or out-of-frame directional microphones as backup - One restrained graphic or colored-light intervention - An episode-specific object or artifact Minimalism is not the absence of objects. It is **few objects with actual purpose**. ## Camera grammar The same basic camera logic should travel across LA, NYC, and Austin: 1. **Environmental master** The room, bodies, distance, and social relation. 2. **Ari single** Intimate enough to read expression, wide enough to preserve the room. 3. **Guest single** Matched in importance, without requiring artificial symmetry. 4. **Artifact camera** Optional overhead or detail angle for objects, cards, drawings, documents, or whatever the episode creates. The edit should cut when attention, tension, or conversational mode changes—not every two seconds. ## Audio grammar No visible microphones means the audio plan must become **more redundant**, not less serious: - Concealed lav on every speaker - Out-of-frame overhead microphone as safety - Isolated tracks - Wardrobe-rustle check - Room tone - Pre-recording movement test - Backup recording path ## Graphic grammar This is where your aesthetic authorship can enter without changing the physical studios. Use: - Kinetic typography - Brief segment interstitials - Episode-specific glyphs, diagrams, or graphic artifacts - Restrained lower thirds that disappear quickly - One changing signal color or visual texture per episode - Thumbnails built around the episode tension rather than merely the guest’s name A useful doctrine: > **The physical room is calm. The graphic layer may become strange.** That allows you to bring your multimedia and generative-environment work into the show without requiring a million-dollar permanent set. ## Visual evolution | Stage | Build | |---|---| | **v0 — Format proof** | Furniture repositioning, hidden audio, controlled lighting, segment cards, unified graphics | | **v1 — Recognition** | Replicated lighting package, custom artifact box/table, stronger recurring visual symbol | | **v2 — Dedicated environment** | Transformable room whose light, projections, and objects change by episode | The v0 set skin can live in one case at each studio. You are changing framing, light, and selected objects—not commandeering the facility. --- # 4. Your mobile show should use a different protocol Your suitcase show should not imitate the flagship with worse equipment. Its distinction is: > The guest’s environment is the third participant. The flagship is a controlled salon. Your show is an encounter, visitation, or field study. ## Mobile format engine # **Place → Object → Intervention → Artifact** ### Place Why are you in this room, workshop, apartment, studio, office, street, archive, theater, garage, or landscape? ### Object The guest shows you something that contains history, method, obsession, contradiction, or failure. ### Intervention You ask them to: - Demonstrate something - Change something - Build something - Destroy or revise something - Walk through a process - Reinterpret an object - Solve an unfamiliar constraint - Take you somewhere else in the space ### Artifact You leave with a new: - Image - Text - Sound - Route - Diagram - Rule - Prediction - Object - Performance - Unanswered question ## The GoPro suitcase grammar Use the cameras deliberately rather than trying to fake conventional studio cinema: - One environmental master - One guest angle - One host angle - One roaming or object camera - Separate local audio for each participant - Synchronization slate at the beginning and after interruptions The wide-angle, spatial quality can become part of the show’s observational identity. The suitcase should carry: - Capture - Audio - Power and media - Metadata workflow - Releases - A small recurring identity object - The segment protocol It should **not carry a fake living room**. ## Relationship between the two shows | Flagship | Your field show | |---|---| | Controlled studio | Guest’s own environment | | Ari + you + guest | Primarily you + guest | | Claim → Test → Artifact | Place → Object → Intervention → Artifact | | Consistent room grammar | Consistent filming protocol | | Weekly franchise potential | Irregular, higher-distinction films | | Entertainment and conversation | Art, documentary, inquiry, encounter | Build both show profiles inside the OS now. Do not immediately burden yourselves with two separate weekly release schedules. The field series can initially operate as occasional films while the flagship proves its cadence. --- # 5. Ari’s operational interface Ari should not receive a complex CRM, daily Slack notifications, a giant list of tasks, or fifteen AI-agent reports. He should receive **one approval digest**. Each candidate card contains: ```text Guest Why them Why now Episode thesis Proposed artifact Best city Contact route Relationship classification Social cost Required Ari effort ``` His controls: ```text APPROVE REJECT PROTECT RELATIONSHIP ADD PERSONAL NOTE ``` Example: ```text Guest: Candidate X Why: Strong fit for an episode about what success destroys. Route: Publicist Relationship: Weak professional adjacency Social cost: Low Ari effort: None Recommended city: Los Angeles [APPROVE] [REJECT] [PROTECT] [VOICE NOTE] ``` ## Ari’s actual weekly job - Approve several candidates - Correct any relationship classifications - Mark protected people - Record a 15–30 second personal note when direct contact materially helps - Show up prepared enough to perform - Record Everything else belongs to the producer and system. Do not force him to “work his network.” Make the system surface **the least socially expensive credible route**: 1. Representative 2. Existing professional channel 3. Weak tie 4. Mutual-contact permission 5. Brief Ari note 6. Direct personal request only when justified This protects close relationships while still using the reality of his professional graph. --- # 6. The shared operating system Every show using the system gets a configuration file—its **Show DNA**: ```text show identity host roles editorial premise guest-selection rubric outreach voice relationship rules segment library visual rules studio options ad rules asset requirements publishing cadence ``` The underlying system then operates the same core workflow: ```text Candidate found → relationship route identified → episode thesis produced → host approval → outreach drafted → human approval → reply classified → studio and calendar routed → guest intake → research and segments → recording → asset production → thank-you and commitments → publication → relationship continuation ``` ## Core data entities ```text SHOW GUEST RELATIONSHIP CONTACT ROUTE APPEARANCE OPPORTUNITY EPISODE STUDIO SEGMENT ARTIFACT SPONSOR ASSET COMMITMENT TOUCHPOINT ``` The critical distinction is that a person is not simply “a lead.” One guest may have several separate appearance opportunities across different shows and years. ## Internal AI capabilities - Network Cartographer - Relationship Capital Governor - Guest Thesis Architect - Outreach Desk - Inbox Triage - Studio Router - Episode Designer - Research Producer - Commercial Break Lab - Asset Producer - Relationship Steward - Operations Analyst These are internal capabilities, not public-facing fake employees. Edison’s 2026 research strongly supports that division: respondents were far more approving of AI performing research, brainstorming, and guest-finding than of an AI-generated voice hosting the show. That is precisely the architecture here—**AI backstage; real people in the relationship and performance layer**. citeturn225752view2 --- # 7. How to sell it without becoming hacky Do not sell: > An AI bot that scrapes celebrities and spams them for your podcast. Do not sell Ari’s contacts. Do not promise celebrity bookings. Do not imply the system replaces a producer. Sell: > **An invisible guest and production desk installed around your show.** The product outcome is: - Better candidates - Better episode premises - Less host labor - Professional outreach - Faster scheduling - Better preparation - Fewer forgotten promises - Stronger guest experience - More usable content per recording - Clear production data ## Start as a managed service Before building a large SaaS platform: 1. Operate it manually and with AI for your flagship. 2. Measure what actually happens. 3. Install it for one or two other shows in Ari’s network. 4. Identify which steps remain custom and which repeat. 5. Productize the repeated layer. The first commercial offer should probably include: - System installation - Show DNA design - Guest pipeline setup - Booking operations - Research and segment briefs - Studio routing - Post-recording follow-through Then add: - Sponsor creative - Asset production - Cross-show guest routing - Analytics - White-label dashboards ## The real network moat A permissioned guest-routing layer across multiple shows and three cities could eventually become extremely valuable. Example: > A guest will already be in New York for two days. The system identifies three relevant shows in the network, obtains permission, and coordinates efficient recordings without duplicate outreach. That creates value for: - The guest - Their representative - The studios - The shows But it must remain permissioned. The network is not a contact list for sale. --- # 8. Advertising should become part of the entertainment design You are right that bored ad reads are usually aesthetically destructive. Build a **Commercial Break Lab** inside the show. The ad is clearly disclosed, factually controlled, and creatively authored. ## Recurring formats ### Product on Trial Ari cross-examines the product. You construct an absurdly overqualified defense. The final line states its actual use plainly. ### The Overbuilt Explanation You create an elaborate philosophy of a basic product. Ari reduces it to the reason anyone would actually buy it. ### Worst Possible Spokesperson One host performs the least appropriate possible sales persona. The other repairs the pitch. ### Impossible Demonstration A short visual scenario exaggerates the problem the product solves, followed by a truthful landing. ### Thirty-Second Film A separately shot miniature scene—not an interrupted interview. ### Sponsored Mechanic A brand funds an existing segment that actually fits the product. The sponsor is disclosed, and editorial control remains with the show. Start with **house ads**: - Ari’s studio network - An email list - Another owned show - A live event - Your own creative project That allows you to prove the advertising format before a sponsor is paying and nervous. IAB’s current podcast creative guidance emphasizes the unusually strong attention and creator influence available in the medium and specifically collects innovative examples across audio, video, social, and live formats. Your instinct is aligned with where serious podcast advertising is already moving: away from generic radio copy and toward creator-native creative. citeturn225752view0 --- # 9. Success requires designing for video, audio, and extraction simultaneously This should be a **video-native master that remains completely intelligible as audio**. That is not optional polish. YouTube announced more than one billion monthly active viewers of podcast content in 2025, and podcast viewing on living-room devices exceeded 700 million hours in October 2025. Edison’s 2026 research also reported record podcast consumption and found that 57% of Americans had both listened to and watched podcasts. citeturn828048search5turn828048search1turn828048search8 Spotify reported that 40% of users who discovered a new show in 2024 selected video, and its study found stronger retention among smaller creators using video. Spotify also explicitly recommends the “close your eyes” test: the episode should still work when consumed as audio. citeturn225752view3 Your no-visible-microphone, living-room visual instinct is therefore not merely aesthetic. It makes the show read more like a television program or filmed encounter on large screens. ## Every recording should produce - One complete episode - One strong trailer - Three primary horizontal clips - Several vertical clips - Chapter markers - Episode-specific stills - One artifact asset - One guest delivery package - Audio edition - Searchable description and transcript Official YouTube guidance recommends multi-angle visual production, meaningful chapters, strong episode-specific thumbnails, and “shoulder content” such as Shorts and clips. Spotify also now distributes episode clips across several discovery surfaces. citeturn225752view1turn225752view4turn225752view5 The segment system makes this easier because the recording already contains discrete editorial units instead of one shapeless two-hour file. --- # 10. The launch sequence Do not start by burning the biggest relationship Ari could possibly contact. ## Private format proof Record three non-public pilots: 1. A professionally familiar guest who will be comfortable 2. A guest from your artistic, academic, literary, or technical world 3. A weak-tie guest who does not owe either of you anything Test: - Whether the host contrast works - Whether the segments feel organic - Whether the Artifact produces anything worthwhile - Whether hidden audio works - Whether the room reads properly - Which sections create the strongest clips - Whether guests actually enjoy the experience ## Production bank Do not launch until roughly six strong episodes are recorded or firmly in the pipeline. A practical launch: - Trailer - Three episodes demonstrating range - Weekly release afterward - Clips and smaller assets between episodes ## Early success metrics Do not judge the show primarily by total views in the first month. Measure: | Category | Metric | |---|---| | Format | Audience retention at each segment transition | | Audience | Returning viewers and subscribers per episode | | Discovery | Clip viewers who reach the full episode | | Booking | Reply rate and approval-to-recording time | | Guest | Satisfaction, referrals, and fulfilled promises | | Production | Time and cost per completed episode | | Business | Sponsor interest, renewal, and revenue per recording | | System | How often a human must correct the AI | The most important early question is: > Are people returning because of the show’s mechanism and host dynamic, or only because they recognize a guest? --- # 11. What should exist by tomorrow morning Not a completed autonomous SaaS company. A **working control plane**. ## The package should contain 1. **Show DNA v0** Audience promise, host functions, exclusions, format engine. 2. **Segment Deck v0** Three fixed segments and a rotating module library. 3. **Visual Language v0** Portable salon kernel, shot grammar, audio rules, graphic rules. 4. **Guest Pipeline v0** Candidate, thesis, route, city, relationship class, next action. 5. **Ari Approval Digest v0** Approve, reject, protect, or add a note. 6. **Outreach Templates v0** Producer-led, prior relationship, and brief Ari note. 7. **Workflow v0** State machine from candidate discovery through publication. 8. **Commercial Break Lab v0** House-ad formats and sponsor intake. 9. **Field Show Profile v0** Your separate portable protocol. 10. **Three pilot episode briefs** Guest, claim, stress test, artifact, visual object, recording city. ## Ari’s morning task His part should take about twelve minutes: - Confirm or revise the one-sentence show promise - Approve three pilot candidates - Mark any protected relationships - Select which relationship routes may use his name - Confirm recurring recording windows - Choose which segment role feels natural to him - Record one short voice note explaining what he wants the show to feel like Then the producer/system proceeds. --- # Starter pack I assembled the v0 operating package with: - Show DNA - Segment deck - Visual-language specification - Field-show profile - Guest-pipeline template - Outreach templates - Agent roles - Workflow events - Ad lab - An interactive local Ari approval dashboard The dashboard is a prototype only—it stores decisions locally and does not send messages or connect to Gmail. [Download the Podcast OS v0 starter pack](sandbox:/mnt/data/podcast_os_v0_starter_pack.zip) The central architecture is now: > **One distinctive flagship format, one separate mobile field protocol, one shared invisible production office, and one minimal approval surface designed around a host who only wants to record.**

Citation Marker: fileciteturn1file0 --- name: github description: Triage and orient GitHub repository, pull request, and issue work through the connected GitHub app. Use when the user asks for general GitHub help, wants PR or issue summaries, or needs repository context before choosing a more specific GitHub workflow. --- # GitHub ## Overview Use this skill as the umbrella entrypoint for general GitHub work in this plugin. It should decide whether the task stays in repo and PR triage or should be handed off to a more specific review, CI, or publish workflow. This plugin is intentionally hybrid: - Prefer the GitHub app from this plugin for repository, issue, pull request, comment, label, reaction, and PR creation workflows. - Use local `git` and `gh` only when the connector does not cover the job well, especially for current-branch PR discovery, branch creation, commit and push, `gh auth status`, and GitHub Actions log inspection. - Keep connector state and local checkout context aligned. If the request is about the current branch, resolve the local repo and branch before acting. Once the intent is clear, route to the specialist skill immediately and do not keep broad GitHub triage in scope longer than needed. ## Connector-First Responsibilities Handle these directly in this skill when the request does not need a narrower specialist workflow: - repository orientation once the repo, PR, issue, or local checkout is identified - recent PR or issue triage - PR metadata summaries - PR patch inspection - PR comments, labels, and reactions - issue lookup and summarization - PR creation after a branch is already pushed Prefer the GitHub app from this plugin for those flows because it provides structured PR, issue, and review-adjacent data without depending on a local checkout. If the repository is not already identifiable from the user request or local git context, ask for the repo instead of pretending there is a repo-search flow that may not exist. ## Routing Rules 1. Resolve the operating context first: - If the user provides a repository, PR number, issue number, or URL, use that. - If the request is about "this branch" or "the current PR", resolve local git context and use `gh` only as needed to discover the branch PR. - If the repository is still ambiguous after local inspection, ask for the repo identifier. 2. Classify the request before taking action: - `repo or PR triage`: summarize PRs, issues, patches, comments, labels, reactions, or repository state - `review follow-up`: unresolved review threads, requested changes, or inline review feedback - `CI debugging`: failing checks, Actions logs, or CI root-cause analysis - `publish changes`: create or switch branches, stage changes, commit, push, and open a draft PR 3. Route to the specialist skill as soon as the category is clear: - Review comments and requested changes: `../gh-address-comments/SKILL.md` - Failing GitHub Actions checks: `../gh-fix-ci/SKILL.md` - Commit, push, and open PR: `../yeet/SKILL.md` 4. Keep the hybrid model consistent after routing: - connector first for PR and issue data - local `git` and `gh` only for the specific gaps the connector does not cover ## Default Workflow 1. Resolve repository and item scope. 2. Gather structured PR or issue context through the GitHub app from this plugin. 3. Decide whether the task stays in connector-backed triage or needs a specialist skill. 4. Route immediately when the work becomes review follow-up, CI debugging, or publish workflow. 5. End with a clear summary of what was inspected, what changed, and what remains. ## Output Expectations - For triage requests, return a concise summary of the repository, PR, or issue state and the next likely action. - For mixed requests, tell the user which specialist path you are taking and why. - For connector-backed write actions, restate the exact PR, issue, label, or reaction target before applying the change. - Never imply that GitHub Actions logs are available through the connector alone. That remains a `gh` workflow. ## Examples - "Use GitHub to summarize the open PRs in this repo and tell me what needs attention." - "Help with this PR." - "Review the latest comments on PR 482 and tell me what is actionable." - "Debug the failing checks on this branch." - "Commit these changes, push them, and open a draft PR."

Citation Marker: fileciteturn16file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: e3911fc57dc1e6bbacda87b9518b2ef8791cd348 [L3] content: # Cronus Metabolus (The Content Yield Engine) [L4] [L5] **Premium asset metabolism for high-yield content distribution.** [L6] [L7] Transforms one premium visual asset (hero film, 3D render, product shoot) into 30+ days of platform-optimized social content. Built for brands that invest heavily in original production but struggle to maintain high-frequency social presence. [L8] [L9] --- [L10] [L11] ## Status: Foundation Established [L12] [L13] - **Organ:** III (Ergon) — Commerce [L14] - **Partners:** Anthony Padavano (engineering) + Scott Lefler (design/marketing/sales) [L15] - **Origin:** Partnership formed 2026-03-23 [L16] [L17] ## The Problem [L18] [L19] Premium brands pay $15-50K for hero films and product renders. These assets get used once. Meanwhile, marketing teams spend 20+ hrs/week manually creating social content. The yield on original investment is too low. [L20] [L21] ## The Solution: Metabolism [L22] [L23] 1. **Ingest** a premium video or render set (The "Hero" asset). [L24] 2. **AI Analysis:** Algorithmic determination of brand-aligned "high-energy" moments (The "Natural Center"). [L25] 3. **Clip/Image Fragmentation:** 10-15 short clips, stills, and carousel fragments, resized per platform. [L26] 4. **Captions & Voice:** AI-generated captions in the brand's trained voice, including hashtag strategy and CTA variants. [L27] 5. **Robust Distribution:** One-click distribution to Instagram, LinkedIn, TikTok, YouTube Shorts, X. [L28] 6. **Yield Reporting:** Weekly engagement metrics tied back to source asset ROI. [L29] [L30] ## Architecture (pnpm monorepo) [L31] [L32] - **apps/**: React dashboard, dual-runtime API (Cloudflare Worker + Fastify), Management CLI. [L33] - **services/**: Specialized microservices for ingestion, extraction, and generation. [L34] - **packages/**: Shared logic for Database (Drizzle), Domain, Storage (R2), Queues (BullMQ/Temporal). [L35] - **infra/**: Dockerized Redis/Postgres, Temporal configuration. [L36] [L37] ## Stack [L38] [L39] - **Frontend:** React 19 + Vite 8 + Tailwind 3 + Lucide. [L40] - **Backend:** Node.js/TypeScript — dual-runtime API: Hono on Cloudflare Worker (production) + Fastify locally (full pipeline). See `CLAUDE.md` → Architecture: Dual-Runtime API. [L41] - **Database:** PostgreSQL (Neon) via Drizzle ORM. [L42] - **Workflow:** Temporal.io for retriable processing pipelines. [L43] - **Queue:** BullMQ + Redis. [L44] - **AI/ML:** FFmpeg, PySceneDetect, Whisper/Deepgram, Claude API. [L45] - **Deploy:** Cloudflare Pages (dashboard) + Cloudflare Worker (API). [L46] [L47] ## Quick Start [L48] [L49] ```bash [L50] # Clone and install [L51] pnpm install [L52] [L53] # Start local infra (Redis/Postgres) [L54] pnpm docker:up [L55] [L56] # Run migrations [L57] pnpm db:migrate [L58] [L59] # Start development environment [L60] pnpm dev [L61] ``` [L62] [L63] ## License [L64] [L65] MIT
