Citation Marker: fileciteturn18file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: d13daa322f230fca3adc871229c86bb46d3e2e24 [L3] content: # CLAUDE.md [L4] [L5] This file provides guidance to Claude Code (claude.ai/code) when working with code in this repository. [L6] [L7] ## What This Is [L8] [L9] **Cronus Metabolus** (formerly Content Engine — Asset Amplifier) is an AI-powered content yield engine. It ingests one premium asset (hero film, 3D render, product shoot), fragments it (clips, stills, captions), and synthesizes 30+ days of platform-optimized social posts. Partnership: Padavano (engineering) + Lefler Design (UI/UX, marketing, sales). Organ III (Ergon — Commerce). MVP operational, production deployed. [L10] [L11] ## Live Deployments [L12] [L13] | Component | URL / Identifier | [L14] |-----------|------------------| [L15] | API (Cloudflare Worker) | https://cronus-api.ivixivi.workers.dev | [L16] | Dashboard (Cloudflare Pages) | https://cronus-dashboard.pages.dev | [L17] | Pitch Decks | https://cronus-metabolus.pages.dev | [L18] | Database | Neon `green-art-84790526` (PostgreSQL 17 + pgvector) | [L19] | R2 bucket | `cronus-assets` | [L20] | Issues | [organvm-iii-ergon/content-engine--asset-amplifier/issues](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/content-engine--asset-amplifier/issues) | [L21] | Project Board | [Operating Board](https://github.com/orgs/organvm-iii-ergon/projects/6) | [L22] [L23] ## Roadmap (GitHub Issues) [L24] [L25] | Phase | Issues | [L26] |-------|--------| [L27] | α (Dashboard) | #6 Asset ROI, #7 Identity Mirror, #8 Brand selector, #9 Content detail | [L28] | β (Platform) | #1 X adapter, #2 TikTok, #3 YouTube, #4 Instagram OAuth, #5 Real publishing | [L29] | γ (Scale) | #13 Agency dashboard, #14 Design resizing | [L30] | infra | #10 CI/CD, #11 BullMQ/Temporal, #12 pgvector migration | [L31] [L32] ## Repo Layout (pnpm + Turbo monorepo) [L33] [L34] Workspace globs in `pnpm-workspace.yaml`: `apps/*`, `services/*`, `packages/*`. Node ≥22, pnpm ≥9, ESM throughout (`"type": "module"`). [L35] [L36] - **`apps/api`** — REST API. **Two runtimes** (see Architecture). [L37] - **`apps/dashboard`** — React 19 + Vite 8 + Tailwind 3. Deployed to Cloudflare Pages. [L38] - **`apps/cli`** — Management CLI (`tsx`-run TS). [L39] - **`packages/`** — `config` (shared TS/ESLint + AI provider resolver), `db` (Drizzle + Neon), `domain` (shared types/Zod), `logger` (Pino), `queue` (BullMQ), `storage` (R2/S3 abstraction). [L40] - **`services/`** — `asset-ingestion`, `fragment-extraction`, `natural-center`, `content-generation`, `scoring`, `scheduler`, `platform-adapter`, `analytics`, `design-resizer`, `audience-engine`. Imported into the API as workspace deps; many are stubs/sources-only and bundled via `wrangler.toml [alias]`. [L41] - **`infra/docker`** — local Postgres + Redis compose. [L42] - **`infra/temporal`** — workflow defs (queued for #11). [L43] [L44] ## Architecture: Dual-Runtime API [L45] [L46] `apps/api` is **deployed as a Cloudflare Worker** (`src/worker.ts`, Hono) but **also runs locally as a Fastify server** (`src/server.ts`). They are not feature-equivalent: [L47] [L48] - `worker.ts` is the canonical production surface — Hono on Cloudflare Workers, R2 binding for asset storage, Neon HTTP driver for Postgres. **Cannot run FFmpeg / native binaries** — the Worker collapses the fragment-extraction pipeline by treating the whole upload as one synthetic fragment, then calls the LLM provider directly to mint per-platform `content_units`. [L49] - `server.ts` is the Fastify dev/full-stack version with route plugins under `src/routes/*` and an `authPlugin`. Use this when you need the full pipeline (queues, services, FFmpeg-bound work). [L50] - `wrangler.toml`'s `[alias]` block is **load-bearing**: it rewrites every `@cronus/*` workspace import to that package's `src/index.ts` so the Worker can bundle without depending on built `dist/` output. When adding a new package consumed by the Worker, add an alias entry. [L51] - Worker secrets are managed via `wrangler secret put`. The Worker copies all bindings into `process.env` in a global middleware so shared packages (`@cronus/config`) work identically in both runtimes, and forces `NODE_ENV=production` to skip Ollama-localhost provider checks. [L52] [L53] When editing API behavior, **check whether you need to update both `server.ts` routes and `worker.ts`** — they drift easily. [L54] [L55] ## DB Conventions (`packages/db`) [L56] [L57] - Drizzle ORM, dialect `postgresql`, schema files in `src/schema/*.ts`, root export `src/schema/index.ts`. [L58] - **Columns are `snake_case` in the DB**, and Drizzle insert/update calls use snake_case keys (`brand_id`, `processing_status`, …). API responses go through `toCamel`/`mapRows` (`src/mappers.ts`) so wire format is camelCase. Mirror this in new routes: snake_case for Drizzle, camelCase for JSON. [L59] - Migrations: `packages/db/src/migrations/`. Drizzle Kit reads `DATABASE_URL` from env (`drizzle.config.ts`). [L60] - Tables: `agencies`, `brands`, `natural_centers`, `assets`, `fragments`, `content_units`, `platform_connections`, `publish_events`, `performance_observations`. [L61] [L62] ## Provider Resolution (`packages/config`) [L63] [L64] `resolveProviders()` returns the active `llm`, `embedding`, `transcription` providers plus full fallback lists. Worker uses cloud providers only (Groq, Gemini, Cerebras, Anthropic, OpenAI); Ollama is skipped when `NODE_ENV=production`. [L65] [L66] ## Key Domain Concepts [L67] [L68] - **Metabolism** — break a Hero asset into fragments (clips/stills/text), then re-synthesize per-platform content. [L69] - **Natural Center** — per-brand identity profile derived from approved content, used to score new fragments for brand-fit. Persisted in `natural_centers`. [L70] - **Approval flow** — `content_units.approval_status ∈ {pending, approved, rejected}`; rejection writes `flagged_reason`. [L71] - **Temporal Workflows** — robust retriable pipelines for FFmpeg + AI generation (queued for #11). [L72] [L73] ## Commands [L74] [L75] ```bash [L76] # Workspace (Turbo orchestrated) [L77] pnpm install [L78] pnpm dev # turbo dev — all apps/services in parallel [L79] pnpm build # turbo build (depends on ^build) [L80] pnpm test # turbo test (Vitest) [L81] pnpm lint # turbo lint (ESLint per workspace) [L82] pnpm typecheck # turbo typecheck (tsc --noEmit) [L83] pnpm format # Prettier write [L84] pnpm format:check # Prettier check [L85] [L86] # Single-package work — use --filter [L87] pnpm --filter @cronus/api dev [L88] pnpm --filter @cronus/api test [L89] pnpm --filter @cronus/db typecheck [L90] [L91] # Single test file / pattern (Vitest) [L92] pnpm --filter @cronus/db test -- src/mappers.test.ts [L93] pnpm --filter @cronus/api test -- -t "brand creation" [L94] [L95] # Database (Drizzle) [L96] pnpm db:migrate # @cronus/db migrate [L97] pnpm db:seed # tsx infra/scripts/seed.ts [L98] [L99] # Local infra [L100] pnpm docker:up # Redis + Postgres via infra/docker/docker-compose.yml [L101] pnpm docker:down [L102] [L103] # Cloudflare Worker (API) [L104] cd apps/api [L105] npx wrangler dev -c wrangler.toml # local Worker dev [L106] npx wrangler deploy -c wrangler.toml # deploy [L107] echo "<value>" | npx wrangler secret put <NAME> -c wrangler.toml [L108] ``` [L109] [L110] ## Required Env / Secrets [L111] [L112] Worker bindings (set via `wrangler secret put`): `DATABASE_URL`, `API_KEY`, `ENCRYPTION_KEY`, `GROQ_API_KEY`, `GEMINI_API_KEY`, `CEREBRAS_API_KEY`, `ANTHROPIC_API_KEY`, `OPENAI_API_KEY`, `CLOUDFLARE_ACCOUNT_ID`, `CLOUDFLARE_API_TOKEN`, `STRIPE_SECRET_KEY`, `STRIPE_WEBHOOK_SECRET`, `STRIPE_PRICE_ID_CREATOR`, `STRIPE_PRICE_ID_STUDIO`, `DASHBOARD_URL`. R2 binding: `ASSETS_BUCKET` → `cronus-assets`. [L113] [L114] For local Fastify dev, mirror the same names in a `.env`. Any budget.** [L21] [L22] $3K to $50K+ | macOS + Apple Silicon | Dante Audio Sync | 4 Tested Profiles | [Quick Start](#quick-start) | [Profiles](#choose-your-profile) | [Why This Exists](#the-problem-this-framework-solves) [L23] [L24] --- [L25] [L26] ## Table of Contents [L27] [L28] - [The Problem This Framework Solves](#the-problem-this-framework-solves) [L29] - [Technical Architecture](#technical-architecture) [L30] - [Choose Your Profile](#choose-your-profile) [L31] - [Installation and Quick Start](#quick-start) [L32] - [Configuration System](#configuration-system) [L33] - [Operational Workflow](#operational-workflow) [L34] - [Features and Capabilities](#features-and-capabilities) [L35] - [Key Statistics](#key-statistics) [L36] - [Real-World Deployments](#real-world-deployments) [L37] - [Documentation Map](#documentation-map) [L38] - [Cross-Organ Context](#cross-organ-context) [L39] - [Roadmap](#roadmap) [L40] - [Community and Research](#community-and-research) [L41] - [Contributing](#contributing) [L42] - [License](#license) [L43] - [Author](#author) [L44] [L45] --- [L46] [L47] ## The Problem This Framework Solves [L48] [L49] Every multi-camera streaming guide ends the same way: "...and then figure out audio sync yourself." [L50] [L51] You have spent thousands on cameras, capture hardware, and a capable Mac. OBS is installed. Dante Controller is open. The hardware is physically connected. Now what? [L52] [L53] - How do you route Dante audio into OBS without drift? [L54] - Why is audio three frames behind video after twenty minutes? [L55] - What encoder settings prevent the stream from dying mid-event? [L56] - How does a volunteer operator reproduce your exact setup next week? [L57] - Where is the single document that ties cameras, audio network, encoding, and streaming into one coherent pipeline? [L58] [L59] That document does not exist. Until now. [L60] [L61] ### What Is Missing from Existing Solutions [L62] [L63] | Problem | Existing Resources | This Framework | [L64] |---|---|---| [L65] | Hardware selection for a specific budget | Scattered forum posts | 4 tested profiles ($3K to $50K+) with full BOMs | [L66] | Dante audio routed into OBS | Fragmented guides across 3 vendors | Complete clock-locked architecture with signal flow diagrams | [L67] | Audio-video synchronization over long broadcasts | "Good luck" | Global sample clock via Dante, measured drift <50ms over 1 hour | [L68] | Volunteer operator handoff | "Watch this 45-minute video" | 8-phase runbook with decision-tree troubleshooting | [L69] | Reproducible streaming setup for academic citation | Nothing | BibTeX-ready, version-locked, config-as-code profiles | [L70] | Pre-stream system validation | Manual checklists | Automated health-check scripts with JSON output | [L71] [L72] ### How This Framework Differs from Products [L73] [L74] | Capability | ATEM Mini | vMix | OBS Alone | This Framework | [L75] |---|---|---|---|---| [L76] | Audio synchronization | HDMI embedded only | Windows audio stack | Manual configuration | Dante global clock | [L77] | Budget flexibility | Fixed hardware tier | Fixed software license | Unknown starting point | 4 documented profiles | [L78] | Operational documentation | Product manual | Tutorial videos | Community wiki fragments | 95% operational coverage | [L79] | Volunteer-ready operation | "Watch the video" | "Watch the video" | "Figure it out" | 8-phase runbook | [L80] | Reproducible configuration | "Buy same hardware" | "Same version" | No mechanism | Config-as-code YAML profiles | [L81] | Pre-stream health checks | None | None | None | Automated shell scripts | [L82] [L83] ATEM and vMix are products. OBS is software. This framework is the **knowledge layer** that makes the entire pipeline work together. It documents not just what to connect, but how, why, and what to do when something breaks. [L84] [L85] --- [L86] [L87] ## Technical Architecture [L88] [L89] The pipeline separates concerns into five independent subsystems connected via standardized protocols. Each subsystem can be understood, debugged, and upgraded independently. [L90] [L91] ### Signal Flow [L92] [L93] ``` [L94] ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ [L95] │ INPUT LAYER │ [L96] │ │ [L97] │ Cameras (HDMI out) Remote Callers (NDI) │ [L98] │ ├─ Camera 1 ──┐ └─ OBS NDI stream ──┐ │ [L99] │ ├─ Camera 2 ──┤ │ │ [L100] │ ├─ Camera 3 ──┤ DeckLink Quad HDMI │ │ [L101] │ └─ Camera 4 ──┘ (Echo Express SE I / TB3) │ │ [L102] │ │ │ │ [L103] ├────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────┼─────────┤ [L104] │ CAPTURE + SYNC LAYER │ │ [L105] │ │ │ │ [L106] │ ▼ ▼ │ [L107] │ OBS Studio ◄──────── Audio ──── MOTU 8PRE-ES │ │ [L108] │ (4× DeckLink inputs) (Dante I/O) │ │ [L109] │ (NDI caller inputs) │ │ │ [L110] │ │ │ │ [L111] │ Dante Network ◄─────────────────┘ │ │ [L112] │ ├─ 48 kHz global sample clock │ │ [L113] │ ├─ AVIO nodes (camera audio converters) │ │ [L114] │ └─ Dedicated managed Ethernet switch │ │ [L115] │ │ [L116] ├──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤ [L117] │ PROCESSING LAYER │ [L118] │ │ [L119] │ Ableton Live (Dante clock master) │ [L120] │ ├─ Input: Camera feeds via Dante │ [L121] │ ├─ Mixing: Per-channel EQ, gain, effects │ [L122] │ ├─ Clock: Drives all Dante devices at 48 kHz │ [L123] │ └─ Output: Stereo master → MOTU → OBS │ [L124] │ │ [L125] │ Blender (optional graphics) │ [L126] │ ├─ Input: Generative parameters via MIDI │ [L127] │ └─ Output: Screen share → OBS │ [L128] │ │ [L129] ├──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤ [L130] │ OUTPUT LAYER │ [L131] │ │ [L132] │ OBS Encoder (H.264 / H.265) │ [L133] │ ├─ Video: 8–25 Mbps (codec-dependent) │ [L134] │ ├─ Resolution: 4K or 1080p mosaic │ [L135] │ ├─ Framerate: 30fps or 60fps │ [L136] │ └─ Protocol: RTMP │ [L137] │ │ │ [L138] │ ▼ │ [L139] │ YouTube Live / Twitch / Custom RTMP │ [L140] │ Local recording (continuous backup) │ [L141] │ │ [L142] └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ [L143] ``` [L144] [L145] ### Clock Synchronization — The Core Innovation [L146] [L147] Audio-video drift is the central unsolved problem in multi-camera streaming. The framework eliminates it through a global clock hierarchy: [L148] [L149] ``` [L150] Ableton Live (Master Clock, 48 kHz) [L151] │ [L152] ▼ sync [L153] Dante Network (all devices phase-locked, <1µs deviation) [L154] │ [L155] ▼ sync [L156] Camera audio converters (AVIO nodes lock to Dante clock) [L157] │ [L158] ▼ result [L159] Zero cumulative drift across all audio sources [L160] ``` [L161] [L162] Every audio device in the pipeline — microphones, camera audio feeds, remote caller returns — is phase-locked to a single 48 kHz sample clock distributed over the Dante audio network. The MOTU 8PRE-ES serves as the primary Dante I/O interface, with Ableton Live acting as the clock master. This architecture means that audio from Camera 1, Camera 4, and a remote NDI caller all arrive at OBS with identical timing. No post-production correction required. [L163] [L164] ### Scaling Architecture [L165] [L166] The framework scales from a single Mac to a multi-room production facility: [L167] [L168] | Phase | Setup | Cameras | Key Addition | [L169] |---|---|---|---| [L170] | Phase 1 (current) | Single M1 Ultra, all local | 4 tested, 8 expandable | DeckLink Quad HDMI | [L171] | Phase 2 | Multi-room, NDI feeds to master | 8–10 | M1 Mac mini per room, NDI aggregation | [L172] | Phase 3 | Building-scale | 10–15 rooms | Redundant Dante switches, NAS archive, analytics | [L173] [L174] --- [L175] [L176] ## Choose Your Profile [L177] [L178] The framework ships with four tested hardware profiles spanning a 15x cost range. Each profile includes a complete bill of materials, configuration files, and operational documentation. [L179] [L180] ``` [L181] ┌─────────────────┐ ┌─────────────────┐ ┌─────────────────┐ ┌─────────────────┐ [L182] │ BUDGET │ │ MOBILE │ │ STUDIO │ │ BROADCAST │ [L183] │ ~$3K │ │ ~$8K │ │ ~$20K │ │ $50K+ │ [L184] ├─────────────────┤ ├─────────────────┤ ├─────────────────┤ ├─────────────────┤ [L185] │ 2 cameras │ │ 2–4 cameras │ │ 4 cameras │ │ 8+ cameras │ [L186] │ USB capture │ │ TB3 capture │ │ DeckLink PCIe │ │ Multi-DeckLink │ [L187] │ USB audio │ │ Portable Dante │ │ Full Dante │ │ Dante + SDI │ [L188] │ Mac mini │ │ MacBook Pro │ │ Mac Studio │ │ Mac Studio/Pro │ [L189] ├─────────────────┤ ├─────────────────┤ ├─────────────────┤ ├─────────────────┤ [L190] │ Best for: │ │ Best for: │ │ Best for: │ │ Best for: │ [L191] │ • Worship │ │ • Touring │ │ • Research │ │ • Esports │ [L192] │ • Classroom │ │ • Remote prod │ │ • Corporate │ │ • Enterprise │ [L193] │ • Simple events │ │ • Venue-hopping │ │ • Full-featured │ │ • Multi-room │ [L194] └─────────────────┘ └─────────────────┘ └─────────────────┘ └─────────────────┘ [L195] ``` [L196] [L197] Profiles are YAML configuration files that override a shared defaults layer. The configuration system computes minimum hardware requirements from production needs (number of cameras, resolution, audio channels) using scaling formulas, then validates that your hardware meets those requirements. See [Configuration System](#configuration-system) for details. [L198] [L199] --- [L200] [L201] ## Quick Start [L202] [L203] ### Prerequisites [L204] [L205] - macOS 13.0 or later (tested on 14.2.1) [L206] - Apple Silicon Mac (M1/M2/M3 — Studio, mini, or MacBook Pro) [L207] - Gigabit Ethernet connection [L208] - [Homebrew](https://brew.sh) package manager [L209] [L210] ### Installation [L211] [L212] ```bash [L213] # Clone the repository [L214] git clone https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/multi-camera--livestream--framework.git [L215] cd multi-camera--livestream--framework [L216] [L217] # Install build dependencies (yq for YAML parsing, gettext for templating) [L218] make install-deps [L219] [L220] # Generate configuration for your hardware profile [L221] make config PROFILE=studio # Options: budget, mobile, studio, broadcast [L222] [L223] # Verify your system meets requirements [L224] ./software/scripts/setup-macos.sh [L225] [L226] # Run pre-stream health check [L227] ./software/scripts/health-check.sh [L228] ``` [L229] [L230] ### First Broadcast — Five-Day Onboarding [L231] [L232] | Day | Task | Reference Document | [L233] |---|---|---| [L234] | 1–2 | Verify hardware against profile BOM | [hardware/COMPATIBILITY.md](hardware/COMPATIBILITY.md) | [L235] | 3–4 | Configure Dante audio network and clock sync | [docs/AUDIO-DANTE.md](docs/AUDIO-DANTE.md) | [L236] | 5 | Full dry run using the 8-phase runbook | [docs/RUNBOOK.md](docs/RUNBOOK.md) | [L237] | 6–7 | Go live with local recording (no public stream) | [docs/STREAMING.md](docs/STREAMING.md) | [L238] [L239] ### Makefile Commands [L240] [L241] ```bash [L242] make config # Generate config from default profile (studio) [L243] make config PROFILE=mobile # Generate config from a specific profile [L244] make docs # Generate documentation from templates [L245] make all # Generate both config and docs [L246] make switch-profile PROFILE=budget # Switch active profile [L247] make list-profiles # List available profiles [L248] make validate # Syntax-check all shell scripts [L249] make health-check #... (truncated)

[First Run](#first-run) [L42] - [CLI Reference](#cli-reference) [L43] - [Labeling Commands](#labeling-commands) [L44] - [Reporting Commands](#reporting-commands) [L45] - [Triage Commands](#triage-commands) [L46] - [Configuring VIP Senders](#configuring-vip-senders) [L55] - [Provider Capabilities Matrix](#provider-capabilities-matrix) [L56] - [Label Taxonomy](#label-taxonomy) [L57] - [Scheduling and Daily Automation](#scheduling-and-daily-automation) [L58] - [Cross-Organ Context](#cross-organ-context) [L59] - [Related Work](#related-work) [L60] - [Contributing](#contributing) [L61] - [License](#license) [L62] - [Author](#author) [L63] [L64] --- [L65] [L66] ## The Problem [L67] [L68] **Email chaos is universal.** Anyone who operates across multiple accounts — a personal Gmail, a work Outlook, an iCloud account for Apple devices — knows the friction. Important messages drown in newsletter noise. Financial alerts compete with marketing spam. Each provider offers its own filter system, but none of them talk to each other. The result is fragmented organization, duplicated effort, and the persistent anxiety of missing something critical. [L69] [L70] Manual approaches fail at scale. "Touch It Once" and "Inbox Zero" philosophies demand a human decision on every single email, which breaks down above a few hundred messages per day. Gmail filters cannot share logic with Outlook rules. iCloud rules cannot reference Gmail labels. The organizational schemes diverge, and the user is left maintaining three separate systems that accomplish the same goal badly. [L71] [L72] This project eliminates that fragmentation. One set of categorization rules. One priority system. One CLI. Every provider. [L73] [L74] --- [L75] [L76] ## Product Overview [L77] [L78] Universal Mail Automation is a Python-based email triage system that applies a unified set of categorization rules across Gmail (via REST API), Outlook.com (via Microsoft Graph API), iCloud and any standard IMAP server, and macOS Mail.app (via AppleScript). The system operates on three coordinated principles: [L79] [L80] 1. **Unified Rules Engine** — A single taxonomy of 28 hierarchical categories (`Dev/GitHub`, `Finance/Banking`, `AI/Services`, `Travel`, `Marketing`, etc.) defined as regex patterns in `core/rules.py`. Define a rule once, and it applies to every provider. [L81] [L82] 2. **Eisenhower Matrix Prioritization** — Every email is assigned to one of four priority tiers (Critical, Important, Delegate, Reference) that determine whether it stays in the inbox, gets archived, gets starred, or simply gets categorized for later retrieval. [L83] [L84] 3. **Time-Based Escalation** — Emails that remain unprocessed age into higher priority tiers. A Tier 4 (Reference) email that sits for 72+ hours automatically escalates to Tier 1 (Critical), ensuring nothing falls through the cracks. [L85] [L86] The system is designed for daily intake, but the primary local path is on-demand. Use `scripts/intake_now.sh` to create/reuse the venv, verify Gmail auth, and write private triage reports under user-local state. A macOS `launchd` job is available only as an explicit opt-in for machines where LaunchAgents are allowed. [L87] [L88] --- [L89] [L90] ## Cloudflare Share Demo [L91] [L92] A public Cloudflare Worker demo is available for quick partner review: [L93] [L94] - Live share URL: <https://uma.4444j99.dev> [L95] - Deployment note: [docs/cloudflare-share-demo.md](docs/cloudflare-share-demo.md) [L96] [L97] This share surface serves the dashboard and a minimal same-origin API. It is not the canonical product backend; the Python application remains authoritative for real provider operations. [L98] [L99] --- [L100] [L101] ## Operator Dashboard [L102] [L103] UMA also includes a private operator dashboard for local mail-ops state: [L104] [L105] - `GET /ops` serves the operator control tower. [L106] - `GET /v1/ops/summary` returns the redacted `uma.ops.summary.v1` payload. [L107] - `GET /v1/ops/history` returns the redacted bounded history index when configured. [L112] - `GET /v1/ops/resolver-ledger` returns redacted resolver proof state merged with local `uma.mail.resolver_receipt.v1` receipts. [L113] - `GET /v1/ops/github-resolver` returns a redacted `uma.github.resolver_snapshot.v1` read-only GitHub CLI/API snapshot for GitHub resolver actions. [L114] - `POST /v1/ops/github-resolver-receipts` records redacted resolver receipts from GitHub provider-read or blocker snapshot candidates and always requires `UMA_OPS_TOKEN`. [L115] - `GET /v1/ops/followup-resolver` returns a redacted `uma.followup.resolver_snapshot.v1` view of mail/LinkedIn follow-up proof from draft approval and delivery receipts. [L116] - `POST /v1/ops/followup-resolver-receipts` records redacted resolver receipts from existing follow-up approval/delivery proof and always requires `UMA_OPS_TOKEN`. [L117] - `GET /v1/ops/external-resolver` returns a redacted `uma.external.resolver_snapshot.v1` view of provider/security/billing/subscription/legal official-surface work with controlled provider hint counts. [L118] - `POST /v1/ops/external-resolver-receipts` records local blocker attestations only when explicitly requested and always requires `UMA_OPS_TOKEN`. [L119] - `POST /v1/ops/resolver-receipts` appends a redacted resolver proof receipt and always requires `UMA_OPS_TOKEN`. [L120] - `GET /v1/ops/action-ledger` returns redacted action status merged with local `uma.mail.action_receipt.v1` proof receipts. [L121] - `POST /v1/ops/action-receipts` appends a redacted local proof receipt and always requires `UMA_OPS_TOKEN`. [L122] - `GET /v1/ops/draft-package/{action_id}?ack_private=true` builds a gated private draft package and always requires `UMA_OPS_TOKEN`. [L123] - `GET /v1/ops/draft-approvals/{action_id}?ack_private=true` returns redacted local draft approval status. Citation Marker: fileciteturn36file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: d23ff4bfb0b936ef933bad03d54954ce4d876f3d [L3] content: [![ORGAN-III: Ergon](https://img.shields.io/badge/ORGAN--III-Ergon-1b5e20?style=flat-square)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon) [L4] [![JavaScript](https://img.shields.io/badge/JavaScript-ES2024-f7df1e?style=flat-square&logo=javascript&logoColor=000)](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript) [L5] [![React](https://img.shields.io/badge/React-18.2-61dafb?style=flat-square&logo=react&logoColor=000)](https://react.dev) [L6] [![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/License-MIT-green?style=flat-square)](LICENSE) [L7] [L8] # Fetch Familiar Friends [L9] [L10] [![CI](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/fetch-familiar-friends/actions/workflows/ci.yml/badge.svg)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/fetch-familiar-friends/actions/workflows/ci.yml) [L11] [![Coverage](https://img.shields.io/badge/coverage-pending-lightgrey)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/fetch-familiar-friends) [L12] [![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/License-MIT-blue.svg)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/fetch-familiar-friends/blob/main/LICENSE) [L13] [![Organ III](https://img.shields.io/badge/Organ-III%20Ergon-F59E0B)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon) [L14] [![Status](https://img.shields.io/badge/status-active-brightgreen)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/fetch-familiar-friends) [L15] [![TypeScript](https://img.shields.io/badge/lang-TypeScript-informational)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/fetch-familiar-friends) [L16] [L17] [L18] **A social pet calendar, care tracker, and gamified companion app for dog and cat owners — built on React 18, Supabase, and Stripe.** [L19] [L20] Fetch Familiar Friends (internally branded *DogTale Daily*) is a consumer-facing B2C progressive web application that transforms everyday pet ownership into an engaging daily ritual. The app delivers personalized dog and cat imagery each day, layered with journaling, health tracking, social networking, a full gamification engine, and a three-tier subscription model. It sits within [ORGAN-III (Ergon)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon), the commerce organ of the eight-organ creative-institutional system, and represents a production-ready consumer product with real monetization infrastructure. [L21] [L22] --- [L23] [L24] ## Table of Contents [L25] [L26] - [Product Overview](#product-overview) [L27] - [Technical Architecture](#technical-architecture) [L28] - [Core Feature Set](#core-feature-set) [L29] - [Installation and Quick Start](#installation-and-quick-start) [L30] - [Project Structure](#project-structure) [L31] - [Data Model](#data-model) [L32] - [API Integrations](#api-integrations) [L33] - [Gamification Engine](#gamification-engine) [L34] - [Subscription and Monetization](#subscription-and-monetization) [L35] - [Testing](#testing) [L36] - [Scripts and Tooling](#scripts-and-tooling) [L37] - [Cross-Organ References](#cross-organ-references) [L38] - [Roadmap](#roadmap) [L39] - [Contributing](#contributing) [L40] - [License](#license) [L41] - [Author](#author) [L42] [L43] --- [L44] [L45] ## Product Overview [L46] [L47] Pet ownership in the digital age lacks a single hub that combines daily delight with practical care management. Fetch Familiar Friends solves this by merging four distinct value propositions into one application: [L48] [L49] 1. **Daily content ritual.** Each day surfaces a new breed-specific dog or cat image via the Dog CEO and TheCatAPI services, accompanied by breed knowledge, fun facts, and a daily content seed that makes every visit feel fresh. [L50] [L51] 2. **Care management.** Owners track vaccinations, vet visits, medications, weight history, grooming schedules, and custom reminders — all attached to individual pet profiles with full health timelines. [L52] [L53] 3. **Social platform.** An activity feed, friend lists, reactions (like, love, paw, wow), comments, and a proximity-based "Nearby Pet Parents" feature create a lightweight social network purpose-built for the pet community. [L54] [L55] 4. **Gamification layer.** Quests, achievements, XP leveling, virtual pets, gym battles, PvP matchmaking, season passes, and leaderboards borrow proven engagement mechanics from mobile gaming to drive daily retention. [L56] [L57] The combination positions the product as a *daily companion app* rather than a single-purpose utility, targeting the recurring-engagement model that sustains consumer subscription businesses. [L58] [L59] ### Target Audience [L60] [L61] - Dog and cat owners who want a daily touchpoint with their pet's life [L62] - Pet parents managing multiple animals and recurring care schedules [L63] - Social pet communities seeking a dedicated, non-generic platform [L64] - Casual gamers who enjoy collection and progression mechanics [L65] [L66] ### Business Model [L67] [L68] The app operates a freemium SaaS model with three subscription tiers managed through Stripe: [L69] [L70] | Tier | AI Messages/Day | Features | [L71] |------|-----------------|----------| [L72] | **Free** | 5 | Core calendar, journaling, favorites, basic health tracking | [L73] | **Premium** | 50 | Full social hub, advanced gamification, coaching hub, extended AI | [L74] | **Luxury** | 500 | Telemedicine, AR camera, unlimited AI, priority support | [L75] [L76] Stripe checkout sessions, customer portal management, and webhook-driven subscription state synchronization are implemented via Supabase Edge Functions running on Deno Deploy. [L77] [L78] --- [L79] [L80] ## Technical Architecture [L81] [L82] The application is built as a single-page progressive web app with a clear separation between the client-side React application and the Supabase backend-as-a-service layer. [L83] [L84] ### Stack Overview [L85] [L86] | Layer | Technology | Purpose | [L87] |-------|-----------|---------| [L88] | **Framework** | React 18.2 (JSX) | Component model, concurrent features | [L89] | **Build** | Vite 7 | Development server, HMR, production bundling | [L90] | **Styling** | Tailwind CSS 3 + custom design tokens | Utility-first CSS with `surface-*` and `primary-*` token system | [L91] | **Animation** | Framer Motion 12 | Page transitions, micro-interactions, reduced-motion awareness | [L92] | **Icons** | Lucide React | Consistent iconography across all components | [L93] | **Backend** | Supabase (PostgreSQL + Auth + Edge Functions) | Database, authentication, real-time subscriptions, serverless compute | [L94] | **Payments** | Stripe (via Supabase Edge Functions) | Checkout sessions, customer portal, webhook processing | [L95] | **Testing** | Vitest + Testing Library + Happy DOM | Unit and integration testing with React component coverage | [L96] | **Linting** | ESLint 9 + Prettier | Code quality and formatting enforcement | [L97] | **PWA** | Custom service worker (`public/sw.js`) + Web App Manifest | Offline support, installability, background sync | [L98] [L99] ### Architecture Diagram [L100] [L101] ``` [L102] Browser (PWA) [L103] ├── React 18 SPA [L104] │ ├── Context Providers (Auth, Subscription, Achievement, ReducedMotion) [L105] │ ├── Lazy-loaded modal system (13 modals, code-split) [L106] │ ├── Custom hooks layer (20+ hooks) [L107] │ └── Service layer (API, analytics, storage, sync, notifications) [L108] │ [L109] ├── Local Storage (resilientStorage with backup/recovery) [L110] │ [L111] └── Supabase Cloud [L112] ├── PostgreSQL (20+ tables, RLS policies, PostGIS) [L113] ├── Auth (email/password, OAuth, profile auto-creation triggers) [L114] ├── Edge Functions (Deno Deploy) [L115] │ ├── ai-chat (AI conversation proxy) [L116] │ ├── create-checkout-session (Stripe integration) [L117] │ ├── create-portal-session (Stripe customer portal) [L118] │ └── stripe-webhook (subscription lifecycle events) [L119] └── Realtime (activity feeds, friend status) [L120] ``` [L121] [L122] ### Key Architectural Decisions [L123] [L124] **Resilient local storage.** The `dogTaleStorage` utility wraps `localStorage` with automatic backup, corruption detection, and recovery. If primary data fails to parse, the system falls back to a backup copy, logs a recovery event, and continues without data loss. This addresses the real-world problem of localStorage corruption in long-running PWAs. [L125] [L126] **Lazy-loaded modal system.** All 13 modals (journal, AI chat, favorites, statistics, keyboard shortcuts, settings, social hub, ASCII visualizer, login, signup, health dashboard, story generator, season pass) are `React.lazy()` imports wrapped in a shared `Suspense` boundary. This keeps the initial bundle focused on the calendar view and defers the cost of feature-rich panels until the user actually opens them. [L127] [L128] **Context-driven feature gating.** Four React context providers wrap the application root: `AuthProvider`, `SubscriptionProvider`, `AchievementProvider`, and `ReducedMotionProvider`. The subscription context gates premium features at the component level. The reduced-motion context respects `prefers-reduced-motion` and propagates the preference to all animated components, ensuring WCAG 2.1 AA compliance for vestibular-sensitive users. [L129] [L130] **Offline-first with cloud sync.** The app works fully offline using localStorage and the service worker cache. When the user authenticates and goes online, the sync service reconciles local state with the Supabase backend, uploading journal entries, favorites, and settings. This dual-write architecture means users never lose work due to connectivity issues. [L131] [L132] --- [L133] [L134] ## Core Feature Set [L135] [L136] ### Daily Calendar [L137] [L138] The primary view is a date-navigable calendar card that fetches a new pet image each day. The `CalendarCard` component integrates breed detection (extracted from the Dog CEO URL pattern), daily content generation (via `src/utils/dailyContent.js`), and inline actions for journaling, AI chat, and favoriting. [L139] [L140] Eight visual themes — Park, Beach, Forest, Tundra, Sunset, Night, Snow, Autumn — apply distinct color palettes through Tailwind utility classes. Themes persist across sessions and can be cycled via keyboard shortcut (`T`). [L141] [L142] ### Journaling [L143] [L144] Each day supports a freeform text journal entry keyed by date string. Entries sync bidirectionally between localStorage and the Supabase `journal_entries` table (which supports mood tags, privacy flags, and per-pet attribution). The month calendar view overlays journal indicators on dates that have entries, giving owners a visual record of their engagement. [L145] [L146] ### Health Dashboard [L147] [L148] The health tracking module manages veterinary records across nine categories: vaccination, vet visit, medication, weight, symptom, allergy, surgery, dental, and other. Each record carries optional fields for vet name, clinic, cost, documents, and a `next_due_date` that feeds the reminders system. Reminders support daily, weekly, monthly, and yearly recurrence with push notification integration via the `notificationService`. [L149] [L150] ### Social Hub [L151] [L152] The social layer implements a full social network feature set: [L153] [L154] - **Activity Feed** — Posts with four reaction types, comments, and visibility controls (private, friends, public) [L155] - **Friends List** — Bidirectional friendship model with pending/accepted/blocked states [L156] - **Nearby Pet Parents** — PostGIS-powered geospatial queries for proximity discovery [L157] - **Coaching Hub** — Directory of certified trainers with session booking [L158] - **Vet Telemedicine** — 24/7 video consultation integration (premium/luxury tiers) [L159] - **Care Instructions** — Structured daily routines, grooming guides, and health monitoring checklists [L160] - **AR Camera** — Augmented reality filters and accessories for pet photos [L161] - **Pet Memorial** — Memorial pages for deceased pets with tribute messages [L162] [L163] ### AI Chat [L164] [L165] An AI conversation interface, proxied through the `ai-chat` Supabase Edge Function, provides breed-specific Q&A. Usage is rate-limited per subscription tier (5/50/500 messages per day), tracked in the `ai_usage` table, and enforced by a server-side `check_ai_rate_limit()` PostgreSQL function. [L166] [L167] ### Story Generator [L168] [L169] The story modal uses journal entries as seed material to generate personalized narratives about the user's pet, combining AI generation with handcrafted templates from `src/utils/storyTemplates.js`. [L170] [L171] --- [L172] [L173] ## Installation and Quick Start [L174] [L175] ### Prerequisites [L176] [L177] - **Node.js** >= 18 (the project uses ES module syntax and Vite 7) [L178] - **npm** or **pnpm** (lockfiles for both are committed) [L179] - A Supabase project (optional — the app works in offline/localStorage-only mode without credentials) [L180] [L181] ### Setup [L182] [L183] ```bash [L184] # Clone the repository [L185] git clone https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/fetch-familiar-friends.git [L186] cd fetch-familiar-friends [L187] [L188] # Install dependencies [L189] npm install [L190] [L191] # Copy environment template and configure (optional, for Supabase integration) [L192] cp .env.example .env [L193] # Edit .env with your Supabase URL, anon key, and optionally Stripe keys [L194] [L195] # Start the development server (opens http://localhost:3000) [L196] npm run dev [L197] ``` [L198] [L199] ### Available Scripts [L200] [L201] | Command | Purpose | [L202] |---------|---------| [L203] | `npm run dev` | Start Vite dev server with HMR on port 3000 | [L204] | `npm run build` | Production build to `dist/` with sourcemaps | [L205] | `npm run preview` | Preview the production build locally | [L206] | `npm run lint` | ESLint check with zero-warning policy | [L207] | `npm run test` | Run Vitest test suite | [L208] | `npm run test:ui` | Run tests with Vitest UI | [L209] | `npm run test:coverage` | Generate V8 coverage report | [L210] | `npm run ingest:breeds` | Ingest breed data from external APIs | [L211] | `npm run ingest:breeds:dogs` | Ingest dog breeds only | [L212] | `npm run ingest:breeds:cats` | Ingest cat breeds only | [L213] | `npm run ingest:validate` | Validate ingested breed data | [L214] | `npm run ingest:stats` | Display breed data statistics | [L215] | `npm run process:docs` | Process documentation files | [L216] | `npm run watch:docs` | Watch and auto-process documentation changes | [L217] | `npm run history:parse` | Parse repository history | [L218] [L219] ### Environment Variables [L220] [L221] The `.env.example` file documents all configuration options. Citation Marker: fileciteturn37file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: 0ceca14f1f2626e85cfcf5a61a1045205e6a4e48 [L3] content: [![ORGAN-VII: Kerygma](https://img.shields.io/badge/ORGAN--VII-Kerygma-6a1b9a?style=flat-square)](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma) [L4] [![POSSE](https://img.shields.io/badge/POSSE-Publish%20Own%20Site%2C%20Syndicate%20Elsewhere-blueviolet?style=flat-square)]() [L5] [![Mastodon](https://img.shields.io/badge/Mastodon-API%20integrated-6364FF?style=flat-square&logo=mastodon&logoColor=white)]() [L6] [![Discord](https://img.shields.io/badge/Discord-Webhooks%20live-5865F2?style=flat-square&logo=discord&logoColor=white)]() [L7] [![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/License-MIT-blue?style=flat-square)](LICENSE) [L8] [![Status](https://img.shields.io/badge/status-active-6a1b9a?style=flat-square)]() [L9] [L10] # Social Automation [L11] [L12] **Backend automation infrastructure for POSSE content distribution -- API integrations, scheduling logic, rate limiting, retry policies, delivery monitoring, and analytics collection for the eight-organ creative-institutional system.** [L13] [L14] Social Automation is the execution engine behind ORGAN-VII (Kerygma), the marketing and distribution organ of the [organvm](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma) ecosystem. Where [announcement-templates](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma/announcement-templates) defines *what* to say and [distribution-strategy](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma/distribution-strategy) defines *when and to whom*, this repository defines *how* -- the technical infrastructure that moves content from composition to delivery across every distribution channel. [L15] [L16] The system implements the POSSE (Publish on Own Site, Syndicate Elsewhere) principle as operational infrastructure. Primary content lives on the ORGAN-V Jekyll/GitHub Pages site. This repository ensures that every publication event triggers a coordinated, reliable, monitored distribution cascade across Mastodon, Discord, and future channels -- without manual intervention, without missed posts, and without silent failures. [L17] [L18] Content distribution at the scale of an eight-organ system is not a simple API call. It is a pipeline with failure modes at every stage: rate limits, network timeouts, API deprecations, format rejections, webhook delivery failures, and scheduling conflicts. This repository treats distribution as an engineering problem deserving the same rigor applied to any production system: retry policies with exponential backoff, circuit breakers for degraded services, delivery confirmation with receipt tracking, and comprehensive error reporting. [L19] [L20] --- [L21] [L22] ## Table of Contents [L23] [L24] - [Overview](#overview) [L25] - [Architecture](#architecture) [L26] - [POSSE Implementation](#posse-implementation) [L27] - [Channel Integrations](#channel-integrations) [L28] - [Scheduling Infrastructure](#scheduling-infrastructure) [L29] - [Error Handling and Resilience](#error-handling-and-resilience) [L30] - [Monitoring and Delivery Confirmation](#monitoring-and-delivery-confirmation) [L31] - [Analytics Pipeline](#analytics-pipeline) [L32] - [Configuration](#configuration) [L33] - [Connection to ORGAN-IV Workflows](#connection-to-organ-iv-workflows) [L34] - [Security and Credential Management](#security-and-credential-management) [L35] - [Metrics and Tracking](#metrics-and-tracking) [L36] - [Cross-Organ Dependencies](#cross-organ-dependencies) [L37] - [Roadmap](#roadmap) [L38] - [Contributing](#contributing) [L39] - [License](#license) [L40] - [Author and Contact](#author-and-contact) [L41] [L42] --- [L43] [L44] ## Overview [L45] [L46] Social Automation bridges the gap between content creation and audience delivery. In the organvm system, content flows through a defined pipeline: [L47] [L48] 1. **Creation** -- ORGAN-V essays, ORGAN-I/II/III repository updates, ORGAN-VI community events generate publication events. [L49] 2. **Templating** -- [announcement-templates](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma/announcement-templates) transforms events into platform-specific content with variable interpolation and quality checklists. [L50] 3. **Scheduling** -- This repository's scheduler determines optimal dispatch timing based on the content calendar, audience engagement patterns, and platform-specific best practices. [L51] 4. **Dispatch** -- Channel-specific adapters format and transmit content via platform APIs and webhooks. [L52] 5. **Confirmation** -- Delivery monitors verify successful receipt and log results. [L53] 6. **Analytics** -- Engagement metrics are collected, aggregated, and reported back to the orchestration hub. [L54] [L55] This repository owns stages 3-6. It is the operational core that turns strategic intent into measurable distribution outcomes. [L56] [L57] ### What Makes This Different [L58] [L59] Most social media automation tools optimize for volume -- schedule as many posts as possible across as many platforms as possible. This system optimizes for **signal integrity**. Every dispatched message is: [L60] [L61] - **Verified** against the source event (no stale or contradictory information reaches audiences) [L62] - **Timed** according to strategic calendaring (not just "best time to post" algorithms, but alignment with grant cycles, conference seasons, and audience attention patterns) [L63] - **Monitored** for delivery success (a failed webhook is not a silent failure -- it triggers retry logic and, if unresolvable, alerts for manual intervention) [L64] - **Measured** for actual impact (engagement metrics flow back to inform future distribution decisions) [L65] [L66] --- [L67] [L68] ## Architecture [L69] [L70] The system is organized into five subsystems that form a linear pipeline with feedback loops: [L71] [L72] ``` [L73] ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ [L74] │ EVENT INGESTION │ [L75] │ GitHub webhooks | RSS polling | Manual triggers │ [L76] └──────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────────┘ [L77] │ [L78] ┌──────────────▼───────────────────────────────────────────────┐ [L79] │ SCHEDULING ENGINE │ [L80] │ Calendar alignment | Rate limiting | Priority queue │ [L81] └──────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────────┘ [L82] │ [L83] ┌──────────────▼───────────────────────────────────────────────┐ [L84] │ DISPATCH ADAPTERS │ [L85] │ Mastodon API | Discord webhooks | RSS bridges │ [L86] └──────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────────┘ [L87] │ [L88] ┌──────────────▼───────────────────────────────────────────────┐ [L89] │ DELIVERY MONITOR │ [L90] │ Receipt verification | Retry logic | Alerting │ [L91] └──────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────────┘ [L92] │ [L93] ┌──────────────▼───────────────────────────────────────────────┐ [L94] │ ANALYTICS COLLECTOR │ [L95] │ Engagement metrics | Aggregation | Reporting │ [L96] └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ [L97] │ [L98] └──────────► ORGAN-IV Orchestration Hub (feedback) [L99] ``` [L100] [L101] ### Event Ingestion [L102] [L103] The ingestion layer accepts publication events from three sources: [L104] [L105] - **GitHub Webhooks:** Repository events (push to main, release creation, README deployment) trigger distribution workflows. The ORGAN-IV `distribute-content.yml` workflow sends structured event payloads to this system. [L106] - **RSS Polling:** The ORGAN-V Jekyll site publishes an Atom feed. The RSS poller checks for new entries on a configurable interval (default: 15 minutes) and generates essay publication events. [L107] - **Manual Triggers:** The workflow dispatch interface allows manual event creation for announcements that do not originate from automated sources (partnership announcements, milestone celebrations, ad-hoc promotions). [L108] [L109] ### Scheduling Engine [L110] [L111] The scheduler transforms raw events into timed dispatch jobs. It considers: [L112] [L113] - **Content calendar:** Preferred posting times from [distribution-strategy](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma/distribution-strategy). Different announcement types have different optimal windows (essay promotions within 24 hours of publication; community event invitations 7 days before the event). [L114] - **Rate limits:** Per-platform dispatch rate limits prevent API throttling. Mastodon: maximum 30 API calls per 5-minute window. Discord webhooks: maximum 30 requests per minute per webhook. [L115] - **Priority queue:** High-priority announcements (system milestones, breaking changes) preempt lower-priority items in the queue. Priority levels: CRITICAL (immediate), HIGH (within 1 hour), NORMAL (next optimal window), LOW (batch with next digest). [L116] - **Deduplication:** The scheduler detects and suppresses duplicate events (e.g., multiple pushes to the same repository within a short window) to prevent announcement spam. [L117] [L118] ### Dispatch Adapters [L119] [L120] Each distribution channel has a dedicated adapter that handles platform-specific formatting, API authentication, and transmission: [L121] [L122] - **Mastodon Adapter:** Uses the Mastodon REST API (v1) for status creation. Handles thread posting (sequential status creation with `in_reply_to_id` chaining), media uploads for images and diagrams, and visibility settings (public, unlisted, followers-only). Implements OAuth 2.0 authentication with token refresh. [L123] - **Discord Adapter:** Uses Discord webhook endpoints for message delivery. Constructs embed objects with organ-specific colors, structured fields, and footer attribution. Supports multiple webhook URLs for channel routing (announcements, releases, community-events, general). [L124] - **RSS Bridge:** Generates and updates syndication feeds that third-party services can consume. Maintains an outbound Atom feed of all announcements for subscribers who prefer feed readers. [L125] [L126] ### Delivery Monitor [L127] [L128] Every dispatched message is tracked through delivery confirmation: [L129] [L130] - **Receipt Verification:** After dispatch, the monitor polls the platform API to confirm the message exists and is publicly visible. Mastodon: verifies status exists via GET `/api/v1/statuses/:id`. Discord: verifies message exists via webhook response (HTTP 204 for success). [L131] - **Retry Logic:** Failed dispatches enter a retry queue with exponential backoff. Retry schedule: immediate retry, then 1 minute, 5 minutes, 15 minutes, 1 hour, 6 hours. Maximum 6 retries before escalation. [L132] - **Circuit Breaker:** If a platform's error rate exceeds a threshold (default: 50% of requests failing over a 10-minute window), the circuit breaker opens and halts all dispatches to that platform. The breaker enters a half-open state after a cooldown period (default: 30 minutes), allowing a single test request. If the test succeeds, normal dispatch resumes; if it fails, the breaker remains open for another cooldown cycle. [L133] - **Alerting:** Unresolvable failures (all retries exhausted, circuit breaker open for extended period) trigger alerts via GitHub Issues in the orchestration-start-here repository, ensuring human attention for persistent problems. [L134] [L135] ### Analytics Collector [L136] [L137] Engagement data flows back from distribution channels to inform strategy: [L138] [L139] - **Mastodon Metrics:** Boosts, favorites, replies, and click-through rates (via UTM parameter tracking on canonical URLs). [L140] - **Discord Metrics:** Reactions, thread replies, and link clicks (approximated via Discord's limited analytics). [L141] - **Aggregation:** Raw metrics are aggregated into per-announcement, per-channel, per-organ, and per-audience-segment summaries. [L142] - **Reporting:** Weekly analytics reports are generated and stored in the orchestration hub. Reports include: top-performing announcements, channel comparison, audience segment engagement, and trend analysis. [L143] [L144] --- [L145] [L146] ## POSSE Implementation [L147] [L148] POSSE (Publish on Own Site, Syndicate Elsewhere) is not just a distribution strategy -- it is an ownership strategy. By publishing canonical content on infrastructure the organvm system controls (GitHub Pages, own-domain Jekyll site) and syndicating copies to third-party platforms, the system maintains control over its content regardless of platform policy changes, algorithm shifts, or service shutdowns. [L149] [L150] ### The POSSE Flow [L151] [L152] ``` [L153] ORGAN-V Jekyll Site (canonical) [L154] │ [L155] ├──► Atom RSS Feed [L156] │ │ [L157] │ └──► RSS Poller (this repo) [L158] │ │ [L159] │ └──► Scheduling Engine [L160] │ │ [L161] ├──► Mastodon (syndicated copy with canonical link) [L162] ├──► Discord (syndicated copy with canonical link) [L163] └──► Future channels (LinkedIn, newsletter, etc.) [L164] ``` [L165] [L166] Every syndicated copy includes a canonical URL pointing back to the ORGAN-V site. This ensures: [L167] [L168] 1. **Search engine authority** accrues to the canonical site, not to third-party platforms. [L169] 2. **Link permanence** -- if a Mastodon instance goes down, the canonical URL remains valid. [L170] 3. **Content versioning** -- corrections and updates happen at the canonical source; syndicated copies can reference the canonical URL for the latest version. [L171] 4. **Analytics consolidation** -- all click-through traffic arrives at the canonical site, providing a single measurement point. [L172] [L173] ### Canonical URL Structure [L174] [L175] The ORGAN-V Jekyll site at `https://organvm-v-logos.github.io/public-process/` publishes essays with URLs following the pattern: [L176] [L177] ``` [L178] /essays/meta-system/01-why-orchestrate-everything.html [L179] /essays/meta-system/02-governance-without-bureaucracy.html [L180] ``` [L181] [L182] Syndicated copies always link to these canonical URLs with UTM parameters for source tracking: [L183] [L184] ``` [L185] ?utm_source=mastodon&utm_medium=social&utm_campaign=essay-01 [L186] ?utm_source=discord&utm_medium=social&utm_campaign=essay-01 [L187] ``` [L188] [L189] --- [L190] [L191] ## Channel Integrations [L192] [L193] ### Mastodon API Integration [L194] [L195] The Mastodon adapter implements the following API operations: [L196] [L197] | Operation | Endpoint | Method | Purpose | [L198] |-----------|----------|--------|---------| [L199] | Post status | `/api/v1/statuses` | POST | Create a new toot | [L200] | Post thread | `/api/v1/statuses` (chained) | POST | Create threaded replies | [L201] | Upload media | `/api/v2/media` | POST | Attach images/diagrams | [L202] | Verify status | `/api/v1/statuses/:id` | GET | Confirm delivery | [L203] | Get notifications | `/api/v1/notifications` | GET | Collect engagement data | [L204] [L205] **Authentication:** OAuth 2.0 bearer token stored as a GitHub Actions secret (`MASTODON_ACCESS_TOKEN`). Token scope: `read write push`. [L206] [L207] **Rate Limiting:** Mastodon instances enforce a rate limit of 300 API calls per 5-minute window. The adapter maintains a sliding window counter and queues requests that would exceed the limit. [L208] [L209] **Thread Posting Algorithm:** For multi-post threads (essay promotions, launch announcements), the adapter posts sequentially with a 2-second delay between posts. Each subsequent post includes the `in_reply_to_id` of the previous post to maintain thread structure. If any post in the thread fails, the adapter logs the partial thread and retries only the failed and subsequent posts. [L210] [L211] ### Discord Webhook Integration [L212] [L213] The Discord adapter uses webhook endpoints (not bot accounts) for simplicity and security: [L214] [L215] | Operation | Mechanism | Purpose | [L216] |-----------|-----------|---------| [L217] | Send message | POST to webhook URL | Deliver announcement | [L218] | Send embed | POST with embed payload | Rich-formatted announcement | [L219] | Confirm delivery | HTTP 204 response | Verify receipt | [L220] [L221] **Webhook Configuration:** Separate webhook URLs for different channels allow routing announcements to appropriate Discord channels. Webhook URLs are stored as GitHub Actions secrets (`DISCORD_WEBHOOK_ANNOUNCEMENTS`, `DISCORD_WEBHOOK_RELEASES`, `DISCORD_WEBHOOK_COMMUNITY`). ... (truncated)

This is where strategic intent is documented, audience segments are defined, channel performance is analyzed, and distribution decisions are made visible. It is the why and when behind every message the system sends into the world. [L15] [L16] This repository is intentionally public. While [announcement-templates](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma/announcement-templates) and [social-automation](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma/social-automation) contain internal operational details, Distribution Strategy operates in the open because transparency about audience strategy is itself a portfolio asset. Grant reviewers evaluate whether applicants understand their audiences. Hiring managers assess whether candidates think strategically about reach and impact. Fellow creative technologists appreciate honest analysis of what works and what does not. Making this strategy public serves all three audiences simultaneously. [L17] [L18] The core thesis: **quality over quantity, POSSE over platform lock-in, evergreen over ephemeral.** The organvm system does not chase viral moments. It builds sustained visibility with the specific audiences that matter for its portfolio, funding, and community goals -- and it does so on infrastructure it controls. [L19] [L20] --- [L21] [L22] ## Table of Contents [L23] [L24] - [Overview](#overview) [L25] - [Distribution Philosophy](#distribution-philosophy) [L26] - [Audience Segmentation](#audience-segmentation) [L27] - [Channel Strategy](#channel-strategy) [L28] - [Content Calendar](#content-calendar) [L29] - [Growth Targets and Benchmarks](#growth-targets-and-benchmarks) [L30] - [Competitive Landscape](#competitive-landscape) [L31] - [Content Types and Formats](#content-types-and-formats) [L32] - [Measurement Framework](#measurement-framework) [L33] - [Strategic Risks and Mitigations](#strategic-risks-and-mitigations) [L34] - [Templates and Resources](#templates-and-resources) [L35] - [Metrics and Tracking](#metrics-and-tracking) [L36] - [Cross-Organ Dependencies](#cross-organ-dependencies) [L37] - [Contributing](#contributing) [L38] - [License](#license) [L39] - [Author and Contact](#author-and-contact) [L40] [L41] --- [L42] [L43] ## Overview [L44] [L45] Distribution strategy for a creative-institutional system differs fundamentally from product marketing or personal brand building. The organvm system is not selling a product, not building a personal following, and not optimizing for engagement metrics as ends in themselves. Instead, it is constructing a portfolio presence that serves three concurrent goals: [L46] [L47] 1. **Grant competitiveness.** Demonstrating organizational capacity, technical depth, and community engagement to funding bodies (Knight Foundation, NEA, Mellon Foundation, NSF, Processing Foundation Fellowship, Google Creative Fellowship). [L48] 2. **Hiring signal.** Providing evidence of systems thinking, production-quality engineering, and architectural reasoning to technical recruiters and engineering managers evaluating portfolio submissions. [L49] 3. **Community contribution.** Sharing knowledge, tools, and process insights with the creative technology community in a way that generates goodwill, collaboration opportunities, and citation. [L50] [L51] These three goals are not in tension -- they are mutually reinforcing. The essay that demonstrates governance thinking to a grant reviewer also shows architectural maturity to a hiring manager and shares useful knowledge with the open-source community. Distribution strategy maximizes this triple-audience leverage by ensuring the right content reaches the right people through the right channels at the right time. [L52] [L53] --- [L54] [L55] ## Distribution Philosophy [L56] [L57] ### POSSE: Publish on Own Site, Syndicate Elsewhere [L58] [L59] The organvm system adopts the POSSE principle as a foundational distribution commitment. The implications are structural: [L60] [L61] - **Canonical content lives on infrastructure we control.** The ORGAN-V Jekyll site at `https://organvm-v-logos.github.io/public-process/` hosts all essays, with an Atom RSS feed for subscribers. GitHub repositories host all documentation and code. These are platforms where content ownership is unambiguous and portability is guaranteed. [L62] - **Social platforms receive syndicated copies, not originals.** Mastodon posts, Discord embeds, and LinkedIn articles are derivative -- they excerpt, summarize, and link back to canonical sources. If Mastodon disappears tomorrow, the content survives. If Discord changes its terms of service, no essays are lost. [L63] - **Syndication serves discovery, not dependency.** Social platforms are discovery mechanisms that introduce audiences to the canonical content. The goal is to move people from platform feeds to owned-site engagement, not to build platform-specific audiences that exist only within walled gardens. [L64] [L65] ### Quality Over Quantity [L66] [L67] The organvm system publishes when it has something worth saying, not because a content calendar demands daily output. The publication rhythm is driven by genuine milestones -- repository deployments, essay completions, system achievements -- not by engagement algorithms that reward frequency over substance. [L68] [L69] This is a strategic choice, not a limitation. In the audiences this system targets (academic researchers, grant reviewers, technical hiring managers), posting frequency has diminishing returns beyond a threshold. A weekly digest with substantive content outperforms daily posts with thin content. A single well-crafted essay with genuine insight generates more lasting impact than a dozen promotional posts. [L70] [L71] ### Evergreen Over Ephemeral [L72] [L73] The content strategy prioritizes artifacts that remain valuable over time: [L74] [L75] - **Essays** explore ideas that are relevant beyond the news cycle. "Why Orchestrate Everything" is not a reaction to a trend -- it is a position paper that remains useful whether read today or in two years. [L76] - **Repository documentation** serves as permanent reference material. A 3,000-word README is a portfolio piece with indefinite shelf life. [L77] - **System documentation** (governance rules, dependency models, orchestration specs) demonstrates institutional maturity regardless of when it is encountered. [L78] [L79] Ephemeral content (event announcements, milestone celebrations) is produced as needed but is not the strategic center. The ratio target is 70% evergreen / 30% ephemeral. [L80] [L81] --- [L82] [L83] ## Audience Segmentation [L84] [L85] ### Segment 1: Academic Researchers and Digital Humanists [L86] [L87] **Profile:** Faculty, postdocs, PhD students, and independent researchers in digital humanities, computational creativity, media studies, science and technology studies, and related fields. They discover work through academic social media (Mastodon is disproportionately popular in this segment), conference proceedings, and citation networks. [L88] [L89] **What they value:** Theoretical rigor, methodological transparency, engagement with relevant literature, conceptual novelty, reproducibility. They evaluate work by its intellectual contribution, not its commercial viability. [L90] [L91] **How to reach them:** [L92] - **Primary channel:** Mastodon (many academics migrated from Twitter; Mastodon's instance model creates concentrated communities in `scholar.social`, `hcommons.social`, etc.) [L93] - **Secondary channel:** ORGAN-V essays (discoverable via RSS, search engines, and citation) [L94] - **Tertiary channel:** Conference submissions and proceedings [L95] [L96] **Content strategy for this segment:** [L97] - Lead with theoretical frameworks and conceptual contributions [L98] - Reference relevant thinkers and traditions (Deleuze, Haraway, Bratton, etc. where genuinely applicable) [L99] - Share process insights (how the recursive engine models ontological layers, how governance structures encode institutional thinking) [L100] - Avoid commercial framing entirely [L101] [L102] **Engagement metrics:** Boosts and replies on Mastodon (especially from accounts with academic institutional affiliations), citations in papers and talks, invitations to speak or collaborate. [L103] [L104] ### Segment 2: Grant Reviewers and Program Officers [L105] [L106] **Profile:** Program staff at foundations and government agencies who evaluate funding applications. They read quickly, assess against rubrics, and look for evidence rather than claims. They are not regular social media consumers but do check applicants' online presence during review periods. [L107] [L108] **What they value:** Organizational capacity, sustainability planning, community impact, alignment with program priorities, evidence of professional infrastructure. They want to see that an applicant can execute at scale, not just ideate. [L109] [L110] **How to reach them:** [L111] - **Primary channel:** Direct (grant applications reference specific URLs -- repos, essays, org profiles) [L112] - **Secondary channel:** GitHub presence (org profiles, repository quality, documentation depth) [L113] - **Tertiary channel:** LinkedIn (for professional visibility during review periods) [L114] [L115] **Content strategy for this segment:** [L116] - Ensure all public-facing assets are grant-reviewer-friendly at all times (not just during application windows) [L117] - Emphasize system-level thinking: 8 organizations, 67 repositories, governance structures, quality processes [L118] - Quantify everything: word counts, test counts, coverage metrics, repository counts [L119] - Connect individual deliverables to broader systemic goals [L120] - Maintain a "grant readiness" posture -- any reviewer checking the system at any time should find professional-grade materials [L121] [L122] **Engagement metrics:** Grant application outcomes, reviewer feedback, shortlist appearances. These are lag indicators with long feedback loops (6-12 months from application to decision). [L123] [L124] ### Segment 3: Hiring Managers and Technical Recruiters [L125] [L126] **Profile:** Engineering managers, technical leads, and recruiters at companies evaluating candidates' portfolios. They spend 30-60 seconds on initial portfolio review and 5-10 minutes on deep dives for shortlisted candidates. [L127] [L128] **What they value:** Production-quality code, architectural reasoning, testing discipline, documentation quality, systems thinking, evidence of shipping real work (not just tutorials and toy projects). They distinguish between "built a thing" and "built a thing that handles failure modes, scales, and is maintained." [L129] [L130] **How to reach them:** [L131] - **Primary channel:** GitHub (org profiles, pinned repositories, README quality, commit history) [L132] - **Secondary channel:** LinkedIn (professional positioning, portfolio links in profile) [L133] - **Tertiary channel:** ORGAN-V essays (especially "Why Orchestrate Everything" and "Governance Without Bureaucracy" -- these demonstrate the kind of thinking hiring managers value) [L134] [L135] **Content strategy for this segment:** [L136] - Repository READMEs are the primary instrument. Every README is written to withstand scrutiny from a senior engineer: specific, honest, technically detailed [L137] - Architecture diagrams, test counts, and coverage metrics appear prominently [L138] - Cross-organ coordination demonstrates project management and systems thinking [L139] - The meta-system itself (orchestrating 67 repos across 8 orgs) is evidence of organizational capacity that most candidates cannot demonstrate [L140] [L141] **Engagement metrics:** Profile views (GitHub analytics), repository stars and forks (lagging indicator), interview invitations (ultimate outcome metric). [L142] [L143] ### Segment 4: Creative Technologists and Fellow Practitioners [L144] [L145] **Profile:** Artists, designers, creative coders, and technologists working at the art-technology intersection. They discover work through Mastodon, conferences (SIGGRAPH, Ars Electronica, ISEA), creative coding communities (Processing, openFrameworks, TouchDesigner), and peer networks. [L146] [L147] **What they value:** Aesthetic ambition, technical craft, conceptual depth, process transparency, generosity with knowledge, community contribution. They evaluate by "would I want to collaborate with this person" and "does this work expand my understanding of what is possible." [L148] [L149] **How to reach them:** [L150] - **Primary channel:** Mastodon (strong creative technology community presence) [L151] - **Secondary channel:** ORGAN-V essays (building-in-public content resonates deeply) [L152] - **Tertiary channel:** Conference presence, community events [L153] [L154] **Content strategy for this segment:** [L155] - Share process, not just results. "Here is what I tried and what broke" is more valuable than "here is the polished output." [L156] - Foreground the creative vision -- what is this system *for*, aesthetically and philosophically? [L157] - Invite collaboration and contribution. Open-source is a community act, not just a licensing decision. [L158] - Acknowledge influences and inspirations explicitly [L159] [L160] **Engagement metrics:** Mastodon engagement (especially replies and conversations, not just boosts), collaboration requests, community event attendance, fork/contribution activity. [L161] [L162] ### Segment 5: Open Source Community [L163] [L164] **Profile:** Developers who discover, use, and contribute to open-source projects. They find work through GitHub trending, Hacker News, Reddit, Mastodon developer communities, and word of mouth. [L165] [L166] **What they value:** Code quality, documentation completeness, maintainer responsiveness, issue triage speed, contribution guidelines, license clarity. They evaluate by "could I contribute to this project" and "would I trust this project as a dependency." [L167] [L168] **How to reach them:** [L169] - **Primary channel:** GitHub (repository quality, issue response time, CONTRIBUTING.md, community health files) [L170] - **Secondary channel:** Mastodon developer communities [L171] - **Tertiary channel:** Hacker News, Reddit (for major releases and milestones only) [L172] [L173] **Content strategy for this segment:** [L174] - Maintain impeccable repository hygiene: clear READMEs, typed contribution guidelines, responsive issue management [L175] - Release notes follow conventional changelog format [L176] - Community health files (CODE_OF_CONDUCT.md, SECURITY.md, CONTRIBUTING.md) are present and substantive in all orgs [L177] - Avoid "marketing speak" entirely -- this audience has zero tolerance for promotional language in technical contexts [L178] [L179] **Engagement metrics:** Stars, forks, issues filed, pull requests submitted, contributor count. [L180] [L181] --- [L182] [L183] ## Channel Strategy [L184] [L185] ### Mastodon (Primary Social Channel) [L186] [L187] **Rationale:** Mastodon's decentralized architecture and community culture align with the organvm system's values. The academic and creative technology communities are strongly represented on Mastodon. The platform's chronological timeline (no algorithmic suppression) means that well-timed, high-quality posts reach followers reliably. [L188] [L189] **Posting strategy:** [L190] - **Frequency:** 3-5 posts per week during active periods; 1-2 per week during quiet periods. [L191] - **Content mix:** 40% essay promotion and system updates, 30% process insights and building-in-public notes, 20% community engagement (replies, boosts of related work), 10% event announcements. [L192] - **Threading:** Long-form announcements use threaded posts (3-7 posts per thread). Each post is self-contained enough to be meaningful if encountered out of sequence. [L193] - **Hashtag strategy:** 2-3 hashtags per post from controlled vocabulary. Never more than 5. No hashtag-stuffing. [L194] - **Engagement:** Reply to every genuine response. Boost related work from peers. Follow back accounts in target audience segments. [L195] [L196] **Performance benchmarks (Year 1):** [L197] - Follower count: 200+ by month 6, 500+ by month 12 [L198] - Average engagement per post: 5+ boosts, 10+ favorites [L199] - Click-through rate to canonical URLs: 3-5% [L200] [L201] ### Discord (Community Channel) [L202] [L203] **Rationale:** Discord provides a persistent, structured community space for deeper engagement than social media allows. It serves as the real-time communication layer for ORGAN-VI (Koinonia) community events and as a distribution channel for system announcements. [L204] [L205] **Posting strategy:** [L206] - **Frequency:** Announcements as events occur (not scheduled -- Discord audiences expect immediacy). [L207] - **Format:** Rich embeds for all announcements (visually distinct from conversation, scannable metadata fields). [L208] - **Channel structure:** Separate channels for announcements (one-way broadcast), releases (technical updates), community-events (interactive),... (truncated)
