Resource uri: /response/turn47 Showing 229 of 338 lines. Response output was truncated at a line boundary to fit the tool response budget. Citation Marker: fileciteturn47file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: 259f6cb026452e2eca9d4dcf69e591f747158d96 [L3] content: [![ORGAN-V: Logos](https://img.shields.io/badge/ORGAN--V-Logos-0d47a1?style=flat-square)](https://github.com/organvm-v-logos) [L4] [![CI](https://github.com/organvm-v-logos/editorial-standards/actions/workflows/ci.yml/badge.svg)](https://github.com/organvm-v-logos/editorial-standards/actions/workflows/ci.yml) [L5] [![Tier: Standard](https://img.shields.io/badge/tier-standard-2196f3?style=flat-square)](https://github.com/organvm-v-logos) [L6] [L7] # editorial-standards [L8] [L9] _Voice, quality, and structure governance for the ORGAN-V discourse layer_ [L10] [L11] --- [L12] [L13] ## Overview [L14] [L15] editorial-standards is the governance layer for everything ORGAN-V publishes. It codifies the rules that determine what makes a piece of writing ready for the public process — the voice it should carry, the structure it should follow, the quality bar it must clear, and the metadata it must declare before it enters the pipeline. [L16] [L17] This repository does not contain essays. It contains the rules that essays must obey. [L18] [L19] The distinction matters. In a system that produces writing at scale — meta-system essays, case studies, retrospectives, post-mortems, methodology explorations — the editorial governance must live separately from the editorial content. Otherwise the rules drift with every new piece, standards become implicit, and quality becomes a matter of whoever last touched the file. editorial-standards makes the implicit explicit. It is the constitution for the discourse layer. [L20] [L21] Concretely, this repository provides: [L22] [L23] - **A voice specification** — the tonal and rhetorical identity that all ORGAN-V writing must express. [L24] - **Document type definitions** — the canonical set of essay types, each with its own structural template and purpose. [L25] - **A frontmatter schema** — the 11 required metadata fields that every essay must declare, with types, constraints, and validation rules. [L26] - **A quality rubric** — a 100-point advisory scoring system across five dimensions, used during review to surface weak spots before publication. [L27] - **Naming conventions** — deterministic rules for file names, series identifiers, and tag taxonomy, so that the essay-pipeline can process content without ambiguity. [L28] - **A review process** — the pre-publish checklist and human synthesis gate that stands between a draft and a deployed essay. [L29] [L30] Everything here is consumed downstream. The essay-pipeline reads the frontmatter schema to validate incoming drafts. The public-process repository is where validated essays land. editorial-standards sits upstream of both, defining the contract they depend on. [L31] [L32] ## The Voice [L33] [L34] ORGAN-V writes for an audience that builds things. The voice is substantive, honest, technical-but-accessible, and process-oriented. It does not perform expertise — it demonstrates it through specificity. It does not hide uncertainty — it names what is unknown and explains why. [L35] [L36] The core principles of the ORGAN-V voice: [L37] [L38] **Substantive over performative.** Every sentence should carry information. Introductions that exist only to "set the stage" without contributing content are cut. If a paragraph can be removed without the reader losing anything, it should be removed. [L39] [L40] **Honest over polished.** The writing acknowledges false starts, dead ends, and things that did not work. The process is the product. A retrospective that only describes successes is not a retrospective — it is marketing. ORGAN-V does not do marketing. [L41] [L42] **Technical but accessible.** The writing assumes an intelligent reader who may not share the author's specific domain expertise. Jargon is used when precise, defined when first introduced, and avoided when a plain word will do. The goal is to be understood on first read, not to signal membership in a community. [L43] [L44] **Process-oriented.** ORGAN-V is interested in how things are built, not just what was built. The method matters. The sequence matters. The decisions that shaped the outcome — including the decisions that were wrong — matter. Writing that presents only the final artifact without explaining the path to it is incomplete. [L45] [L46] **Structurally transparent.** The reader should always know where they are in a piece. Headings are descriptive, not clever. Section order follows logical dependency (context before analysis, analysis before conclusion). The structure itself is an argument about what matters. [L47] [L48] These principles are not optional stylistic preferences. They are the editorial standard. Writing that violates them is sent back for revision. [L49] [L50] ## Document Types [L51] [L52] ORGAN-V recognizes five canonical document types. Each serves a distinct purpose and carries its own structural expectations. [L53] [L54] ### Meta-System Essay [L55] [L56] The flagship form. Meta-system essays explore how the eight-organ system works, why it is designed the way it is, and what principles govern its operation. They are the primary output of ORGAN-V and the most common content in public-process. [L57] [L58] **Structure:** Introduction (context + thesis), body sections (each advancing the argument), cross-references to other organs and repos, conclusion (synthesis + forward-looking implications). [L59] [L60] **Length:** 3,000-6,000 words. [L61] [L62] **Examples:** "01-orchestrate," "02-governance," "05-five-years" in the public-process essays collection. [L63] [L64] ### Case Study [L65] [L66] A detailed examination of a specific project, feature, or system component. Case studies are empirical — they describe what happened, not what should happen. They follow the arc of a problem through its resolution (or its failure to resolve). [L67] [L68] **Structure:** Context (what existed before), challenge (what needed to change), approach (what was tried), outcome (what resulted), reflection (what was learned). [L69] [L70] **Length:** 2,000-4,000 words. [L71] [L72] ### Retrospective [L73] [L74] A time-bounded look back at a sprint, phase, or milestone. Retrospectives are honest about what went well and what did not. They are not victory laps — they are diagnostic instruments. [L75] [L76] **Structure:** Scope (what period/milestone is covered), objectives (what was intended), execution (what actually happened), delta analysis (gaps between intent and outcome), lessons (what changes going forward). [L77] [L78] **Length:** 1,500-3,000 words. [L79] [L80] ### Post-Mortem [L81] [L82] Written after something fails. Post-mortems are blameless, specific, and action-oriented. They exist to prevent recurrence, not to assign fault. [L83] [L84] **Structure:** Incident summary (what broke), timeline (when things happened), root cause analysis (why it broke), impact assessment (what was affected), remediation (what was done), prevention (what will be done differently). [L85] [L86] **Length:** 1,000-3,000 words. [L87] [L88] ### Methodology Essay [L89] [L90] An explanation of a process, framework, or approach used within the system. Methodology essays are instructional — they describe how to do something, and why that approach was chosen over alternatives. [L91] [L92] **Structure:** Motivation (why this method exists), prior art (what alternatives exist), the method (step-by-step), trade-offs (what this method sacrifices), application (where it has been used). [L93] [L94] **Length:** 2,000-5,000 words. [L95] [L96] ## Frontmatter Schema [L97] [L98] Every ORGAN-V essay must declare exactly 11 frontmatter fields. No more, no fewer. The frontmatter is the machine-readable contract that the essay-pipeline uses to validate, route, and publish content. [L99] [L100] | Field | Type | Required | Constraints | [L101] |---|---|---|---| [L102] | `title` | string | yes | 5-120 characters. No colons in the first 40 characters. | [L103] | `slug` | string | yes | Lowercase, hyphenated, 3-80 characters. Must match filename. | [L104] | `date` | ISO 8601 date | yes | Format: `YYYY-MM-DD`. Must not be in the future. | [L105] | `author` | string | yes | GitHub handle, prefixed with `@`. | [L106] | `document_type` | enum | yes | One of: `meta-system-essay`, `case-study`, `retrospective`, `post-mortem`, `methodology-essay`. | [L107] | `series` | string | no | Series identifier if part of a multi-part sequence. Lowercase, hyphenated. | [L108] | `series_order` | integer | conditional | Required if `series` is set. 1-indexed. | [L109] | `tags` | array of strings | yes | 2-8 tags from the controlled vocabulary. Lowercase, hyphenated. | [L110] | `organs_referenced` | array of integers | yes | Roman numeral organ numbers (1-8) that the essay references. Minimum 1. | [L111] | `status` | enum | yes | One of: `draft`, `review`, `published`, `archived`. | [L112] | `abstract` | string | yes | 80-300 characters. One-sentence summary. No markdown. | [L113] [L114] ### Field Details [L115] [L116] **title** is the human-readable essay title. The colon restriction in the first 40 characters prevents ambiguity in YAML parsing and ensures clean rendering in RSS feeds and social cards. [L117] [L118] **slug** is the URL-safe identifier. It must match the filename (minus the date prefix and extension). For a file named `2026-02-17-editorial-governance.md`, the slug is `editorial-governance`. [L119] [L120] **date** is the publication date. Drafts use their creation date. The date must not be in the future because the essay-pipeline uses date ordering for feed generation and will reject future-dated entries. [L121] [L122] **author** is the GitHub handle of the primary author. For AI-assisted writing, the human author is listed here; AI contribution is noted in the body or acknowledgments. [L123] [L124] **document_type** maps to one of the five canonical types defined above. This field drives template selection and structural validation in the essay-pipeline. [L125] [L126] **series** and **series_order** are used together for multi-part essays. A series like `meta-system-foundations` with three parts would have `series_order` values of 1, 2, and 3. The essay-pipeline uses these to generate series navigation. [L127] [L128] **tags** must come from the controlled vocabulary defined in this repository's `tags.yaml` (to be created as the tag taxonomy grows). Tags are the primary discovery mechanism in public-process. [L129] [L130] **organs_referenced** is the cross-referencing backbone. Every essay must declare which organs it discusses. This field feeds the organ-level index pages and enables readers to explore the system by organ. [L131] [L132] **status** controls visibility. Only `published` essays appear in the public feed. `review` essays are visible to reviewers. `draft` and `archived` are not rendered. [L133] [L134] **abstract** is the single-sentence summary used in feed entries, social cards, and search results. It must be plain text (no markdown) and must fit the character constraints. [L135] [L136] ## Quality Rubric [L137] [L138] The quality rubric is a 100-point advisory scoring system. It is not a gate — essays are not blocked from publication based on their score. It is a diagnostic tool that surfaces weak spots during review so that authors can address them before publication. [L139] [L140] The rubric has five dimensions, each worth 20 points: [L141] [L142] ### Clarity (20 points) [L143] [L144] How easily can the reader understand the essay on first read? Clarity scores assess sentence structure, paragraph organization, jargon management, and logical flow. A 20/20 clarity score means a competent reader outside the author's specific domain can follow the argument without re-reading. [L145] [L146] - **16-20:** Clear, well-organized, minimal jargon or jargon well-defined. [L147] - **11-15:** Generally clear with occasional dense passages. [L148] - **6-10:** Requires significant effort to follow; restructuring needed. [L149] - **1-5:** Unclear; major rewrite required. [L150] [L151] ### Accuracy (20 points) [L152] [L153] Are the claims correct? Are the technical details right? Accuracy scores assess factual correctness, proper use of terminology, and whether code samples, configuration examples, or system descriptions match the actual implementation. [L154] [L155] - **16-20:** All claims verifiable; technical details correct. [L156] - **11-15:** Minor inaccuracies that do not affect the argument. [L157] - **6-10:** Contains errors that could mislead the reader. [L158] - **1-5:** Fundamentally inaccurate; requires fact-checking pass. [L159] [L160] ### Insight Density (20 points) [L161] [L162] Does the essay reward the reader's time? Insight density measures the ratio of novel or useful information to total word count. High insight density means the essay is tightly written — every section advances the reader's understanding. Low density means the essay is padded with filler, repetition, or obvious observations. [L163] [L164] - **16-20:** Nearly every paragraph offers something new or useful. [L165] - **11-15:** Strong core insights with some padding. [L166] - **6-10:** Insight buried under excessive context or repetition. [L167] - **1-5:** Little new information; could be reduced to a fraction of its length. [L168] [L169] ### Cross-Referencing (20 points) [L170] [L171] Does the essay connect to the broader system? Cross-referencing scores assess how well the essay links to other organs, repos, essays, and system concepts. ORGAN-V writing does not exist in isolation — it exists to document a system, and that system context must be present. [L172] [L173] - **16-20:** Rich, meaningful connections to other system components. [L174] - **11-15:** Some cross-references; could be better integrated. [L175] - **6-10:** Minimal system context; reads as standalone. [L176] - **1-5:** No cross-references; disconnected from the system. [L177] [L178] ### Portfolio Relevance (20 points) [L179] [L180] Does this essay belong in the public process? Portfolio relevance assesses whether the essay contributes to the system's public narrative. It asks: if someone is reading the public-process collection to understand this system, does this essay help? Or is it internal documentation that does not serve an external reader? [L181] [L182] - **16-20:** Essential reading for understanding the system. [L183] - **11-15:** Useful but not critical; adds depth. [L184] - **6-10:** Marginal relevance; might be better as internal documentation. [L185] - **1-5:** Does not belong in the public collection. [L186] [L187] ### Scoring Guidelines [L188] [L189] Scores are assigned by the reviewer during the review process. The total score is the sum of all five dimensions (0-100). Scores are advisory — they inform the author, they do not block publication. However, essays scoring below 60 are flagged for revision, and essays below 40 are returned to draft status with specific feedback. [L190] [L191] The rubric is intentionally calibrated to be difficult. A score of 80+ represents genuinely excellent work. A score of 60-79 represents solid work that meets the standard. The system does not grade on a curve. [L192] [L193] ## Naming Conventions [L194] [L195] ### File Naming (_posts/) [L196] [L197] All essays in public-process follow the Jekyll `_posts/` convention: [L198] [L199] ``` [L200] YYYY-MM-DD-slug.md [L201] ``` [L202] [L203] - **Date prefix:** ISO 8601 date, matching the `date` frontmatter field. [L204] - **Slug:** Lowercase, hyphenated, 3-80 characters. Must match the `slug` frontmatter field. [L205] - **Extension:** Always `.md`. [L206] [L207] Examples: [L208] - `2026-02-17-editorial-governance.md` [L209] - `2026-01-15-orchestration-patterns.md` [L210] - `2026-03-01-five-year-roadmap-retrospective.md` [L211] [L212] ### Series Naming [L213] [L214] Series identifiers are lowercase, hyphenated strings that group related essays: [L215] [L216] ``` [L217] meta-system-foundations [L218] building-in-public [L219] organ-deep-dives [L220] ``` [L221] [L222] Series names should be descriptive and stable. Once published, a series name should not change (it is used in URLs and cross-references). [L223] [L224] ### Tag Taxonomy [L225] [L226] Tags are drawn from a controlled vocabulary. The initial tag set includes: [L227] [L228] - **System tags:** `meta-system`, `orchestration`, `governance`, `architecture`, `infrastructure` [L229] - **Organ tags... (truncated)

Named after the medieval *studium generale* — not a building but a right: the authority to teach, examine, and confer recognition anywhere. [L8] [L9] ## Role in the System [L10] [L11] The planning corpus (`organvm-corpvs-testamentvm`) governs **system state** — registry, rules, metrics, planning. This repo governs **process and knowledge** — how we work, how we learn, how we evaluate, and how we certify original contributions. The SGO shares IRA evaluation machinery with `auto-revision-epistemic-engine` for multi-model evaluative consensus. [L12] [L13] **Organ:** META-ORGANVM | **Tier:** standard | **Status:** GRADUATED [L14] [L15] ## Directory Structure [L16] [L17] ``` [L18] governance/ SGO constitutional documents (charter, defense protocol, faculty registry, senate config) [L19] strategy/ Defense rubrics (universal + per-faculty) and external feedback tracker [L20] research/ Research corpus (47+ docs) and dissertation-institutional-authority/ (11 chapters) [L21] commissions/ Research commission inquiry log (SGO-2026-D-001, SGO-2026-D-002) [L22] standards/ Governing standards: 64 METADOCs and SOPs [L23] templates/ Reusable review scaffolds (session reviews, audits, decision logs) [L24] sessions/ Dated session logs (append-only archive) [L25] lessons/ Extracted principles (living documents, updated as patterns emerge) [L26] archive/ Superseded standards (never deleted, moved here) [L27] ``` [L28] [L29] ## SGO Academic Structure [L30] [L31] **Faculties:** 8 faculties mapping 1:1 to ORGANVM organs, each with its own rubric dimensions and methodological traditions. [L32] [L33] **Academic Tiers:** [L34] [L35] | Tier | Name | Panel | ICC Threshold | [L36] |------|------|-------|--------------| [L37] | I | Paper | 4 standing | > 0.61 | [L38] | II | Thesis | 4 standing + 1 guest | > 0.70 | [L39] | III | Dissertation | 4 standing + 2 guests + Provost | > 0.75 | [L40] [L41] **Inaugural Dissertations:** [L42] - SGO-2026-D-001: *Precision Over Volume* — MCDA framework for application pipeline management (Faculty of Applied Systems) [L43] - SGO-2026-D-002: *Recursive Institutional Governance* — Multi-model evaluative consensus (Faculty of Governance + Meta-Cognition) [L44] [L45] ## Standards [L46] [L47] | Document | Scope | [L48] |----------|-------| [L49] | `governance/charter.yaml` | SGO constitutional document — identity, authority, principles | [L50] | `governance/defense-protocol.yaml` | 8-stage defense protocol with ICC thresholds | [L51] | `governance/faculty-registry.yaml` | 8 faculties, rubric mappings, guest examiners | [L52] | `governance/senate-config.yaml` | Standing senate (4 models), convocation rules | [L53] | `strategy/defense-rubrics/universal.yaml` | 8 evaluation dimensions with 1-10 scale anchors | [L54] | `METADOC--research-standards.md` | Architectural typology and research methodology | [L55] | `SOP--session-self-critique.md` | Daily session review process | [L56] | `SOP--cross-agent-handoff.md` | Receiving work from external AI agents | [L57] [L58] ## Usage [L59] [L60] 1. Update `lessons/derived-principles.md` with any new patterns [L64] [L65] ## Versioning [L66] [L67] Standards are never overwritten. Revisions create new versions (`-v2.md`, `-v3.md`). Pre-Production [L13] [L14] ### 1.1 Object Selection [L15] [L16] - [ ] Object selected from candidates list (`amp-lab/object-candidates.md`) [L17] - [ ] Research brief completed (`templates/object-research-brief.md`) [L18] - [ ] Chris sign-off on object choice [L19] - [ ] Priority score confirmed >= 3 [L20] [L21] **Selected object:** [name] [L22] **Research brief link:** [path or URL] [L23] [L24] ### 1.2 Film Research [L25] [L26] **Target:** 50-70+ films spanning 1901-present [L27] [L28] - [ ] Initial film list compiled (aim for 100+ candidates) [L29] - [ ] List narrowed to strongest 50-70 appearances [L30] - [ ] Films organized by era/theme/meaning (not just chronology) [L31] - [ ] Cross-checked against multiple sources (film databases, essays, academic references) [L32] - [ ] Director diversity verified (not over-indexed on one filmmaker) [L33] - [ ] Genre spread confirmed (drama, horror, comedy, animation, documentary, international) [L34] - [ ] Historical range confirmed (silent era through contemporary) [L35] [L36] **Film count:** ___ [L37] **Earliest film:** [title, year] [L38] **Latest film:** [title, year] [L39] **Directors represented:** ___ [L40] [L41] ### 1.3 Clip Sourcing and Timestamping [L42] [L43] - [ ] Clips identified with exact timestamps for each film [L44] - [ ] Source noted for each clip (streaming platform, physical media, public domain archive) [L45] - [ ] Fair use assessment completed for each clip (transformative commentary, brief duration) [L46] - [ ] Clip quality rated (HD preferred, SD acceptable for rare/historic footage) [L47] - [ ] Backup sources identified for critical clips [L48] [L49] **Clip log format:** [L50] [L51] | # | Film | Year | Director | Timestamp | Source | Quality | Fair Use Notes | [L52] |---|------|------|----------|-----------|--------|---------|----------------| [L53] | 1 | | | | | | | | [L54] [L55] ### 1.4 Narration Script [L56] [L57] - [ ] Thesis statement drafted [L58] - [ ] Script structured by theme/meaning (not chronological order) [L59] - [ ] Opening hook written (sets up the question the episode answers) [L60] - [ ] Historical context sections written [L61] - [ ] Analysis/pattern sections written [L62] - [ ] Closing synthesis written [L63] - [ ] Script timed against target (narrated sections = ~6-7 minutes of 10-12 total) [L64] - [ ] Chris review and revision [L65] - [ ] Final script locked [L66] [L67] **Word count:** ___ [L68] **Estimated narrated runtime:** ___ [L69] [L70] ### 1.5 Music Selection [L71] [L72] - [ ] ETCETER4 briefed on episode tone and pacing [L73] - [ ] Music style/mood agreed (underscore, not competing with narration) [L74] - [ ] Tracks delivered and reviewed [L75] - [ ] Licensing/rights confirmed for YouTube use [L76] - [ ] Music cue sheet created [L77] [L78] --- [L79] [L80] ## 2. Production Structure [L81] [L82] The Object Lessons hybrid format alternates between narrated analysis and non-narrated supercut sequences. [L83] [L84] ### COLD OPEN (0:00 - 1:00) [L85] [L86] **Type:** Non-narrated supercut montage [L87] **Purpose:** Immediate visual impact -- the most striking, surprising, or beautiful appearances of the object across cinema history [L88] [L89] - [ ] 8-15 clips selected for maximum visual diversity [L90] - [ ] Pacing builds energy (slow cuts to rapid) [L91] - [ ] No text overlays, no narration -- pure cinema [L92] - [ ] Music bed established [L93] - [ ] Final clip creates natural transition into thesis [L94] [L95] **Clip count:** ___ [L96] **Music cue:** ___ [L97] [L98] ### THESIS (1:00 - 2:00) [L99] [L100] **Type:** Narrated introduction [L101] **Purpose:** Frame the question -- what does this object mean in cinema? Why does it keep appearing? [L102] [L103] - [ ] Opens with a provocative claim or question [L104] - [ ] Establishes the object's ubiquity across film history [L105] - [ ] Previews the journey without spoiling the analysis [L106] - [ ] Hooks the viewer into staying for the full essay [L107] - [ ] Transition into historical survey feels natural [L108] [L109] **Key line:** ___ [L110] [L111] ### HISTORICAL SURVEY (2:00 - 8:00) [L112] [L113] **Type:** Narrated analysis with supporting clips [L114] **Purpose:** The core essay -- tracing the object's meaning across directors, eras, and genres [L115] [L116] Structure options (choose one or blend): [L117] - **Chronological:** Silent era to present, showing how meaning evolves [L118] - **Thematic:** Grouped by what the object represents (innocence, power, death, etc.) [L119] - **Dialectical:** Contrasting pairs (sacred vs. profane, luxury vs. poverty) [L120] [L121] - [ ] 3-5 major sections/themes identified [L122] - [ ] Each section has 8-15 supporting clips [L123] - [ ] Narration leads, clips illustrate (not the reverse) [L124] - [ ] Cross-director connections highlighted [L125] - [ ] At least one unexpected or obscure example per section [L126] - [ ] Pacing varies (dense analysis alternates with breathing room) [L127] [L128] **Sections:** [L129] [L130] | Section | Theme | Key Films | Duration | [L131] |---------|-------|-----------|----------| [L132] | 1 | | | | [L133] | 2 | | | | [L134] | 3 | | | | [L135] | 4 | | | | [L136] | 5 | | | | [L137] [L138] ### ANALYSIS (8:00 - 10:00) [L139] [L140] **Type:** Narrated synthesis [L141] **Purpose:** Emergent patterns -- what does the full survey reveal that individual examples don't? [L142] [L143] - [ ] Patterns identified across the survey (recurring contexts, director obsessions, cultural shifts) [L144] - [ ] At least one insight that could only emerge from seeing all appearances together [L145] - [ ] Connection to broader filmmaking craft (cinematography, production design, mise-en-scene) [L146] - [ ] Avoids being reductive -- acknowledges complexity and contradiction [L147] - [ ] Sets up the emotional resonance of the closing montage [L148] [L149] ### CLOSING MONTAGE (10:00 - 11:00) [L150] [L151] **Type:** Non-narrated supercut [L152] **Purpose:** Recontextualize -- after the analysis, the viewer sees these images differently [L153] [L154] - [ ] 10-20 clips, mix of previously shown and new [L155] - [ ] Pacing and music build to an emotional crescendo [L156] - [ ] Final image is carefully chosen (lingers, resonates) [L157] - [ ] No narration -- let the accumulated meaning do the work [L158] - [ ] Distinct feel from cold open (cold open = curiosity, closing = understanding) [L159] [L160] **Final image:** ___ [L161] [L162] ### CTA + END SCREEN (11:00+) [L163] [L164] **Type:** Narrated call to action + YouTube end screen elements [L165] **Purpose:** Convert viewers into subscribers and drive to next video [L166] [L167] - [ ] "Which object should we explore next?" (engagement prompt) [L168] - [ ] Subscribe reminder (brief, not desperate) [L169] - [ ] End screen: next video suggestion, subscribe button, playlist link [L170] - [ ] Pinned comment prepared (question + link to related episode) [L171] [L172] --- [L173] [L174] ## 3. Post-Production [L175] [L176] ### 3.1 SEO Optimization [L177] [L178] - [ ] SEO checklist completed (`templates/seo-optimization-checklist.md`) [L179] - [ ] Title finalized (not "Object Lesson: X" -- descriptive and searchable) [L180] - [ ] Description written with timestamps, film list, and links [L181] - [ ] Tags selected (8-12) [L182] - [ ] Thumbnail created and reviewed [L183] - [ ] Chapter markers set [L184] [L185] ### 3.2 Thumbnail [L186] [L187] - [ ] 1280x720, 16:9, under 2MB [L188] - [ ] Object is visual hero with cinematic lighting [L189] - [ ] 3-5 words of bold text [L190] - [ ] Readable at mobile size [L191] - [ ] Consistent with Object Lessons visual template [L192] - [ ] A/B variant prepared [L193] [L194] ### 3.3 Shorts Extraction [L195] [L196] - [ ] 3-5 Shorts identified from episode footage [L197] - [ ] Each Short is self-contained (makes sense without full video context) [L198] - [ ] Text overlay: "Full video on my channel" [L199] - [ ] Pinned comment on each Short links to full episode [L200] - [ ] Shorts scheduled across the following 2-3 weeks [L201] [L202] **Shorts plan:** [L203] [L204] | # | Clip/Theme | Duration | Scheduled Date | [L205] |---|------------|----------|----------------| [L206] | 1 | | | | [L207] | 2 | | | | [L208] | 3 | | | | [L209] | 4 | | | | [L210] | 5 | | | | [L211] [L212] ### 3.4 Upload and Launch [L213] [L214] - [ ] Video uploaded (unlisted initially for review) [L215] - [ ] All metadata applied (title, description, tags, thumbnail, end screens, cards) [L216] - [ ] Premiere scheduled or publish time set (optimal: Tuesday/Thursday, 12-3pm EST) [L217] - [ ] Community post announcing release (if 500+ subs) [L218] - [ ] Pinned comment posted immediately after publish [L219] - [ ] Shared to relevant subreddits, social, and collaborator networks [L220] [L221] --- [L222] [L223] ## 4. Tracking [L224] [L225] ### 4.1 Content ID Claims [L226] [L227] | Claim Date | Film Claimed | Claimant | Status | Resolution | [L228] |------------|-------------|----------|--------|------------| [L229] | | | | | | [L230] [L231] ### 4.2 Performance Metrics [L232] [L233] | Metric | 24 Hours | 7 Days | 30 Days | 90 Days | [L234] |--------|----------|--------|---------|---------| [L235] | Views | | | | | [L236] | Watch time (hours) | | | | | [L237] | Average view duration | | | | | [L238] | Click-through rate | | | | | [L239] | Likes | | | | | [L240] | Comments | | | | | [L241] | Shares | | | | | [L242] | Subscribers gained | | | | | [L243] | Impressions | | | | | [L244] [L245] ### 4.3 Shorts Performance [L246] [L247] | Short | Views (7d) | Views (30d) | Full Video Clicks | Subs Gained | [L248] |-------|-----------|-------------|-------------------|-------------| [L249] | 1 | | | | | [L250] | 2 | | | | | [L251] | 3 | | | | | [L252] | 4 | | | | | [L253] | 5 | | | | | [L254] [L255] ### 4.4 Lessons Learned [L256] [L257] **What worked:** [L258] [L259] **What to improve:** [L260] [L261] **Audience feedback themes:**

Citation Marker: fileciteturn56file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: 1d35923312d36aca06a3fb0b8f70d5cdab33d021 [L3] content: # CLAUDE.md [L4] [L5] This file provides guidance to Claude Code when working in this repository. [L6] [L7] ## What This Is [L8] [L9] **Object Lessons — The Recurring Objects of Cinema.** Editorial website for the AMP Lab Media YouTube channel ([@AmpLabMedia](https://youtube.com/@AmpLabMedia)). Built with Astro 5.x, deployed to Cloudflare Pages at [object-lessons.pages.dev](https://object-lessons.pages.dev) / custom domain `objectlessons.film`. [L10] [L11] The site traces recurring objects (milk, mirrors, cigarettes, clocks, doors, guns…) across a hundred years of film, pairing YouTube essays with written research briefs and a 253-film YAML database. [L12] [L13] **Author:** Anthony James Padavano [L14] **Organ:** META-ORGANVM | **Parent repo:** `praxis-perpetua` (own git repo, tracked as a nested project — not a submodule of praxis-perpetua) [L15] [L16] ## Commands [L17] [L18] ```bash [L19] npm install [L20] [L21] npm run dev # local dev server (astro dev) [L22] npm run build # production build: validate → og → astro build [L23] npm run validate # category validation (scripts/validate-categories.ts) [L24] npm run og # generate OG images (scripts/generate-og-images.ts) [L25] npm run preview # preview production build locally [L26] npm run check # astro type check [L27] ``` [L28] [L29] The `build` script always runs validation and OG generation before the Astro build. Never skip them. [L167] [L168] ## Important Notes [L169] [L170] - **React 18 is required.** Do not upgrade to React 19. Cloudflare Workers' runtime lacks `MessageChannel` at module initialization time, which React 19 requires. The `react` and `react-dom` dependencies are pinned to `18.3.1`. [L171] - **Essays are full research briefs.** The `.mdx` bodies under `src/content/essays/` are complete scholarly texts. Never condense or summarize them — treat the full text as load-bearing content. [L172] - **V1 YouTube IDs are placeholders.** The four V1 episodes (balloons, cereal, eggs, telephones) have `youtube_id: "PLACEHOLDER"`. Replace with real IDs before publishing the V2 site. [L173] - **Validate before building.** `npm run validate` checks that all `symbolic_category` kebab slugs in the film database match the schemas. The build will fail if validation fails — this is intentional. [L174] - **D1 is only available at runtime.** During `astro dev` with `platformProxy: { enabled: true }`, Wrangler proxies D1 locally. Without it, D1 bindings are `undefined` — the API routes handle this gracefully with 503 responses.

Build this as a modular monorepo with `apps/web`, `apps/api`, and `apps/worker`, backed by shared `packages/domain`, `packages/database`, `packages/client-sdk`, `packages/config`, and `packages/observability`. The database must preserve a strict split between mutable `scene` state and immutable `scene_version` snapshots, with normalized `version_line` rows and atomic version-creation transactions. The API owns parsing, persistence, versioning, diagnostics orchestration, and render-job submission; the worker owns asynchronous audio rendering through provider adapters and artifact storage. Implement in sequence: schema and contracts first, then scene import and parse review, then versioning, then render pipeline and playback, then diagnostics and share flows. Do not let the system collapse into generic TTS architecture; preserve dramatic language as structured temporal data across schema, services, events, and UI. [L17] **Canonical descriptive title:** **Speech-Score Composition System**. [L18] **Permitted secondary designation:** **Post-Script Theatrical Composition Environment**. [L19] This naming is the cleanest convergence of the project materials because it preserves the central claim that language is handled as semantic content, rhythmic material, and executable score, while also preserving the system-level scope across writing, notation, rehearsal, rendering, and live execution. fileciteturn3file9turn3file17turn3file13 [L20] [L21] ## 1. $DEFINITION [L22] [L23] **$SPEECH_SCORE_ENGINE := a compositional system for authoring, analyzing, rehearsing, rendering, and performing polyvocal speech works in which text, timing, recurrence, vocal distribution, and spatial notation are interoperable across analog and digital forms.** Its core premise is that a dramatic utterance is never only text on a page; it is also a timed event, a vocal gesture, a positional relation among voices, and a repeatable performance object. fileciteturn3file9turn3file12 [L24] [L25] The system exists to convert language into a scored, repeatable, transformable performance object across page, audio, rehearsal, and live event. Its primary law is not merely that dialogue can be heard, but that meaning can be reorganized through timing, repetition, overlap, silence, redistribution, and recurrence without ceasing to be language. That is why the project is properly defined as a speech-score system rather than a script tool or voice utility. fileciteturn3file13turn3file6turn3file17 [L26] [L27] ## 2. $SYSTEM_SCOPE [L28] [L29] Inside the canonical system boundary belong six domains: authorship, notation, analysis, rehearsal, rendering, and execution. Authorship covers the creation and transformation of phrase-events. Notation covers score-encoding of speech. Analysis covers dramaturgical and rhythmic inspection. Rehearsal covers the generation of performer-facing and conductor-facing artifacts. Rendering covers audio, score, and related output views. Execution covers live coordination of voices, cues, and timing states. This scope is explicit in the system materials and should remain stable. fileciteturn3file12turn3file9 [L30] [L31] The commercially accessible surface of the system may present as a **dramaturgical-audio workbench** or **listening-first revision environment for performative text**, but that is the entry layer, not the total ontology. The surface solves the immediate workflow problem of hearing and revising dialogue quickly. The deeper layer remains the polyphonic speech-composition engine. Internal documentation should preserve this two-layer distinction. fileciteturn3file11turn3file14turn3file19 [L32] [L33] ## 3. $STRICT_TERMINOLOGY [L34] [L35] **$PHRASE_EVENT** is the primary compositional unit. Do not use “line” as the fundamental ontological term except when referring to imported legacy scripts. A line is a legacy textual presentation. A phrase-event is the actual system object: a bounded utterance or fragment with semantic content, speaker assignment, temporal position, duration behavior, and relational status. fileciteturn3file12turn3file9 [L36] [L37] **$VOICE_CHANNEL** is the canonical term for the speaking carrier in the system. Do not collapse this into “character” except in user-facing simplifications. A character may map to one or more voice-channels, and a voice-channel may carry character, chorus, synthetic, or abstract textual functions. The system therefore distinguishes character identity from voice-channel identity. fileciteturn3file6turn3file17 [L38] [L39] **$TEMPORAL_RELATION** is the primary organizing principle. Do not describe the system as plot-first, script-first, or scene-first at the deep-structure level. Scene remains a user-facing container, but temporal relation governs composition. This includes cue order, overlap, delay, subdivision, recurrence, silence, and relative entry logic. fileciteturn3file17turn3file6 [L40] [L41] **$TIMING_SUBSTRATE** is the canonical term for the temporal kernel. Do not replace it with vague terms like “playback settings” when discussing core architecture. The timing substrate handles clock time, beat time, relative cue time, and elastic time, and is what permits literary timing and compositional timing to coexist in one engine. fileciteturn3file6 [L42] [L43] **$NOTATION_RENDERER** is the canonical output term for score views. Do not refer to these outputs merely as “formatted scripts.” The renderer produces readable script view, rhythmic score view, spatial matrix view, and performer-specific part view. fileciteturn3file6 [L44] [L45] **$REHEARSAL_KERNEL** is the canonical bridge from composition objects to practical rehearsal artifacts. Use this instead of generic phrases like “practice mode” when referring to the system service. It generates cue sheets, click guides, count-ins, stems, and section isolations. fileciteturn3file6 [L46] [L47] **$LIVE_EXECUTION_LAYER** is the canonical term for real-time coordination during performance. Do not reduce this to “presentation mode.” It includes conductor view, countdown view, tempo map, monitor prompts, and rule-bound override behavior. fileciteturn3file6 [L48] [L49] **$ANALYSIS_ENGINE** is the canonical term for dramaturgical diagnostics. Do not call this “AI critique” in core documentation. Its job is narrower and more defensible: identification of pacing drag, weak speaker differentiation, rhythm monotony, broken interruption logic, overlong turns, and related structural issues. fileciteturn3file6turn3file14 [L50] [L51] ## 4. $EXCLUSIONS [L52] [L53] The system is **not** canonically defined as text-to-speech software. Synthetic voice is infrastructure within the system, not the category to which the system belongs. Any naming, pitch, or documentation that frames the project primarily as a TTS layer weakens the ontology and collapses the moat. fileciteturn3file9turn3file8turn3file19 [L54] [L55] The system is **not** merely a playwriting app, a podcast maker, a voice generator, a screenplay reader, or a one-off experimental play framework. Those may be adjacent use cases, source materials, or entry surfaces, but the canonical project is the underlying generative framework for an entire class of works in which speech behaves as composed performance. fileciteturn3file17turn3file13 [L56] [L57] The system is **not** a total production-management platform. Costume, scenic design, payroll, ticketing, and unrelated production logistics remain outside the formal boundary unless later integrated as secondary modules. This exclusion should remain explicit in all architectural definitions. fileciteturn3file12 [L58] [L59] For MVP framing, the system is **not yet** defined by full overlap composition, conductor infrastructure, multimodal staging visualization, deep collaboration, or institutional administration. Those belong to later layers, not the canonical proof point. The first proof remains: users can hear and revise dialogue as performed time. fileciteturn3file10turn3file15 [L60] [L61] ## 5. $NAMING_RULES [L62] [L63] At the **system level**, use **Title Case** for outward-facing category names and **$UPPER_SNAKE_CASE** for canonical internal entities. Example: “Speech-Score Composition System” in prose; **$SPEECH_SCORE_ENGINE** in specs. This keeps the conceptual name legible while preserving machine-like ontological precision. [L64] [L65] At the **object level**, prefer names that indicate function rather than medium artifact. Use **$PHRASE_EVENT**, **$VOICE_CHANNEL**, **$TIMING_SUBSTRATE**, **$NOTATION_RENDERER**, **$REHEARSAL_KERNEL**, and **$LIVE_EXECUTION_LAYER**. Avoid weak nouns such as “block,” “piece,” “thing,” “snippet,” “clip,” or “dialogue chunk” in formal documentation unless they are explicitly mapped as aliases for onboarding. [L66] [L67] At the **user-surface level**, permit simplified expressions only where adoption clarity requires them. “Scene,” “speaker,” “readback,” and “dialogue playback” are acceptable UX language, but internal schemas and specifications should always map those terms back to the deeper canonical object model. This preserves the long-term ontology while supporting the commercial wedge of a dramaturgical-audio studio. fileciteturn3file11turn3file19 [L68] [L69] At the **category level**, prefer names that preserve the project’s hybrid ontology. Approved pattern families are: **speech-score**, **polyvocal**, **post-script**, **dramaturgical-audio**, **performance-writing**, and **temporal writing intelligence**. Dispreferred pattern families are: **AI voice**, **script reader**, **podcast engine**, **theatre chatbot**, and **audio toy**, because they either over-trivialize or misclassify the system. fileciteturn3file19turn3file14turn3file8 [L70] [L71] At the **narrative level**, one sentence should always remain stable: [L72] [L73] **The system treats language as a dual-aspect object: semantic content and timed performance.** [L74] [L75] That sentence is the naming test. If a new label obscures either side of that duality, reject it. This follows directly from the system invariant that language must remain both meaning-bearing and temporally operative. fileciteturn3file6 [L76] [L77] ## 6. $CANONICAL_EXPRESSION_SET [L78] [L79] Use this as the stable vocabulary core for future specs. It isolates the operative problem: [L39] [L40] **how to make short-form theatre function with musical precision in audio and live performance.** [L41] [L42] Your reaction is not primarily literary. It is infrastructural. You are asking: [L43] [L44] “How is this conducted?” [L45] “Is there a click track?” [L46] “How do they keep the internal rhythm aligned?” [L47] “How is the timing held together?” [L48] [L49] That means your attention is on **execution architecture**, not merely on text appreciation. Citation Marker: fileciteturn61file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: c98798a2653dfaf3eb80655446250672a3167611 [L3] content: # Salon Archive [L4] [L5] [![ORGAN-VI: Koinonia](https://img.shields.io/badge/ORGAN--VI-Koinonia-4a148c?style=flat-square)](https://github.com/organvm-vi-koinonia) [L6] [![Access: Private](https://img.shields.io/badge/Access-Private%20%2F%20Invitation--Only-333333?style=flat-square)]() [L7] [![Status: Active](https://img.shields.io/badge/Status-Active-4caf50?style=flat-square)]() [L8] [L9] > Transcripts, session metadata, topic taxonomy, and discovery infrastructure for salon-style intellectual gatherings within the organvm eight-organ system. [L10] [L11] [Purpose](#purpose) | [Participation Model](#participation-model) | [Session Formats](#session-formats) | [Archive Structure](#archive-structure) | [Metadata Schema](#metadata-schema) | [Topic Taxonomy](#topic-taxonomy) | [Transcription Pipeline](#transcription-pipeline) | [Search & Discovery](#search--discovery) | [Community Guidelines](#community-guidelines) | [Access & Invitations](#access--invitations) | [Contributing](#contributing) | [Channels & Platforms](#channels--platforms) | [Code of Conduct](#code-of-conduct) | [Inspirations & Lineage](#inspirations--lineage) | [Author & Contact](#author--contact) [L12] [L13] --- [L14] [L15] ## Purpose [L16] [L17] The salon archive is the institutional memory of ORGAN-VI. Every gathering, conversation, and collaborative encounter that takes place within the koinonia community produces ideas, connections, and insights that deserve to persist beyond the moment of their articulation. This repository provides the infrastructure to capture, organize, index, and make discoverable the full output of the salon program. [L18] [L19] Without an archive, salons become ephemeral social events — enjoyable but structurally invisible. The conviction underlying this repository is that intellectual community produces *artifacts*, not just experiences. A well-indexed transcript is as much an output of the organvm system as a deployed application or a published essay. The salon archive transforms dialogue into a searchable, cross-referenced knowledge base that feeds back into the system: theorists in ORGAN-I discover connections they missed, artists in ORGAN-II find provocations for new work, and the public-process documentation in ORGAN-V draws on salon discussions for essay material. [L20] [L21] This is not a simple recording dump. The archive is designed with the same care applied to any other production system in the eight-organ model. Every session receives structured metadata, every transcript is indexed against the topic taxonomy, and every cross-reference to work in other organs is explicitly linked. The goal is an archive that becomes more valuable over time — a compounding resource for the community and for the system as a whole. [L22] [L23] ## Participation Model [L24] [L25] Salon participation operates on an invitation-only basis. This is a deliberate architectural choice, not gatekeeping for its own sake. The salons are designed for a specific kind of engagement: participants are expected to have read preparatory materials, to engage substantively with the session's provocation, and to contribute to the documented outcomes. Open attendance would dilute the depth of exchange that makes the format generative. [L26] [L27] Invitations are extended by the salon facilitator in consultation with the ORGAN-VI community steward. The criteria for invitation are intellectual engagement and willingness to participate in structured dialogue, not credentials or institutional affiliation. A thoughtful autodidact is as welcome as an academic; a practicing artist is as welcome as a theorist. What matters is the capacity and willingness to contribute to the shared inquiry. [L28] [L29] Participants in any given salon session are recorded in the session metadata (with consent). Repeat participation builds familiarity and depth — the salon program is designed for an evolving cohort, not a rotating cast of strangers. Over time, participants develop shared references, running threads, and the kind of productive shorthand that makes deep intellectual work possible in a group setting. [L30] [L31] New participants are typically introduced through a specific session rather than joining the archive directly. The archive itself is accessible to all community members — you do not need to have attended a salon to read its transcript — but the live sessions maintain a curated participant list to preserve the conditions for substantive exchange. [L32] [L33] ## Session Formats [L34] [L35] The salon program offers several distinct formats, each designed for a different mode of intellectual engagement. The format is selected by the facilitator based on the session's topic, the available participants, and the desired outcome. [L36] [L37] ### Lightning Talks [L38] [L39] Short, focused presentations (10-15 minutes) followed by structured Q&A. Lightning talks are designed for sharing work-in-progress, introducing new concepts, or presenting preliminary findings. The constraint of brevity forces clarity — speakers must distill their contribution to its essential argument. Lightning talk sessions typically feature three to four speakers, with facilitated discussion weaving the presentations into a coherent conversation. These sessions are particularly effective for surfacing unexpected connections between different parts of the organvm system. [L40] [L41] ### Deep Dives [L42] [L43] Extended single-topic explorations (60-90 minutes) in which a facilitator guides the group through a prepared investigation of a specific question, framework, or problem. Deep dives begin with a provocation — a reading, a demonstration, a question — and proceed through structured phases of exploration, critique, and synthesis. The facilitator's role is not to lecture but to maintain the thread of inquiry, ensuring the group goes deep rather than wide. Deep dives produce the most substantive archive entries, as the extended format allows ideas to develop through multiple iterations of challenge and refinement. [L44] [L45] ### Socratic Dialogues [L46] [L47] Facilitated exchanges structured around a specific question, using the Socratic method of guided inquiry. The facilitator poses questions rather than providing answers, drawing out the assumptions, implications, and contradictions in participants' positions. Socratic dialogues are particularly valuable for examining foundational commitments — what do we mean by "recursive" in the context of ORGAN-I? What constitutes "community" in a distributed creative practice? These sessions often surface disagreements and tensions that are productive rather than divisive, revealing the genuine complexity of the system's conceptual landscape. [L48] [L49] ### Collaborative Ideation [L50] [L51] Working sessions designed to produce specific outputs: a new project concept, a curriculum outline, an essay draft, a governance proposal. Collaborative ideation sessions are less about exploration and more about production. They begin with a clear brief, proceed through structured brainstorming and critique, and conclude with an artifact that can be developed further. The archive for these sessions includes not only the transcript but the output artifact itself, with attribution to all contributors. [L52] [L53] ## Archive Structure [L54] [L55] The archive is organized around sessions as the primary unit. Each session receives its own directory containing all associated materials. [L56] [L57] ``` [L58] sessions/ [L59] YYYY-MM-DD--session-slug/ [L60] transcript.md # Full session transcript (cleaned, attributed) [L61] metadata.yaml # Structured session metadata [L62] notes.md # Facilitator notes and post-session reflections [L63] materials/ # Pre-session readings, slides, reference materials [L64] outputs/ # Artifacts produced during the session [L65] media/ # Audio recordings, images, screenshots (if applicable) [L66] ``` [L67] [L68] Sessions are indexed by date, topic, format, and participants. The archive's root contains aggregate indices that allow multiple navigation paths into the material: [L69] [L70] ``` [L71] index/ [L72] by-date.md # Chronological session listing [L73] by-topic.md # Sessions organized by topic taxonomy [L74] by-format.md # Sessions grouped by format type [L75] by-participant.md # Sessions indexed by participant (with consent) [L76] cross-references.md # Links between sessions and other organ repos [L77] taxonomy/ [L78] organ-mapping.yaml # Topic-to-organ alignment [L79] themes.yaml # Cross-cutting themes across sessions [L80] ``` [L81] [L82] The archive structure is designed to be both human-browsable and machine-parseable. A researcher can navigate the index files directly; a script can parse the YAML metadata to generate visualizations, statistics, or cross-reference reports. [L83] [L84] ## Metadata Schema [L85] [L86] Every session carries structured metadata in YAML format. The schema captures the information necessary for indexing, cross-referencing, and retrospective analysis. [L87] [L88] ```yaml [L89] session: [L90] id: "2026-03-15--recursive-ontology" [L91] date: 2026-03-15 [L92] format: deep-dive [L93] facilitator: "@4444J99" [L94] duration_minutes: 90 [L95] participants: [L96] - name: "Participant Name" [L97] handle: "@github-handle" [L98] consent_to_archive: true [L99] topic: [L100] title: "Recursive Ontology and Self-Referential Systems" [L101] taxonomy_tags: [L102] - recursion [L103] - ontology [L104] - self-reference [L105] organ_alignment: [L106] primary: ORGAN-I [L107] secondary: [ORGAN-II, ORGAN-IV] [L108] cross_references: [L109] - repo: "organvm-i-theoria/recursive-engine--generative-entity" [L110] context: "Discussion of RE:GE's ritual syntax as a model for recursive ontology" [L111] - repo: "organvm-ii-poiesis/metasystem-master" [L112] context: "Metasystem-master's orchestration layer as practical instantiation" [L113] materials: [L114] - type: reading [L115] title: "Hofstadter, Strange Loops chapter 12" [L116] - type: demonstration [L117] title: "Live walkthrough of recursive-engine ritual execution" [L118] outputs: [L119] - type: insight [L120] description: "Proposed extension of ritual syntax to handle nested self-reference" [L121] - type: action_item [L122] description: "Draft specification for recursive ritual nesting (assigned to @4444J99)" [L123] transcript_word_count: 4200 [L124] notes: "Particularly generative session. The connection between ritual syntax and self-referential ontology had not been explicitly drawn before." [L125] ``` [L126] [L127] The schema is extensible — additional fields can be added for specific session formats — but these core fields are required for every archived session. The `organ_alignment` field is particularly important: it makes explicit how each salon session connects to the broader eight-organ system, enabling the kind of cross-organ discovery that makes the archive a system resource rather than a standalone document collection. [L128] [L129] ## Topic Taxonomy [L130] [L131] The salon topic taxonomy is aligned with the eight-organ model, providing a shared vocabulary for categorizing and cross-referencing session content. [L132] [L133] **ORGAN-I (Theory) Topics:** recursion, ontology, epistemology, self-reference, formal systems, symbolic logic, framework design, philosophical foundations, system theory, emergence, autopoiesis, strange loops, category theory applied. [L134] [L135] **ORGAN-II (Art) Topics:** generative art, algorithmic composition, performance systems, interactive media, aesthetic philosophy, creative process, artistic intention, medium specificity, audience experience, exhibition design, sound art, visual systems, procedural generation. [L136] [L137] **ORGAN-III (Commerce) Topics:** creative sustainability, product design, business models for art, SaaS architecture, public data infrastructure, open source economics, value capture, ethical commerce, pricing philosophy, B2B creative tools. [L138] [L139] **ORGAN-IV (Orchestration) Topics:** governance models, dependency management, promotion criteria, system health, automation philosophy, workflow design, institutional structure, decision-making processes, quality gates. [L140] [L141] **ORGAN-V (Public Process) Topics:** building in public, transparency, documentation as practice, essay craft, long-form writing, publication ethics, audience relationship, knowledge sharing. [L142] [L143] **ORGAN-VI (Community) Topics:** facilitation, salon design, curriculum development, collaborative inquiry, community governance, inclusion, intellectual hospitality, gathering design, feedback culture. [L144] [L145] **ORGAN-VII (Marketing) Topics:** distribution strategy, audience building, POSSE methodology, content repurposing, announcement craft, narrative framing, public communication. [L146] [L147] **Cross-cutting themes** span multiple organs: sustainability, scale, ethics, recursion-in-practice, human-AI collaboration, institutional design, portfolio as practice, creative infrastructure. [L148] [L149] ## Transcription Pipeline [L150] [L151] Session transcription follows a defined pipeline to ensure accuracy, attribution, and archival quality. [L152] [L153] **Stage 1: Capture.** Sessions are recorded (audio or video, with participant consent). The raw recording is stored in the session's `media/` directory. For text-based sessions (asynchronous or chat-format), the raw log serves as the capture artifact. [L154] [L155] **Stage 2: Draft Transcription.** AI-assisted transcription generates a first-pass transcript from the recording. Speaker attribution is assigned using voice identification or manual tagging. The draft transcript preserves the full session content, including digressions, tangents, and informal exchanges. [L156] [L157] **Stage 3: Editorial Review.** The facilitator reviews the draft transcript for accuracy, corrects misattributions, and annotates key moments (insights, decisions, action items). Participants are invited to review their own contributions for accuracy. The editorial review preserves the conversational character of the session while ensuring factual correctness. [L158] [L159] **Stage 4: Structuring.** The reviewed transcript is structured with section headings, cross-reference links, and taxonomy tags. Key quotes are highlighted. The `metadata.yaml` file is populated from the structured transcript. [L160] [L161] **Stage 5: Archival.** The structured transcript and metadata are committed to the archive. Index files are updated. Cross-reference links to other organ repos are verified and added to the cross-references index. [L162] [L163] The pipeline is designed to minimize the time between session and archival — ideally, a session is fully archived within one week of occurrence. The AI-assisted transcription stage reduces the bottleneck of manual transcription, while the editorial and structuring stages ensure quality. [L164] [L165] ## Search & Discovery [L166] [L167] The archive supports multiple discovery paths to serve different use cases. [L168] [L169] **Chronological browsing** — the `by-date.md` index provides a timeline view of all sessions, useful for understanding the salon program's evolution and for finding sessions from a specific period. [L170] [L171] **Topic-based search** — the `by-topic.md` index and `taxonomy/themes.yaml` allow navigation by subject matter. A researcher interested in recursion can find all sessions that engaged with recursive systems, regardless of format or date. [L172] [L173] **Cross-organ discovery** — the `cross-references.md` index maps salon sessions to specific repositories in other organs. A contributor to ORGAN-I's recursive-engine can find all salon discussions that referenced their work. This bidirectional linking makes the archive a navigation tool for the entire system, not just ORGAN-VI. [L174] [L175] **Participant-based access** — the `by-participant.md` index (populated with consent) allows participants to find all sessions they attended, creating a personal record of engagement with the community. [L176] [L177] **Full-text search** — transcript files are plain Markdown, enabling standard text search across the full archive. Combined with the structured metadata, this allows queries such as "all deep-dive sessions in which ontology was discussed and ORGAN-II repos were referenced." [L178] [L179] ## Community Guidelines [L180] [L181] The salon community operates according to principles that prioritize generative exchange over performative debate. [L182] [L183] **Intellectual generosity.** Assume good faith. Engage with the strongest version of a position, not the weakest. Offer your knowledge freely and receive others' contributions with curiosity rather than defensiveness. [L184] [L185] **Structured spontaneity.** Salons are facilitated, not free-form. Respect the facilitator's role in maintaining focus and managing time. Within the structure, be willing to follow unexpected threads — the most valuable insights often emerge from productive tangents. [L186] [L187] **Attribution and credit.** Ideas shared in salons belong to the community, but attribution matters. When an insight from a salon session influences work in another organ, credit the session and the contributor. The archive exists partly to make this attribution possible. [L188] [L189] **Consent and privacy.** Participation in a salon implies consent to archival of the session transcript, but participants can request redaction of specific contributions. The archive respects boundaries: some discussions may be marked as off-the-record at the facilitator's discretion. [L190] [L191] **Preparation.** Salon participants are expected to engage with pre-session materials. The depth of the exchange depends on participants arriving with shared context. If you cannot prepare for a session, communicate this to the facilitator in advance. [L192] [L193] ## Access & Invitations [L194] [L195] Access to the salon archive is governed by ORGAN-VI community membership. All community members can read archived transcripts. Participation in live sessions requires a specific invitation for each session or series. [L196] [L197] Invitations are extended based on relevance to the session topic, track record of substantive participation, and the facilitator's judgment about group composition. The... (truncated)

One home for transcripts, prompts, notes, and orphan artifacts across every agent and tool — so the local drive can be safely purged of regenerable junk. [L7] [L8] > **Restore principle:** this remote is canonical; local is a disposable cache. A fresh machine reconstitutes from `chezmoi apply` + `git clone` of the repo fleet + this archive. [L9] [L10] ## Usage [L11] [L12] This repository is not an installable package today: there is no `pyproject.toml`, [L13] `setup.py`, `package.json`, or exported console script. Run the repo-local shell [L14] and Python entrypoints from the repository root. [L15] [L16] ### Install [L17] [L18] ```bash [L19] git clone https://github.com/organvm/session-meta.git [L20] cd session-meta [L21] python3 --version [L22] ``` [L23] [L24] The ingest, analysis, scheduler, and test entrypoints use the Python standard [L25] library. `make lint` additionally expects `ruff` to be installed in the active [L26] Python environment. [L27] [L28] ### Run the ingest pipeline [L29] [L30] The canonical producer for the atom corpus is: [L31] [L32] ```bash [L33] ./ingest/refresh-atoms.sh [L34] ``` [L35] [L36] It writes, by default: [L37] [L38] - `ingest/manifest.jsonl` [L39] - `ingest/atoms.jsonl` [L40] [L41] `refresh-atoms.sh` always scans `data/session-transcripts`, then adds live JSONL [L42] provider roots only when the directories exist: [L43] [L44] - `~/.claude/projects` as `claude-projects` [L45] - `~/.codex/sessions` as `codex` [L46] - `~/.local/share/gemini` as `gemini` [L47] - `~/.cache/chatgpt-export` as `chatgpt` [L48] [L49] DB-backed sources are handled by `atomize.py`'s DB-extractor pass, not by [L50] `--extra-root`. When a manifest row has `fmt=="db"` and a registered source, that [L51] manifested DB path is used with the row's sha as provenance. If no manifested DB [L52] path is present: [L53] [L54] - `opencode` falls back to `data/session-transcripts/opencode/opencode.db`, then [L55] `~/.local/share/opencode/opencode.db`. [L56] - `antigravity` falls back to `data/session-transcripts/antigravity/conversations/*.db` [L57] and emits best-effort recovered atoms tagged with `meta.recovery`. [L58] [L59] `refresh-atoms.sh` has no CLI flags. Override output paths with env vars for [L60] dry-runs: [L61] [L62] ```bash [L63] MANIFEST_OUT=scratch-manifest.jsonl ATOMS_OUT=scratch-atoms.jsonl ./ingest/refresh-atoms.sh [L64] ``` [L65] [L66] ### Key Commands + Flags [L67] [L68] Pipeline stages can be run directly: [L69] [L70] ```bash [L71] python3 ingest/manifest.py data/session-transcripts --out manifest.jsonl [L72] python3 ingest/atomize.py --manifest manifest.jsonl --out atoms.jsonl [L73] ``` [L74] [L75] - `python3 ingest/manifest.py <root>` [L76] - `--out PATH` (default: `ingest/manifest.jsonl`) [L77] - `--extra-root PATH:source` (repeatable; walks JSONL files only and forces `source`) [L78] - `--merge` (preserve existing manifest rows and upsert by path) [L79] - `python3 ingest/atomize.py` [L80] - `--manifest PATH` (default: `ingest/manifest.jsonl`) [L81] - `--out PATH` (default: `ingest/atoms.jsonl`) [L82] - `--source SOURCE` (limit processing to one source and its DB extractor, e.g. [L83] `codex` or `opencode`) [L84] [L85] `atomize.py` resolves relative manifest paths relative to the manifest file's [L86] directory. For direct ad hoc runs, keep the manifest at the repo root as shown [L87] above, or otherwise place it so its relative paths resolve to the files indexed [L88] by `manifest.py`. [L89] [L90] When atomizing manifest rows that contain absolute paths from `--extra-root`, [L91] set `INGEST_TRUSTED_ROOTS` to an `os.pathsep`-separated list of allowed roots. [L92] On macOS/Linux that separator is `:`. If unset, `atomize.py` trusts only [L93] `~/.claude/projects` for absolute manifest paths. [L94] [L95] Additional local entrypoints: [L96] [L97] - `python3 ingest/redact.py < input.txt > output.txt` redacts stdin to stdout and reports [L98] redaction counts on stderr. It has no flags. [L99] - `python3 analysis/federated_friction.py [--since YYYY-MM-DD] [--until YYYY-MM-DD] [--all] [--json-only]` [L100] writes `analysis/reports/friction-*.json`. Unless `--json-only` is set, it [L101] also writes `analysis/reports/LATEST.md` and prints the Markdown report. [L102] Defaults are `--since 2026-04-30` and `--until 2026-06-02`; `--all` ignores [L103] that date window. [L104] [L105] Scheduler actions are outward-facing GitHub issue updaters: [L106] [L107] - `python3 scheduler/agent-actions/plans-orphan-audit.py` [L108] - `python3 scheduler/agent-actions/limen-disposition-triage.py` [L109] [L110] They do not define CLI options. Configure them through environment/auth state: [L111] `GH_TOKEN`, `GITHUB_TOKEN`, or `gh auth token`; default `GITHUB_REPOSITORY` is [L112] `organvm/session-meta`. When credentials are available, the scripts may create, [L113] patch, or comment on their rolling GitHub issues. [L114] [L115] Run local checks with Make: [L116] [L117] ```bash [L118] make test [L119] make lint [L120] ``` [L121] [L122] ## What's here (`data/`) [L123] [L124] ### `data/session-transcripts/` — every agent's raw sessions [L125] | Path | Source | Contents | [L126] |---|---|---| [L127] | `claude/` | `~/.claude/projects` | Claude Code transcripts (2,627 `.jsonl`; Binary files were never text-rewritten. **`SECRETS-TO-REVOKE.txt` (kept local, NOT in this repo)** lists real-format credentials found in the logs — rotate any still active. [L156] [L157] ## Intentionally excluded (with reasons — not "swept under the rug") [L158] - **Credential stores** (Cursor/OpenAI/Perplexity/Notion/Claude-Desktop binary leveldb/IndexedDB): hold live auth tokens that can't be cleanly separated from content in binary form. Their conversations are **cloud-backed** (re-accessible by login); the correct preservation is each tool's **native export** (done for ChatGPT). See `data/session-transcripts/EXCLUDED-credential-stores.md`. [L159] - **Vendor installers** (`WarpPreview.dmg`, `iMazing3forMac.dmg`, `1Password Installer.app`): re-downloadable from the vendor. [L160] - **Proven duplicates** (`hash.zip` == the uncompressed `chatgpt/attachments/` already here; ChatGPT `source/conversations.json` == `export/`). [L161] - **Regenerable caches** (node_modules, GPUCache, browser profiles, VM images).
