Citation Marker: fileciteturn64file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: 9fb099a206de48f4c82d199a9c6e43bc6b785213 [L3] content: ![ORGAN-IV: Orchestration](https://img.shields.io/badge/ORGAN--IV-Orchestration%20(Taxis)-4A90D9?style=for-the-badge) ![Python 3.12](https://img.shields.io/badge/Python-3.12-3776AB?style=for-the-badge&logo=python&logoColor=white) ![GitHub Actions](https://img.shields.io/badge/GitHub%20Actions-5%20Workflows-2088FF?style=for-the-badge&logo=githubactions&logoColor=white) ![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/License-MIT-green?style=for-the-badge) [L4] [L5] # orchestration-start-here [L6] [L7] [![CI](https://github.com/organvm-iv-taxis/orchestration-start-here/actions/workflows/ci.yml/badge.svg)](https://github.com/organvm-iv-taxis/orchestration-start-here/actions/workflows/ci.yml) [L8] [![Coverage](https://img.shields.io/badge/coverage-pending-lightgrey)](https://github.com/organvm-iv-taxis/orchestration-start-here) [L9] [![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/License-MIT-blue.svg)](https://github.com/organvm-iv-taxis/orchestration-start-here/blob/main/LICENSE) [L10] [![Organ IV](https://img.shields.io/badge/Organ-IV%20Taxis-10B981)](https://github.com/organvm-iv-taxis) [L11] [![Status](https://img.shields.io/badge/status-active-brightgreen)](https://github.com/organvm-iv-taxis/orchestration-start-here) [L12] [![Python](https://img.shields.io/badge/lang-Python-informational)](https://github.com/organvm-iv-taxis/orchestration-start-here) [L13] [L14] [L15] **The central nervous system of an eight-organ creative-institutional architecture.** This repository is the single coordination point for 171 repositories distributed across 8 GitHub organizations, enforcing governance rules, validating cross-organ dependencies, running monthly system-wide health audits, and automating the promotion pipeline that moves work from theoretical research through artistic expression into commercial products. Everything in this system — every README deployed, every dependency validated, every essay published — flows through the data structures and automation pipelines defined here. [L16] [L17] --- [L18] [L19] ## Table of Contents [L20] [L21] - [Architectural Overview](#architectural-overview) [L22] - [The Eight-Organ Model](#the-eight-organ-model) [L23] - [Registry Documentation](#registry-documentation) [L24] - [Governance Model](#governance-model) [L25] - [GitHub Actions Workflows](#github-actions-workflows) [L26] - [Python Validation Scripts](#python-validation-scripts) [L27] - [Cross-Organ Dependency Map](#cross-organ-dependency-map) [L28] - [Portfolio Positioning](#portfolio-positioning) [L29] - [Quick Start](#quick-start) [L30] - [Contributing](#contributing) [L31] - [System Map](#system-map) [L32] - [Related Resources](#related-resources) [L33] [L34] --- [L35] [L36] ## Architectural Overview [L37] [L38] The organvm system organizes creative and institutional work into eight organs — discrete functional domains, each with its own GitHub organization, repository constellation, and operational mandate. The architecture draws from biological systems: organs are specialized but interdependent, and the system's health depends on coordination between them rather than any single organ's output. [L39] [L40] **Why eight organs?** The division mirrors the lifecycle of ideas in creative-institutional practice. Theoretical research (ORGAN-I) generates conceptual frameworks. Artistic practice (ORGAN-II) transforms those frameworks into experiential work. Commerce (ORGAN-III) packages that work for economic sustainability. The remaining five organs handle orchestration, public documentation, community, marketing, and meta-coordination — the connective tissue that makes the system function as a coherent whole rather than a collection of disconnected projects. [L41] [L42] **Why parallel launch?** Traditional project launches are sequential: build, then document, then ship. This system launched all eight organs simultaneously to demonstrate integrated systems thinking. A grant reviewer or hiring manager seeing one organ can follow dependency links to discover the entire architecture. The parallel approach also prevents the common failure mode of launching a single polished project while the supporting infrastructure remains invisible. [L43] [L44] **How this hub coordinates everything.** `orchestration-start-here` is the only repository that has read/write awareness of the entire system. It holds the canonical registry of all repositories, the governance rules that constrain how work flows between organs, and the automation workflows that enforce those constraints. When a developer opens a pull request in any organ, the `validate-dependencies` workflow fetches the registry from this hub to check for violations. When the monthly audit runs, it reads the registry and governance rules from this hub to produce a system-wide health report. The hub is the source of truth; individual organs are the locus of work. [L45] [L46] ``` [L47] ┌──────────────────────────────┐ [L48] │ orchestration-start-here │ [L49] │ │ [L50] │ registry.json │ [L51] │ governance-rules.json │ [L52] │ 5 workflows · 3 scripts │ [L53] └──────────┬───────────────────┘ [L54] │ [L55] ┌────────────────────┼────────────────────┐ [L56] │ │ │ [L57] ┌────────▼──────┐ ┌─────────▼────────┐ ┌────────▼──────┐ [L58] │ ORGAN-I │ │ ORGAN-II │ │ ORGAN-III │ [L59] │ Theory │──▶ Art │──▶ Commerce │ [L60] │ 18 repos │ │ 22 repos │ │ 21 repos │ [L61] └───────────────┘ └──────────────────┘ └───────────────┘ [L62] │ │ │ [L63] ┌────────▼──────┐ ┌─────────▼────────┐ ┌────────▼──────┐ [L64] │ ORGAN-V │ │ ORGAN-VI │ │ ORGAN-VII │ [L65] │ Process │ │ Community │ │ Marketing │ [L66] │ 2 repos │ │ 3 repos │ │ 4 repos │ [L67] └───────────────┘ └──────────────────┘ └───────────────┘ [L68] │ [L69] ┌──────────▼───────────────────┐ [L70] │ meta-organvm │ [L71] │ (Umbrella Org) │ [L72] └──────────────────────────────┘ [L73] ``` [L74] [L75] --- [L76] [L77] ## The Eight-Organ Model [L78] [L79] | # | Organ | Domain | GitHub Organization | Repos | Flagship | [L80] |---|-------|--------|-------------------|-------|----------| [L81] | I | Theoria | Theory — epistemology, recursion, ontology | [organvm-i-theoria](https://github.com/organvm-i-theoria) | 18 | [recursive-engine--generative-entity](https://github.com/organvm-i-theoria/recursive-engine--generative-entity) | [L82] | II | Poiesis | Art — generative, performance, experiential | [organvm-ii-poiesis](https://github.com/organvm-ii-poiesis) | 22 | [metasystem-master](https://github.com/organvm-ii-poiesis/metasystem-master) | [L83] | III | Ergon | Commerce — SaaS, B2B, B2C products | [organvm-iii-ergon](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon) | 21 | [public-record-data-scrapper](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/public-record-data-scrapper) | [L84] | IV | Taxis | Orchestration — governance, routing, validation | [organvm-iv-taxis](https://github.com/organvm-iv-taxis) | 9 | **this repository** | [L85] | V | Logos | Public Process — essays, building in public | [organvm-v-logos](https://github.com/organvm-v-logos) | 2 | [public-process](https://github.com/organvm-v-logos/public-process) | [L86] | VI | Koinonia | Community — salons, reading groups | [organvm-vi-koinonia](https://github.com/organvm-vi-koinonia) | 3 | org profile | [L87] | VII | Kerygma | Marketing — POSSE distribution, announcements | [organvm-vii-kerygma](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma) | 4 | org profile | [L88] | VIII | Meta | Umbrella — cross-organ coordination | [meta-organvm](https://github.com/organvm) | 1 | org profile | [L89] [L90] The Greek suffix naming convention (Theoria, Poiesis, Ergon, Taxis, Logos, Koinonia, Kerygma) is drawn from Aristotelian categories of knowledge and action. Each name maps to the organ's function: *theoria* for contemplative knowledge, *poiesis* for creative making, *ergon* for productive work, *taxis* for ordering and arrangement. [L91] [L92] --- [L93] [L94] ## Registry Documentation [L95] [L96] The `registry.json` file is the single source of truth for the entire system. Every repository's status, dependencies, documentation state, promotion status, and portfolio relevance is tracked here. If the registry and reality disagree, either the registry must be updated or reality must be fixed — this is Article I of the system constitution. [L97] [L98] ### Schema [L99] [L100] Each repository entry contains the following fields: [L101] [L102] | Field | Type | Description | [L103] |-------|------|-------------| [L104] | `name` | string | Repository name (without org prefix) | [L105] | `org` | string | GitHub organization (e.g., `organvm-i-theoria`) | [L106] | `status` | enum | `ACTIVE`, `ARCHIVED`, `PLANNED` | [L107] | `public` | boolean | Whether the repository is publicly visible | [L108] | `description` | string | Human-readable description (synced to GitHub) | [L109] | `documentation_status` | enum | `DEPLOYED`, `FLAGSHIP README DEPLOYED`, `INFRASTRUCTURE`, `EMPTY`, `SKELETON` | [L110] | `portfolio_relevance` | enum | `CRITICAL`, `HIGH`, `MEDIUM`, `LOW`, `INTERNAL` | [L111] | `dependencies` | array | List of `org/repo` strings this repo depends on | [L112] | `promotion_status` | enum | `LOCAL`, `CANDIDATE`, `PUBLIC_PROCESS`, `GRADUATED`, `ARCHIVED` | [L113] | `tier` | enum | `flagship`, `standard`, `stub`, `archive`, `infrastructure` | [L114] | `last_validated` | ISO 8601 | Date of last automated validation pass | [L115] [L116] ORGAN-III repositories carry two additional fields: [L117] [L118] | Field | Type | Description | [L119] |-------|------|-------------| [L120] | `type` | enum | `SaaS`, `B2B`, `B2C`, `internal` | [L121] | `revenue` | string | Revenue model description | [L122] [L123] ### Top-Level Structure [L124] [L125] ```json [L126] { [L127] "version": "2.0", [L128] "schema_version": "0.2", [L129] "deployment_model": "PARALLEL_ALL_ORGANS", [L130] "summary": { [L131] "total_repos": 79, [L132] "operational_organs": 8, [L133] "portfolio_strength": "SILVER (ALL ORGANS)" [L134] }, [L135] "organs": { [L136] "ORGAN-I": { [L137] "name": "Theory", [L138] "launch_status": "OPERATIONAL", [L139] "repositories": [ ... ] [L140] } [L141] } [L142] } [L143] ``` [L144] [L145] ### Querying the Registry [L146] [L147] The registry is plain JSON — queryable with `jq`, Python, or any JSON-aware tool. [L148] [L149] **Count repos per organ:** [L150] [L151] ```bash [L152] jq '.organs | to_entries[] | {organ: .key, count: (.value.repositories | length)}' registry.json [L153] ``` [L154] [L155] **Find all flagship repos:** [L156] [L157] ```bash [L158] jq '[.organs[].repositories[] | select(.tier == "flagship")] | .[].name' registry.json [L159] ``` [L160] [L161] **List all dependencies for a specific repo:** [L162] [L163] ```bash [L164] jq '.organs[].repositories[] | select(.name == "metasystem-master") | .dependencies' registry.json [L165] ``` [L166] [L167] **Find repos with CRITICAL portfolio relevance:** [L168] [L169] ```bash [L170] jq '[.organs[].repositories[] | select(.portfolio_relevance == "CRITICAL")] | .[] | "\(.org)/\(.name)"' registry.json [L171] ``` [L172] [L173] --- [L174] [L175] ## Governance Model [L176] [L177] The governance model is codified in `governance-rules.json` — a machine-readable constitution derived from six articles and four amendments. Governance is not advisory; it is enforced by automated workflows that block violations before they reach the main branch. [L178] [L179] ### The Six Constitutional Articles [L180] [L181] | Article | Title | Enforcement | [L182] |---------|-------|-------------| [L183] | I | Registry as Single Source of Truth | Automated — monthly audit reconciles | [L184] | II | Unidirectional Dependencies | Automated — PR validation blocks back-edges | [L185] | III | All Eight Organs Visible at Launch | Audit — monthly check confirms presence | [L186] | IV | Documentation Precedes Deployment | Manual — phase gates enforced by review | [L187] | V | Portfolio-Quality Documentation | Manual — Stranger Test scoring (>=90/100) | [L188] | VI | Promotion State Machine | Automated — state transitions validated | [L189] [L190] ### The Four Amendments [L191] [L192] | Amendment | Title | Context | [L193] |-----------|-------|---------| [L194] | A | Bronze Tier Launch Path | 5 perfect repos beat 44 mediocre repos | [L195] | B | Coordination Overhead Budget | 10% of phase TE budgeted for reconciliation | [L196] | C | Registry Schema Completeness | dependencies[], promotion_status, tier, last_validated required | [L197] | D | AI Non-Determinism Acknowledgment | All AI-generated deliverables require human review | [L198] [L199] ### Promotion State Machine [L200] [L201] Repositories progress through five states. No state may be skipped. The `promote-repo` workflow validates transitions against the governance rules before execution. [L202] [L203] ```mermaid [L204] stateDiagram-v2 [L205] direction LR [L206] LOCAL --> CANDIDATE : Ready for review [L207] CANDIDATE --> PUBLIC_PROCESS : Documented and meets quality gates [L208] PUBLIC_PROCESS --> GRADUATED : Publicly discussed in ORGAN-V essays [L209] GRADUATED --> ARCHIVED : Feature complete and stable [L210] ARCHIVED : End of active development [L211] ``` [L212] [L213] **Transition requirements:** [L214] [L215] - **LOCAL to CANDIDATE**: Repository exists, has at least a skeleton README, is tracked in registry [L216] - **CANDIDATE to PUBLIC_PROCESS**: Documentation status is DEPLOYED, README meets 2,000+ word minimum, no dependency violations, portfolio relevance assessed [L217] - **PUBLIC_PROCESS to GRADUATED**: Featured in at least one ORGAN-V essay, all dependencies validated, community health files present [L218] - **GRADUATED to ARCHIVED**: Explicit decision to freeze; documentation preserved, dependencies updated [L219] [L220] ### Cross-Organ Promotion Rules [L221] [L222] Beyond the state machine, two specific cross-organ promotion paths exist: [L223] [L224] **Theory to Art** (`promote-to-art`): An ORGAN-I theoretical framework has been validated enough to inspire artistic implementation in ORGAN-II. Requirements: promotion_status >= CANDIDATE, documentation DEPLOYED, comprehensive README (... (truncated)

Citation Marker: fileciteturn68file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: e4a675a38368d6252ee6bb2835a614ea27976060 [L3] content: [![ORGAN-VII: Kerygma](https://img.shields.io/badge/ORGAN--VII-Kerygma-6a1b9a?style=flat-square)](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma) [L4] [![Templates](https://img.shields.io/badge/templates-multi--platform-9c27b0?style=flat-square)]() [L5] [![POSSE](https://img.shields.io/badge/POSSE-enabled-blueviolet?style=flat-square)]() [L6] [![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/License-MIT-blue?style=flat-square)](LICENSE) [L7] [![Status](https://img.shields.io/badge/status-active-6a1b9a?style=flat-square)]() [L8] [L9] # Announcement Templates [L10] [L11] **A structured template library for all outbound communications across the eight-organ creative-institutional system -- platform-specific formats, tone calibration, variable interpolation, and quality checklists for every announcement type.** [L12] [L13] Announcement Templates is the canonical library of reusable communication artifacts for ORGAN-VII (Kerygma), the marketing and distribution organ of the [organvm](https://github.com/organvm-vii-kerygma) ecosystem. Every public-facing message that leaves this system -- whether a Mastodon thread announcing a new essay, a Discord embed promoting a release, or a press release introducing the project to grant reviewers -- originates from or is validated against templates maintained here. [L14] [L15] The problem this repository solves is consistency at scale. An eight-organ system spanning 67+ repositories across 8 GitHub organizations produces a continuous stream of announcements: new releases, essay publications, community events, partnership opportunities, grant application supplements, conference submissions, and system-wide milestones. Without a centralized template library, each announcement becomes an ad-hoc composition -- inconsistent in voice, variable in quality, and disconnected from the strategic positioning that makes this system legible to its target audiences. [L16] [L17] This repository provides the structured alternative: a template for every announcement type, calibrated for every distribution channel, with built-in quality gates and variable interpolation from the system's machine-readable registry. [L18] [L19] --- [L20] [L21] ## Table of Contents [L22] [L23] - [Overview](#overview) [L24] - [Target Audiences](#target-audiences) [L25] - [Template Categories](#template-categories) [L26] - [Platform-Specific Formats](#platform-specific-formats) [L27] - [Tone and Voice Guides](#tone-and-voice-guides) [L28] - [Variable Interpolation](#variable-interpolation) [L29] - [Quality Checklists](#quality-checklists) [L30] - [Publishing Calendar Integration](#publishing-calendar-integration) [L31] - [Template Inventory](#template-inventory) [L32] - [Usage Workflow](#usage-workflow) [L33] - [Metrics and Tracking](#metrics-and-tracking) [L34] - [Cross-Organ Dependencies](#cross-organ-dependencies) [L35] - [Contributing](#contributing) [L36] - [License](#license) [L37] - [Author and Contact](#author-and-contact) [L38] [L39] --- [L40] [L41] ## Overview [L42] [L43] Announcement Templates operates as a content factory floor. Raw inputs -- a new repository reaching DEPLOYED status, a meta-system essay clearing editorial review, a community salon date being confirmed -- enter the template pipeline and emerge as platform-ready communications. The templates themselves are not static boilerplate; they are structured documents with variable slots, conditional sections, tone modifiers, and post-composition checklists that ensure every outbound message meets quality standards. [L44] [L45] The library is organized along two axes: **announcement type** (what kind of event is being communicated) and **distribution channel** (where the message will appear). A single event -- say, the publication of a new ORGAN-V essay -- generates multiple template instantiations: a Mastodon thread (segmented into 500-character posts with strategic thread structure), a Discord embed (rich formatting with metadata fields), a potential LinkedIn article (long-form professional framing), and an email newsletter segment (personal tone with context-setting). Each format serves a different audience and respects different platform conventions, but all derive from the same source event and maintain consistent factual content. [L46] [L47] ### Design Principles [L48] [L49] 1. **Single Source, Multiple Outputs.** Every announcement begins as a canonical event record. Templates transform that record into channel-specific formats. This prevents factual drift between platforms and ensures that a correction in one place propagates everywhere. [L50] [L51] 2. **Voice Consistency With Channel Adaptation.** The organvm system has a distinctive voice -- intellectually serious without being academic, technically precise without being exclusionary, strategically aware without being corporate. Templates encode this voice while adapting register for each platform: more conversational on Mastodon, more structured on Discord, more formal in press materials. [L52] [L53] 3. **Machine-Readable Inputs.** Templates consume data from `registry-v2.json` and the orchestration hub's governance files. Repository names, descriptions, organ assignments, documentation status, and dependency relationships are injected automatically, reducing manual transcription errors and keeping announcements synchronized with the actual state of the system. [L54] [L55] 4. **Quality Gates Before Publication.** Every template includes a pre-publication checklist. No announcement ships without confirming: factual accuracy (cross-checked against registry), link validity (all URLs tested), tone compliance (reviewed against voice guide), platform format compliance (character limits, embed constraints), and strategic alignment (does this announcement serve the current phase's goals?). [L56] [L57] --- [L58] [L59] ## Target Audiences [L60] [L61] Understanding who receives these announcements determines every template design decision. The organvm system targets five distinct audience segments, each with different information needs, attention patterns, and credibility markers. [L62] [L63] ### Academic Researchers and Theorists [L64] [L65] Researchers in digital humanities, computational creativity, media studies, and systems theory represent the intellectual core audience. They evaluate work based on conceptual rigor, citation of relevant literature, methodological transparency, and contribution to ongoing scholarly conversations. Announcements targeting this segment emphasize the theoretical frameworks underlying the system (recursive ontology, rhizomatic coordination, stigmergic communication models), reference relevant thinkers and traditions, and position deliverables within research contexts. Tone: precise, substantive, respectful of disciplinary conventions. [L66] [L67] ### Grant Reviewers and Funding Bodies [L68] [L69] Program officers at foundations (Knight, Mellon, NEA, NSF) and fellowship committees assess organizational capacity, sustainability planning, community impact, and alignment with funding priorities. They read quickly, evaluate against rubrics, and look for evidence of professional infrastructure. Announcements targeting this segment lead with outcomes and impact, demonstrate institutional maturity (governance structures, quality processes, documentation standards), and connect individual deliverables to broader systemic goals. Tone: confident, evidence-oriented, outcome-focused. [L70] [L71] ### Hiring Managers and Technical Recruiters [L72] [L73] Engineering managers, CTO offices, and technical recruiters evaluating portfolio evidence look for production-quality code, architectural reasoning, testing discipline, and systems thinking at scale. Announcements targeting this segment emphasize technical sophistication (test counts, coverage metrics, architecture decisions, scalability characteristics), demonstrate real engineering work (not toy projects), and position the eight-organ system as evidence of extraordinary organizational capacity. Tone: technically specific, results-oriented, pragmatic. [L74] [L75] ### Fellow Artists and Creative Technologists [L76] [L77] Practitioners working at the intersection of art and technology -- generative artists, creative coders, interactive designers, performance technologists -- evaluate work based on aesthetic ambition, technical craft, conceptual depth, and community contribution. Announcements targeting this segment foreground the creative vision, share process insights (building in public), and invite participation and collaboration. Tone: collegial, exploratory, generous with knowledge. [L78] [L79] ### Open Source Community [L80] [L81] Developers, contributors, and users of open-source tools evaluate projects based on code quality, documentation completeness, maintainer responsiveness, and community health. Announcements targeting this segment follow open-source communication conventions (changelogs, release notes, migration guides), emphasize contribution pathways, and demonstrate commitment to sustainable maintenance. Tone: direct, inclusive, technically clear. [L82] [L83] --- [L84] [L85] ## Template Categories [L86] [L87] ### Launch Announcements [L88] [L89] Used when a repository, organ, or system-wide milestone reaches a deployment threshold. Launch templates are the highest-stakes communications in the library -- they establish first impressions and set expectations for everything that follows. [L90] [L91] **Included templates:** [L92] - **Repository Launch** -- announces a single repo reaching DEPLOYED status. Includes: repo name, organ assignment, description, key technical highlights, link to README, link to org profile. Conditional sections for flagship vs. standard tier. [L93] - **Organ Launch** -- announces an entire organ reaching operational status. Includes: organ name and domain, repository count, flagship highlights, portfolio angle, governance status. Used during system-wide launch coordination. [L94] - **System Milestone** -- announces cross-organ achievements (all 8 organs operational, validation complete, 100K words deployed). Includes: quantitative metrics, timeline narrative, strategic framing for portfolio audiences. [L95] [L96] ### Release Notes [L97] [L98] Used when existing repositories ship significant updates, new features, or breaking changes. Release note templates follow semantic versioning conventions and include structured sections for additions, changes, deprecations, and migration instructions. [L99] [L100] **Included templates:** [L101] - **Feature Release** -- new capability added to an existing repo. Structured: summary, motivation, technical approach, usage examples, testing evidence, upgrade path. [L102] - **Bug Fix Release** -- targeted fix for a reported issue. Structured: issue reference, root cause, fix description, affected versions, verification steps. [L103] - **Breaking Change Release** -- updates that require consumer action. Structured: what changed, why, migration instructions (step-by-step), deprecation timeline, support resources. [L104] [L105] ### Essay Promotion [L106] [L107] Used when ORGAN-V (Public Process) publishes new essays on the Jekyll/GitHub Pages site. Essay promotion templates are critical to the POSSE workflow -- they drive traffic from social platforms back to the canonical publication site. [L108] [L109] **Included templates:** [L110] - **Essay Announcement** -- standard promotion for a new essay. Includes: title, thesis statement (one sentence), key arguments (bullet points), word count, reading time estimate, canonical URL, author attribution. [L111] - **Essay Series Update** -- announces a new entry in an ongoing series. Includes: series context, previous entries (with links), how this entry advances the argument, canonical URL. [L112] - **Essay Highlight** -- re-promotes an existing essay for a new context (relevant conference, related news event, grant deadline alignment). Includes: original publication date, relevance hook, canonical URL. [L113] [L114] ### Community Event Invitations [L115] [L116] Used for ORGAN-VI (Koinonia) community events -- salons, reading groups, workshops, collaborative sessions. [L117] [L118] **Included templates:** [L119] - **Salon Announcement** -- structured invitation with: topic, date/time (with timezone), format (synchronous/asynchronous), preparation materials, participation guidelines, RSVP mechanism. [L120] - **Reading Group Session** -- announces a reading group meeting with: selected text, discussion questions, facilitator, logistics. [L121] - **Workshop/Sprint** -- announces a collaborative working session with: objective, prerequisites, expected outcomes, contribution recognition. [L122] [L123] ### Press and Institutional Communications [L124] [L125] Formal communications for external institutional audiences -- press releases, partnership announcements, grant supplements. [L126] [L127] **Included templates:** [L128] - **Press Release** -- standard press release format (headline, dateline, lead paragraph, body, boilerplate, contact). Calibrated for arts/technology media outlets. [L129] - **Partnership Announcement** -- announces collaborations with other organizations, institutions, or communities. [L130] - **Grant Supplement** -- supplementary material for grant applications that references the organvm system as evidence of institutional capacity. [L131] [L132] --- [L133] [L134] ## Platform-Specific Formats [L135] [L136] ### Mastodon (Primary Social Channel) [L137] [L138] Mastodon's 500-character limit and thread-based conversation model require careful segmentation. Templates for Mastodon follow these structural rules: [L139] [L140] - **Thread architecture:** Opening post establishes the hook (what and why). Subsequent posts deliver detail. Final post provides the canonical link and call to action. Thread length: 3-7 posts depending on announcement type. [L141] - **Character budgeting:** Each post reserves 30 characters for the thread indicator (e.g., "1/5") and 23 characters for a hashtag block. Effective content budget: ~447 characters per post. [L142] - **Hashtag strategy:** Maximum 3 hashtags per post, drawn from a controlled vocabulary: `#CreativeTech`, `#OpenSource`, `#GenerativeArt`, `#BuildingInPublic`, `#DigitalHumanities`, `#SystemsThinking`, organ-specific tags. [L143] - **Alt text requirements:** All images include descriptive alt text. Screenshots of code or architecture diagrams include text-equivalent summaries. [L144] - **Content warnings:** Used appropriately for long threads (CW: long thread, 5 posts) to respect timeline etiquette. [L145] [L146] ### Discord (Community Channel) [L147] [L148] Discord embeds provide rich formatting with structured metadata fields. Templates for Discord use the embed specification: [L149] [L150] - **Embed structure:** Title (announcement headline), description (2-3 sentence summary), color (organ-specific hex -- ORGAN-VII uses `#6a1b9a`), fields (key-value metadata pairs), footer (timestamp and organ attribution), thumbnail (organ badge or repo logo). [L151] - **Field conventions:** Fields include: Organ, Repository, Status, Links. Additional fields vary by announcement type. [L152] - **Channel targeting:** Templates specify which Discord channel receives each announcement type (general, announcements, releases, community-events). [L153] - **Mention policy:** Templates indicate when `@everyone` or role mentions are appropriate (system milestones only) vs. when they should be avoided (routine updates). [L154] [L155] ### LinkedIn (Professional Visibility) [L156] [L157] LinkedIn articles and posts serve the hiring manager and grant reviewer audiences. Templates for LinkedIn adopt professional register: [L158] [L159] - **Post format:** 1,300-character posts for standard announcements. Long-form articles (1,500-2,500 words) for system milestones and essay re-publications. [L160] - **Professional framing:** Every announcement connects to professional competencies: systems architecture, project management, technical writing, open-source stewardship. [L161] - **Media attachments:** Architecture diagrams, metric dashboards, and progress visualizations enhance professional credibility. [L162] [L163] ### Email Newsletter [L164] [L165] Newsletter templates serve subscribers who prefer consolidated updates over real-time social feeds: [L166] [L167] - **Digest format:** Weekly or biweekly compilation of announcements, organized by organ. [L168] - **Personal tone:** Newsletter voice is slightly more informal and reflective than social posts -- it speaks directly to the reader, acknowledges the journey, and provides context that social posts cannot. [L169] - **Sections:** Featured announcement, organ roundup, upcoming events, reading recommendations, metrics snapshot. [L170] [L171] --- [L172] [L173] ## Tone and Voice Guides [L174] [L175] ### System-Wide Voice Principles [L176] [L177] The organvm system speaks with a voice that is **intellectually serious without being academic**, **technically precise without being exclusionary**, and **strategically aware without being corporate**. Every template encodes these three tensions. [L178] [L179] - **Intellectual seriousness** means: claims are substantiated, frameworks are named and attributed, complexity is respected rather than flattened. We do not simplify for simplification's sake. [L180] - **Technical precision** means: numbers are specific (not "many tests" but "1,312 tests passing"), architectures are described accurately, limitations are acknowledged honestly. [L181] - **Strategic awareness** means: every announcement serves a purpose beyond information transmission. We are building a portfolio, establishing credibility, and inviting participation. Templates encode this intentionality. [L182] [L183] ### Organ-Specific Voice Modifiers [L184] [L185] Each organ has a tonal register that reflects its domain: [L186] [L187] | Organ | Voice Modifier | Example Register | [L188] |-------|---------------|------------------| [L189] | I (Theory) | Contemplative, precise | "The recursive engine demonstrates that..." | [L190] | II (Art) | Evocative, process-oriented | "This work explores the space between..." | [L191] | III (Commerce) | Pragmatic, results-oriented | "The system processes 10K records/second..." | [L192] | IV (Orchestration) | Architectural, systematic | "Governance rules ensure that..." | [L193] | V (Public Process) | Reflective, narrative | "Building this in public means..." | [L194] | VI (Community) | Warm, invitational | "Join us for a conversation about..." | [L195] | VII (Marketing) | Strategic, audience-aware | "This positions the system as..." | [L196] | VIII (Meta) | Integrative, panoramic | "Across all eight organs..." | [L197] [L198] ### Anti-Patterns [L199] [L200] Templates include explicit anti-pattern warnings to prevent voice degradation: [L201] [L202] - **No hype language.** Never use "revolutionary", "game-changing", "disrupting". The work speaks for itself. [L203] - **No false modesty.** Do not undersell genuine achievements... (truncated)

Citation Marker: fileciteturn70file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: 60e134e2765d9a873aa9b5d6498e51484294ec75 [L3] content: [![ORGAN-III: Ergon](https://img.shields.io/badge/ORGAN--III-Ergon-1b5e20?style=flat-square)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon) [L4] [![TypeScript](https://img.shields.io/badge/TypeScript-5.7-3178C6?style=flat-square&logo=typescript&logoColor=white)](https://www.typescriptlang.org/) [L5] [![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/License-MIT-yellow?style=flat-square)](./LICENSE) [L6] [![React 19](https://img.shields.io/badge/React-19-61DAFB?style=flat-square&logo=react&logoColor=black)](https://react.dev/) [L7] [![Vite 7](https://img.shields.io/badge/Vite-7-646CFF?style=flat-square&logo=vite&logoColor=white)](https://vite.dev/) [L8] [![CI](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/mirror-mirror/actions/workflows/ci.yml/badge.svg)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/mirror-mirror/actions/workflows/ci.yml) [L9] [![Status](https://img.shields.io/badge/status-active-brightgreen)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/mirror-mirror) [L10] [![Coverage](https://img.shields.io/badge/coverage-pending-lightgrey)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/mirror-mirror) [L11] [L12] # Mirror-Mirror [L13] [L14] > **The Consultation Gap is bleeding salon and beauty enterprise revenue.** [L15] > Every time a client hesitates because they "can't picture it," every time a chemical service is refunded because it didn't match the vision, and every time a high-end salon loses a booking to indecision—that is systemic revenue leakage. Mirror-Mirror eliminates the gap. It is a production-grade, medical-fidelity "phygital" diagnostic and try-on engine that turns aesthetic uncertainty into guaranteed conversion. Built on React 19, TypeScript, and Vite, it bridges hyper-realistic beauty simulation with professional cosmetology operations. [L16] > [L17] > [**Deploy this for your shop →**](mailto:anthony@organvm.com) [L18] > *(If you are a technical recruiter or engineering leader, this repository is the proof-of-work for my architectural weight. [Work with the team that built this →](mailto:anthony@organvm.com))* [L19] [L20] --- [L21] [L22] ## What It Is [L23] [L24] Mirror-Mirror is a React 19 single-page application that operates as a comprehensive virtual beauty platform. The core proposition is straightforward: before you sit in a salon chair for two hours and spend significant money, you should be able to see — with medical-grade precision — exactly what a copper balayage, wolf cut, or platinum bob will look like on your actual face, under realistic lighting, with honest diagnostics about your skin condition and face geometry. [L25] [L26] Mirror-Mirror is a B2C product within [ORGAN-III: Ergon](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon), the commerce organ of the [ORGAN system](https://github.com/meta-organvm). It demonstrates how consumer-facing software can deliver genuinely useful utility — not novelty filters, but a truth engine that helps people make informed decisions about their appearance before committing time and money to a salon visit. [L27] [L28] ### The Truth Engine Philosophy [L29] [L30] The **Truth Level** slider is the philosophical and technical core of Mirror-Mirror. It controls the realism of the visualization: [L31] [L32] | Truth Range | Mode | Use Case | [L33] |---|---|---| [L34] | 0–30 | Flattering | Inspiration, mood boards | [L35] | 30–70 | Balanced | Default — accurate color, slight lighting optimization | [L36] | 70–100 | Unflinching | Professional diagnostic — accurate skin texture, visible imperfections | [L37] [L38] This is not a filter intensity slider. It determines which rendering pipeline is used, affecting skin texture mapping, color accuracy algorithms, and lighting simulation. The design philosophy: users should always have the power to choose how honestly the mirror reflects them. [L39] [L40] --- [L41] [L42] ## Who Pays [L43] [L44] Mirror-Mirror serves two distinct user bases with different payment models: [L45] [L46] ### Clients (B2C) [L47] [L48] **Primary revenue source.** Individual consumers pay for: [L49] - **Mirror+ subscriptions** ($9.99/month via Stripe) — unlocks premium features [L50] - **Salon booking commissions** — appointments booked through the platform [L51] - **Badge marketplace** — badge-based rewards and premium content [L52] [L53] ### Stylists & Salons (B2B) [L54] [L55] **Growth revenue.** Professionals and salon owners pay for: [L56] - **Studio tier** — team management, client consultation workflows, advanced analytics [L57] - **API access** — enterprise licensing of the Truth Engine for salons, beauty brands, and cosmetology professionals [L58] - **ShareFile integration** — Studio-tier stylist dashboard with OAuth 2.0 authentication [L59] [L60] ### Beauty Brands (B2B) [L61] [L62] **Strategic revenue.** Brands pay for: [L63] - **Sponsored designer looks** — exclusive branded AR filters and style packs (Valentino, Balenciaga, Tom Ford, Dior, Gucci, Versace) [L64] - **API licensing** — integrate medical-grade diagnostics into their own apps [L65] - **Trend data** — anonymized analytics on style preferences and engagement [L66] [L67] --- [L68] [L69] ## Pricing & Monetization [L70] [L71] Mirror-Mirror uses a three-tier subscription model with additional revenue streams: [L72] [L73] ### Subscription Tiers [L74] [L75] | Tier | Price | Audience | Key Features | [L76] |---|---|---|---| [L77] | **Free** | $0 | All users | Virtual try-on, skin analysis (basic), trend carousel, avatar creation, haircut/makeup studio, before/after comparisons, social challenges, badge earning | [L78] | **Pro** (Mirror+) | $9.99/mo | Enthusiasts | Everything in Free + AR beauty filters (6 types with intensity sliders), face landmark tracking (68-point mesh), team chat (voice-to-text), AI outfit recommendations, weekly digest emails, extended analytics, priority booking, exclusive designer looks | [L79] | **Studio** | Custom | Salons / Stylists | Everything in Pro + stylist dashboard, client consultation management, transformation proposal workflow, team coordination, advanced analytics, ShareFile integration, API access | [L80] [L81] ### Additional Revenue [L82] [L83] | Source | Description | [L84] |---|---| [L85] | **Badge Marketplace** | Earned achievement badges traded for Mirror+ days, salon credits ($25–$50), premium AR filter packs, virtual styling sessions, product bundles. Badge scarcity drives engagement. | [L86] | **Challenge Prizes** | Brand-sponsored challenge prize pools (gift cards, Mirror+ subscriptions, salon credits, cash). Brands pay for visibility; winners get rewards. | [L87] | **Sponsored Designer Looks** | Exclusive AR filters and style packs from luxury brands. Brand licensing revenue. | [L88] | **API Licensing** | Enterprise API access to the Truth Engine for third-party applications. Contact `api-support@organvm.com`. | [L89] | **Salon Booking Commission** | Per-appointment commission on salon bookings made through the platform. | [L90] [L91] ### Feature Gating [L92] [L93] Features are gated by tier. The application uses a `SubscriptionTier` type (`free` / `pro` / `studio`) with a rank-based permission system. Gated features include AR filters, face landmark tracking, and team chat — locked behind upsell banners with clear messaging about the required tier. [L94] [L95] ### Payments Infrastructure [L96] [L97] Subscriptions are processed through **Stripe Checkout** with serverless API functions: [L98] - `POST /api/checkout/session` — creates a Stripe Checkout session [L99] - `POST /api/webhooks/stripe` — handles `customer.subscription.updated` events [L100] - Stripe webhooks write subscription state to GitHub Spark KV (`mirror-plus-subscribed`, `mirror-plus-renewal-date`, `mirror-plus-join-date`) [L101] - Server-side HMAC-authenticated API keys guard checkout and webhook endpoints [L102] - Badge-based rewards can auto-renew subscriptions without additional payment [L103] [L104] ### Monetization Design Principles [L105] [L106] 1. **Free tier is genuinely useful** — try-on, analysis, and social features are free to maximize adoption and data network effects [L107] 2. **Pro features solve real pain** — AR filters, face tracking, and team chat are professional tools, not arbitrary locks [L108] 3. **Studio targets willingness-to-pay** — salon professionals derive direct revenue from the platform [L109] 4. **Badges create a secondary economy** — achievement-based rewards reduce churn and increase engagement without subscription fatigue [L110] 5. **Brand licensing is the high-margin layer** — sponsored looks and API access generate revenue without per-user friction [L111] [L112] --- [L113] [L114] ## Installation [L115] [L116] ### Prerequisites [L117] [L118] - **Node.js** 18+ (v25 recommended) [L119] - **npm** 9+ (ships with Node.js) [L120] - A GitHub account with access to the [GitHub Spark](https://github.com/features/spark) platform (for `@github/spark` hooks and plugins) [L121] [L122] ### Local Development [L123] [L124] ```bash [L125] git clone https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/mirror-mirror.git [L126] cd mirror-mirror [L127] npm install [L128] npm run dev [L129] ``` [L130] [L131] ### Available Scripts [L132] [L133] | Script | Command | Description | [L134] |---|---|---| [L135] | `dev` | `vite` | Start the Vite development server with HMR | [L136] | `build` | `tsc -b --noCheck && vite build` | Type-check and produce optimized production build | [L137] | `lint` | `eslint .` | Run ESLint across all source files | [L138] | `preview` | `vite preview` | Serve the production build locally | [L139] | `test` | `vitest run` | Run the test suite | [L140] | `optimize` | `vite optimize` | Pre-bundle dependencies for faster cold starts | [L141] | `deploy` | `bash scripts/deploy.sh` | One-command deploy (Docker by default; supports `vercel`/`netlify`) | [L142] | `docker:build` | `docker build -t mirror-mirror:latest .` | Build production container | [L143] | `docker:run` | `docker run --rm -p 8080:80 mirror-mirror:latest` | Run at http://localhost:8080 | [L144] [L145] ### API Authentication [L146] [L147] Serverless user API keys are issued by `POST /api/auth/keys` and verified by `GET /api/auth/verify`. Both endpoints use server-only HMAC configuration. [L148] [L149] Required server secrets: [L150] [L151] ```bash [L152] MIRROR_AUTH_SECRET="32+ character signing secret" [L153] MIRROR_AUTH_ISSUER_SECRET="32+ character issuer secret" [L154] ``` [L155] [L156] For local development, put these in `.env.local`. For production Vercel functions, configure in Project Settings → Environment Variables. Do not use `VITE_` prefixes; they must never be bundled into browser code. [L157] [L158] ### Deployment [L159] [L160] ```bash [L161] npm run deploy # Docker image at http://localhost:8080 [L162] npm run deploy -- vercel # or: netlify | docker [L163] ``` [L164] [L165] Mirror-Mirror ships with Docker, Vercel, and Netlify targets. Use Vercel when the checkout/license API routes must be live; Docker and Netlify serve the static SPA shell. See **[docs/DEPLOYMENT.md](docs/DEPLOYMENT.md)** for the full guide. [L166] [L167] --- [L168] [L169] ## Usage [L170] [L171] ### Client Mode (default) [L172] [L173] The primary user experience — 40 feature tabs organized in a horizontal scrollable tab strip using Radix UI's accessible `Tabs` primitive with Framer Motion animations. [L174] [L175] **Key workflows:** [L176] [L177] | Flow | Path | Purpose | [L178] |---|---|---| [L179] | **Discovery** | Trends → AI Styling → Try-On → Haircut/Makeup → Book | Inspiration to appointment in one flow | [L180] | **Diagnostic** | Analyze (Skin) → Landmarks → Coaching → History | Medical-grade skin analysis + tracking | [L181] | **Social** | Challenges → Teams → Team Chat → Voting → Leaderboard | Competitions and community engagement | [L182] | **Collection** | Badge Hub → Trading → Collections → Goals → Marketplace | Gamified badge economy | [L183] [L184] ### Stylist Mode [L185] [L186] Toggled via the header mode switch. The stylist dashboard replaces the tab navigation with a professional management interface for: [L187] - Client consultation management [L188] - Transformation proposal review [L189] - Team coordination (voice-enabled chat) [L190] - Appointment schedule management [L191] - Client transformation history tracking [L192] [L193] ### Feature Map [L194] [L195] | Cluster | Tabs | Purpose | [L196] |---|---|---| [L197] | **Core Try-On** | Try-On, Haircut, Makeup, Wardrobe, Avatar | Virtual visualization with photo and live video modes | [L198] | **Analysis** | Analyze (Skin), Landmarks, History, Trends | Medical-grade skin diagnostics, facial geometry mapping | [L199] | **AI Intelligence** | AI Outfits, AI Styling, Predictions, Coaching | ML-powered recommendations, trend predictions, style coaching | [L200] | **Social** | Challenges, Teams, Team Chat, Voting, Compare | Collaborative competitions, voice-enabled chat, democratic voting | [L201] | **Badge Economy** | Badge Hub, Trading, Collections, Goals, Simulator, Trade Analytics, Marketplace, Rarity Marketplace, Team Sets | Gamified badge economy with P2P trading and rarity tiers | [L202] | **Scheduling** | Book, Group, Reminders, Calendar, Digest, Scheduler, Notifications | Appointment booking, group coordination, calendar sync | [L203] | **Business** | Mirror+, Proposals, AR Filters, Prizes, Analytics, Voting Analytics, Testing | Subscription tier, proposal workflows, prize distribution | [L204] [L205] --- [L206] [L207] ## Citation Marker: fileciteturn75file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: 1ccd6ff79a62f405e480d8eb3ad0ccd1a6223973 [L3] content: # Records Watch [L4] [L5] Face: public [L6] [L7] [![CI](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/the-actual-news/actions/workflows/ci.yml/badge.svg)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/the-actual-news/actions/workflows/ci.yml) [L8] [![Coverage](https://img.shields.io/badge/coverage-pending-lightgrey)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/the-actual-news) [L9] [![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/License-MIT-blue.svg)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/the-actual-news/blob/main/LICENSE) [L10] [![Organ III](https://img.shields.io/badge/Organ-III%20Ergon-F59E0B)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon) [L11] [![Status](https://img.shields.io/badge/status-active-brightgreen)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/the-actual-news) [L12] [![TypeScript](https://img.shields.io/badge/lang-TypeScript-informational)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/the-actual-news) [L13] [L14] **Verifiable civic record platform — news as a public service.** [L15] [L16] Records Watch is a platform that rebuilds journalism as a public utility by making truthfulness and accountability the product and making attention economically irrelevant. Every published story ships with a machine-readable verification spine: atomic claims, content-addressed evidence graphs, and append-only correction history. Publication is gated by deterministic quality policies — not engagement metrics, not editorial discretion, not advertising revenue targets. If a story does not meet the evidence threshold defined in a versioned policy pack, it does not publish. Period. [L17] [L18] This is not a news aggregator. It is not a fact-checking overlay. It is a full-stack platform that produces verifiable reporting artifacts — stories where every factual claim is traceable to primary evidence, where contradictions are structural objects rather than vibes, and where corrections are first-class immutable events with reason codes rather than quiet edits that vanish from the record. [L19] [L20] --- [L21] [L22] ## Table of Contents [L23] [L24] - [Why This Exists](#why-this-exists) [L25] - [The Three-Layer Model](#the-three-layer-model) [L26] - [Technical Architecture](#technical-architecture) [L27] - [Database Design](#database-design) [L28] - [The Publish Gate](#the-publish-gate) [L29] - [Core Protocol Spec](#core-protocol-spec) [L30] - [Conformance Test Suite](#conformance-test-suite) [L31] - [Project Structure](#project-structure) [L32] - [Installation and Quick Start](#installation-and-quick-start) [L33] - [Environment Configuration](#environment-configuration) [L34] - [API Reference](#api-reference) [L35] - [Roadmap](#roadmap) [L36] - [Cross-Organ Context](#cross-organ-context) [L37] - [Design Principles](#design-principles) [L38] - [Contributing](#contributing) [L39] - [License](#license) [L40] - [Author](#author) [L41] [L42] --- [L43] [L44] ## Why This Exists [L45] [L46] There was a time when news was the news. Before privatization and the attention economy, journalism operated as a public service without intentional bias — or at least, with structural incentives that did not actively reward bias. The advertising-funded, engagement-optimized model that replaced it has produced a media environment where outrage is more profitable than accuracy, where corrections are buried rather than celebrated, and where readers have no way to distinguish a well-sourced investigation from a thinly-sourced opinion piece dressed in the language of reporting. [L47] [L48] Records Watch starts from a different premise: **what if the platform itself made truthfulness the product?** Not as a layer on top of existing media (fact-checking is reactive and always too late), but as the native architecture of how stories are composed, verified, published, and corrected. [L49] [L50] The core insight is that bias disputes become tractable when they are about specific, inspectable objects. Instead of arguing about whether a publication is "biased," readers, verifiers, and publishers all operate on the same structured objects: claims with time bounds and confidence scores, evidence with provenance chains, and edges that link one to the other with typed relations and strength values. Disagreement moves from the realm of vibes into the realm of data. [L51] [L52] The economic model follows from the architecture: if the platform's value is the verification spine (not the narrative), then funding can be decoupled from attention. Membership, bounties, and quality-weighted compensation replace advertising. Revenue accrues from the reliability of the record, not from how many people clicked. [L53] [L54] --- [L55] [L56] ## The Three-Layer Model [L57] [L58] Every story on Records Watch ships with three publicly accessible layers: [L59] [L60] ### Layer A: Narrative [L61] [L62] The human-readable piece, written for comprehension. This is what readers see first — a well-crafted story that communicates what happened, why it matters, and what remains uncertain. The narrative is authored by journalists and stored as immutable story versions (Markdown snapshots). Once published, a version cannot be altered; changes produce a new version. [L63] [L64] ### Layer B: Claims Ledger [L65] [L66] A machine-readable set of atomic claims extracted from the narrative. Each claim is typed (`factual`, `statistical`, `attribution`, or `interpretation`), carries time bounds, jurisdiction scope, entity references, and two confidence scores — one from the extraction model (`confidence_model`) and one from human review (`confidence_review`). Claims have a support status that progresses through a lifecycle: `unsupported` at extraction, `partially_supported` as evidence accumulates, `supported` when sufficient primary evidence exists, or `contradicted` when evidence refutes the claim. [L67] [L68] ### Layer C: Evidence Graph [L69] [L70] A directed graph linking claims to primary evidence objects. Evidence is content-addressed using `sha256` hashes, ensuring tamper-evidence — if anyone modifies the underlying document, the hash breaks. Each evidence object carries full provenance metadata: source class (primary record, primary media, primary dataset, secondary, commentary, or unknown), publisher, URL, collection timestamp, license, and a provenance chain for derived artifacts. The edges connecting claims to evidence are typed (`supports`, `contradicts`, or `context`) and carry a strength value between 0 and 1. [L71] [L72] This three-layer model turns "is this article trustworthy?" into a question with a structured, inspectable answer. [L73] [L74] --- [L75] [L76] ## Technical Architecture [L77] [L78] The platform is a TypeScript monorepo managed with pnpm workspaces, organized into five backend microservices, a Next.js frontend application, and a shared contracts layer. [L79] [L80] ``` [L81] ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ [L82] │ User Surfaces │ [L83] │ ┌──────────┐ ┌──────────────┐ ┌─────────────────┐ │ [L84] │ │ Reader │ │ Verifier │ │ Publisher │ │ [L85] │ │ (Web) │ │ (Web) │ │ (Web) │ │ [L86] │ └────┬──────┘ └──────┬───────┘ └───────┬──────────┘ │ [L87] │ │ │ │ │ [L88] └───────┼──────────────────┼────────────────────┼───────────────┘ [L89] │ │ │ [L90] ▼ ▼ ▼ [L91] ┌──────────────┐ ┌──────────────┐ ┌──────────────┐ [L92] │ Gateway │ │ Verify │ │ Story │ [L93] │ BFF :8080 │ │ :8084 │ │ :8081 │ [L94] └──────┬───────┘ └──────┬───────┘ └──────┬───────┘ [L95] │ │ │ [L96] ├──────────────────┼───────────────────┤ [L97] │ │ │ [L98] ▼ ▼ ▼ [L99] ┌──────────────┐ ┌──────────────┐ ┌──────────────┐ [L100] │ Claim │ │ Evidence │ │ PostgreSQL │ [L101] │ :8082 │ │ :8083 │ │ :5432 │ [L102] └──────────────┘ └──────────────┘ └──────────────┘ [L103] ``` [L104] [L105] ### Services [L106] [L107] | Service | Port | Responsibility | [L108] |---------|------|----------------| [L109] | **Gateway** | 8080 | Public-facing BFF (Backend for Frontend). Serves the feed, individual story bundles, and the publish endpoint. Implements the full publish gate algorithm in a single transactional SQL query. | [L110] | **Story** | 8081 | Story CRUD operations — create, update, version management. Handles the draft/review/published state machine. | [L111] | **Claim** | 8082 | Claim extraction from narrative text. Interfaces with the model gateway for automated claim identification. | [L112] | **Evidence** | 8083 | Evidence object registration. Computes content hashes, validates provenance metadata, and stores content-addressed objects. | [L113] | **Verify** | 8084 | Verification task management. Generates review tasks for evidence gaps, collects structured verdicts from reviewers. | [L114] [L115] ### Frontend Application [L116] [L117] The `apps/public-web/` directory contains a Next.js application providing three user-facing surfaces: [L118] [L119] - **Reader** (`/`) — Public product homepage, story feed, audience capture, membership/sponsor calls to action, and a quality-ranked ledger rather than an engagement-ranked feed. [L120] - **Verifier** (`/verify`) — Internal claim review queues, evidence gap alerts, and structured review submission forms. This workspace is disabled by default in the public container. [L121] - **Story Detail** (`/story/[story_id]`) — Full story bundle display with claims, evidence edges, and corrections timeline. [L122] - **Distribution Kit** (`/distribution`) — Public share copy, atom share packets, offer packets, conversion steps, feeds, conversion routes, and boundary notes for launch distribution. [L123] - **Public Principles** (`/principles`) — Reader-facing editorial firewall, funding boundary, and public/internal separation. [L124] - **Launch Status** (`/launch`, `/launch.json`, `/share-kit.json`) — Public-safe readiness ledger and machine-readable sharing packet for audience, revenue, analytics, growth capabilities, share packets, offer packets, conversion steps, distribution routes, and deployment-boundary setup. [L125] [L126] ### Productized Public Surface [L127] [L128] Docker is not required for the public website. The required local launch path is: [L129] [L130] ```bash [L131] pnpm launch:local [L132] ``` [L133] [L134] After deployment, verify the live public origin with: [L135] [L136] ```bash [L137] PUBLIC_ENV_FILE=.env.public PUBLIC_WEB_BASE_URL=https://recordswatch.org pnpm launch:deployed [L138] ``` [L139] [L140] The public app can also be packaged separately from internal services when Docker is useful: [L141] [L142] ```bash [L143] make up-public [L144] ``` [L145] [L146] Reader-safe configuration lives in `.env.public.example` and uses `NEXT_PUBLIC_*` values only. Internal service configuration lives in `.env.internal.example` and should be supplied from a secret manager in real deployments. [L147] [L148] The public revenue and audience loops are configured by URL, not by browser-side credentials: [L149] [L150] - `NEXT_PUBLIC_NEWSLETTER_URL` [L151] - `NEXT_PUBLIC_MEMBERSHIP_URL` [L152] - `NEXT_PUBLIC_SPONSOR_URL` [L153] - `NEXT_PUBLIC_SITE_URL` [L154] - `NEXT_PUBLIC_ANALYTICS_DOMAIN` [L155] [L156] Public CTAs and atom-share links emit keyless, Plausible-compatible browser events when `NEXT_PUBLIC_ANALYTICS_DOMAIN` is configured. The default domain is the public site host; provider-side analytics setup remains external and must not put API keys in the public app. `NEXT_PUBLIC_ANALYTICS_SCRIPT_URL` must remain the CSP-allowed Plausible browser script URL. [L157] [L158] `/launch.json` exposes the same public-safe launch state for POSSE/distribution automation: ready counts, growth capability flags, public routes, feeds, atom share packets, public offer packets, conversion steps, conversion route status, public event names, and internal-boundary warnings. `/share-kit.json` exposes the narrower public sharing packet: launch copy, tracked public routes, atoms, social-card paths, share packets, public offers, conversion steps, and internal-boundary warnings. Neither route may include credentials, internal hostnames, reviewer queues, or user data. [L159] [L160] `/launch` is the human operator index for those artifacts. It links to `/launch.json`, `/share-kit.json`, `/distribution`, and feed endpoints while showing only public readiness status and boundary guidance. [L161] [L162] `/distribution` includes copy-ready controls for launch copy, public route URLs, atom URLs, atom text, and offer packets so social posts, newsletters, and partner handoffs do not require scraping rendered pages. [L163] [L164] Every public clip and story page also carries its own share panel: X, LinkedIn, email, social-card, copy URL, and copy text controls. [L165] [L166] `/principles` is the public trust page for the editorial firewall, funding boundary, correction visibility, and internal systems boundary. [L167] [L168] `/site.webmanifest` and `/icon.svg` provide branded install/bookmark metadata, public shortcuts, and share-target metadata without adding private configuration. [L169] [L170] Stable public conversion routes live at `/go/briefing`, `/go/membership`, and `/go/sponsor`. They preserve UTM parameters, redirect to hosted provider URLs when configured, and fall back to the matching public offer page while providers are pending. [L171] [L172] Set the provider env URLs to hosted signup, checkout, membership, or intake destinations; do not point them back to `/go/*`, `/briefing`, `/membership`, `/sponsor`, `/api/*`, or `/v1/*` on the public app. [L173] [L174] The public web app also serves a reader-safe same-origin API at `/v1/feed` and `/v1/story/:story_id`, so an initial public launch can use the web origin as `NEXT_PUBLIC_PUBLIC_API_URI` before a separate gateway is deployed. [L175] [L176] The public smoke gate also verifies response hardening headers and scans launch-critical responses for credential-like values or internal configuration names. [L177] [L178] The conversion boundary gate verifies that same-origin fake provider URLs stay pending, hosted provider URLs mark launch health ready, and `/go/*` redirects preserve UTM attribution. [L179] [L180] `pnpm launch:report` writes `docs/public-launch-report.md`, a current public launch handoff covering blockers, public routes, audience/revenue links, and internal-only values. [L181] [L182] See `docs/deployment-boundaries.md` for the public/internal split. [L183] See `docs/public-launch-checklist.md` for the public launch gate and revenue/audience readiness checks. [L184] [L185] Generate the public-only launch env template with: [L186] [L187] ```bash [L188] pnpm public-env:template > .env.public [L189] pnpm launch:local [L190] pnpm launch:report [L191] PUBLIC_ENV_FILE=.env.public PUBLIC_WEB_BASE_URL=https://recordswatch.org pnpm launch:deployed [L192] ``` [L193] [L194] The `.github/workflows/public-launch.yml` workflow runs the required public launch gates and database-backed conformance checks on pull requests and pushes. It also has a non-blocking container build job for packaging validation. [L195] The workflow uploads public launch report artifacts for both the default example env and the strict generated public env template. [L196] [L197] ### Event-Driven Pipeline [L198] [L199] State changes flow through a PostgreSQL-backed event outbox pattern: [L200] [L201] 1. **Ingest** — Evidence objects registered with content hashing (`sha256`) [L202] 2. **Extract** — Claims auto-extracted from narrative by model gateway [L203] 3. **Verify** — Tasks assigned to reviewers, structured verdicts collected [L204] 4. **Gate** — Policy-based publish gate evaluation (deterministic) [L205] 5. **Publish** — Story state updated + `story.published.v1` event emitted to outbox [L206] 6. **Correct** — Append-only corrections to the ledger (never delete, only add context) [L207] [L208] --- [L209] [L210] ## Database Design [L211] [L212] The PostgreSQL schema consists of 10 domain tables plus an event outbox, organized around the verification spine: [L213] [L214] ```sql [L215] -- Identity [L216] actors -- Platform participants with roles [L217... (truncated)

Citation Marker: fileciteturn77file0 [L1] encoding: utf-8 [L2] sha: a6702df51c4b5b12fb4dd3d0c769b2812aebb729 [L3] content: # application-pipeline [L4] [L5] [![Quality](https://github.com/4444j99/application-pipeline/actions/workflows/quality.yml/badge.svg)](https://github.com/4444j99/application-pipeline/actions/workflows/quality.yml) [L6] ![Python 3.11+](https://img.shields.io/badge/python-3.11%2B-blue) [L7] ![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/license-MIT-green) [L8] ![Tests: 1489](https://img.shields.io/badge/tests-1489%20passing-brightgreen) [L9] [L10] Career application infrastructure treating job, grant, and residency search as a **conversion pipeline**. Implements a "Cathedral → Storefront" philosophy: preserving deep, immersive systemic work while providing high-signal, scannable entry points for reviewers. [L11] [L12] ## Motivation [L13] [L14] Application processes waste enormous energy on redundant writing. This system treats narrative content as **reusable atoms** (blocks), composes them into **target-specific molecules** (submissions), tracks outcomes as **conversion data**, and uses that data to improve future applications. [L15] [L16] ## Quick Start [L17] [L18] ```bash [L19] pip install -e ".[dev]" # Install with dev dependencies [L20] make verify-quick # Deterministic local verification (python -m pytest) [L21] [L22] python scripts/run.py standup # Daily dashboard [L23] python scripts/run.py campaign # Deadline-aware campaign view [L24] python scripts/run.py score <id> # Score a single entry [L25] python scripts/run.py submit <id> # Generate portal-ready checklist [L26] python scripts/run.py --help # All 40+ commands [L27] ``` [L28] [L29] ## Pipeline State Machine [L30] [L31] ```mermaid [L32] stateDiagram-v2 [L33] [*] --> research [L34] research --> qualified : score ≥ threshold [L35] qualified --> drafting : blocks assigned [L36] drafting --> staged : content complete [L37] staged --> submitted : portal submission [L38] submitted --> acknowledged : confirmation received [L39] acknowledged --> interview : interview scheduled [L40] interview --> outcome : terminal result [L41] [L42] qualified --> deferred : blocked externally [L43] drafting --> deferred [L44] deferred --> qualified : re-activate [L45] deferred --> staged : re-activate [L46] [L47] outcome --> [*] [L48] [L49] note right of outcome [L50] accepted | rejected [L51] withdrawn | expired [L52] end note [L53] ``` [L54] [L55] ## Architecture [L56] [L57] ``` [L58] pipeline/ One YAML per application (state machine entries) [L59] active/ Actionable entries (research → staged) [L60] submitted/ Post-submission tracking [L61] closed/ Terminal outcomes [L62] research_pool/ Auto-sourced candidates (~940 entries) [L63] blocks/ Modular narrative building blocks (tiered: 60s / 2min / 5min / cathedral) [L64] variants/ A/B tracked material versions (cover letters, project descriptions) [L65] materials/ Resumes (base + tailored batches), CVs, work samples [L66] targets/ Target research, profiles (44 target-specific JSONs) [L67] signals/ Conversion analytics (logs, patterns, outreach) [L68] strategy/ Scoring rubric, identity positions, market intelligence [L69] scripts/ 52 Python CLI tools (all import from pipeline_lib.py) [L70] tests/ 1263 pytest tests [L71] docs/ Architecture rationale and workflow guides [L72] ``` [L73] [L74] ## Scoring Model [L75] [L76] Every entry is scored across 8 weighted dimensions: [L77] [L78] | Dimension | Weight | What it measures | [L79] |-----------|--------|------------------| [L80] | Mission Alignment | 20% | How well the opportunity matches identity position | [L81] | Evidence Match | 15% | Strength of portfolio evidence for this target | [L82] | Track Record Fit | 15% | Relevant experience alignment | [L83] | Financial Alignment | 10% | Compensation / grant amount viability | [L84] | Effort to Value | 15% | Time investment vs. potential return | [L85] | Strategic Value | 10% | Network effects, learning, positioning | [L86] | Deadline Feasibility | 10% | Can we produce quality work in time? | [L87] | Portal Friction | 5% | Submission complexity and platform friction | [L88] [L89] ## Identity Positions [L90] [L91] Every application aligns to one of five canonical positions: [L92] [L93] 1. **Independent Engineer** — AI lab roles. Focus: infrastructure, testing, AI-conductor methodology [L94] 2. **Systems Artist** — Art grants/residencies. Focus: governance as artwork, systemic scale [L95] 3. **Educator** — Academic roles. Focus: teaching complex systems at scale [L96] 4. **Creative Technologist** — Tech grants/consulting. Focus: AI orchestration, creative instruments [L97] 5. **Community Practitioner** — Identity-specific funding. Focus: precarity-informed systemic practice [L98] [L99] ## Session Sequences [L100] [L101] | Workflow | Commands | [L102] |----------|----------| [L103] | **Morning** | `standup` → `followup` → `outcomes` → `campaign` | [L104] | **Submit** | `campaign` → `check <id>` → `submit <id>` → `record <id>` | [L105] | **Research** | `hygiene` → `scoreall` → `qualify` → `enrichall` | [L106] | **Analyze** | `funnel` → `conversion` → `velocity` → `dashboard` | [L107] | **Strategy** | `startup` → `funding` → `differentiate` → `tracker` | [L108] [L109] ## Development [L110] [L111] ```bash [L112] make verify # Full verification gates (matrix + lint + validate + full tests) [L113] make verify-quick # Faster local verification loop [L114] make preflight # Staged preflight gate [L115] python scripts/verification_matrix.py --strict # Module-to-verification route coverage [L116] python -m ruff check scripts/ tests/ # Lint only [L117] python -m pytest tests/ -v # Full test suite only [L118] python scripts/validate.py # YAML schema validation [L119] ``` [L120] [L121] CI runs on every push/PR via `.github/workflows/quality.yml` with a Python 3.11/3.12 matrix; scheduled full regression also uploads verification artifacts. [L122] [L123] ## License [L124] [L125] [MIT](LICENSE) [L126] [L127] <!-- SYSTEM-NAV-START --> [L128] [L129] --- [L130] [L131] <sub>[Portfolio](https://4444j99.github.io/portfolio/) · [System Directory](https://4444j99.github.io/portfolio/directory/) · [ORGAN 4444J99](https://4444J99.github.io/) · Part of the <a href="https://4444j99.github.io/portfolio/directory/">ORGANVM eight-organ system</a></sub> [L132] [L133] <!-- SYSTEM-NAV-END -->

[L6] [L7] [![License: MIT](https://img.shields.io/badge/License-MIT-blue.svg)](LICENSE) [L8] [![TypeScript](https://img.shields.io/badge/TypeScript-5.9-3178c6?logo=typescript&logoColor=white)](https://www.typescriptlang.org/) [L9] [![Tests: 3,399](https://img.shields.io/badge/tests-3%2C399%20passing-brightgreen)](https://github.com/organvm-iii-ergon/public-record-data-scrapper) [L10] [![Deploy: Vercel](https://img.shields.io/badge/deploy-Vercel-black?logo=vercel)](https://public-record-data-scrapper.vercel.app) [L11] [L12] --- [L13] [L14] ## What It Does [L15] [L16] 1. **Collects** UCC-1 filing data from state Secretary of State portals — 4 collectors implemented (CA API, TX bulk, FL vendor, NY portal scraper) with per-state strategies (API, bulk download, vendor feed, scrape) and fallback. FL and NY are credential-gated and fail closed when unconfigured. [L17] 2. **Enriches** each filing with free public data (SEC EDGAR, OSHA, USPTO, Census Bureau) plus optional, key-gated sources (SAM.gov, D&B, Clearbit, ZoomInfo) that fail closed — returning a named error, never fabricated data — when no API key is configured [L18] 3. **Scores** every prospect 0--100 on financing likelihood, assigns a health grade (A--F), and flags growth signals (hiring, permits, equipment purchases, expansion) [L19] 4. **Delivers** results through a React web dashboard, REST API, or CLI tool [L20] [L21] ### Example Output [L22] [L23] ```json [L24] { [L25] "company": "Pacific Coast Distributors LLC", [L26] "state": "CA", [L27] "ucc_filings": [ [L28] { [L29] "filing_number": "2024-0847291", [L30] "secured_party": "National Funding Inc", [L31] "filing_date": "2024-03-15", [L32] "type": "UCC-1" [L33] } [L34] ], [L35] "enrichment": { [L36] "revenue_estimate": "$2.4M", [L37] "employee_count": 34, [L38] "growth_signals": ["hiring_detected", "new_permits", "equipment_purchase"], [L39] "health_grade": "B+", [L40] "priority_score": 82, [L41] "industry": "Wholesale Distribution" [L42] }, [L43] "recommendation": "HIGH PRIORITY - Active financing, strong growth signals, clean compliance record" [L44] } [L45] ``` [L46] [L47] --- [L48] [L49] ## Usage [L50] [L51] ```bash [L52] git clone https://github.com/organvm/public-record-data-scrapper.git [L53] cd public-record-data-scrapper [L54] npm ci [L55] ``` [L56] [L57] The root package is a private npm workspace. It does not declare a top-level `bin` [L58] or package export; use the npm scripts below. [L59] [L60] ### Run the app [L61] [L62] The web app runs from `apps/web` through the root workspace script: [L63] [L64] ```bash [L65] npm run dev [L66] ``` [L67] [L68] Vite is configured to bind `127.0.0.1:5173`. [L69] [L70] The API and worker read configuration from process environment variables. The [L71] server requires `JWT_SECRET` at startup. To use the local Docker database and [L72] Redis services: [L73] [L74] ```bash [L75] docker-compose up -d db redis [L76] [L77] export DATABASE_URL=postgresql://postgres:postgres@localhost:5432/ucc_mca [L78] export REDIS_URL=redis://localhost:6379 [L79] export JWT_SECRET=replace-with-local-secret [L80] [L81] npm run db:migrate [L82] npm run seed [L83] [L84] npm run dev:server # API only [L85] npm run dev:worker # Worker only [L86] npm run dev:full # Web + API + worker [L87] ``` [L88] [L89] Health checks (running API on default `3000`): [L90] [L91] ```bash [L92] curl -fsS http://localhost:3000/api/health # Basic liveness [L93] curl -fsS http://localhost:3000/api/health/detailed # Dependency status [L94] ``` [L95] [L96] Other root run/build entrypoints: [L97] [L98] ```bash [L99] npm run preview # Preview the built web app [L100] npm run build # Build apps/web into dist/ [L101] npm run build:server # Bundle dist/server.cjs and dist/worker.cjs [L102] npm run build:render # Build web + server/worker bundles [L103] npm start # Run dist/server.cjs [L104] npm run start:worker # Run dist/worker.cjs [L105] npm run dev:desktop # Run apps/desktop dev script [L106] npm run dev:mobile # Run apps/mobile Expo start script [L107] ``` [L108] [L109] ### CLI tools [L110] [L111] The CLI is registered in `scripts/cli-scraper.ts` and executed as: [L112] [L113] ```bash [L114] npm run scrape -- <command> [flags] [L115] ``` [L116] [L117] See command-level help with: [L118] [L119] ```bash [L120] npm run scrape -- --help [L121] npm run scrape -- scrape-ucc --help [L122] ``` [L123] [L124] The CLI supports `CA`, `TX`, `FL`, and `NY` for commands that validate state [L125] through `SUPPORTED_CLI_STATES`. [L126] [L127] ```bash [L128] # Scrape UCC filings for a company [L129] npm run scrape -- scrape-ucc -c "Company Name" -s CA -o ./results.json [L130] # required: -c|--company <name>, -s|--state <code> [L131] # optional: -o|--output <file> (default: ./output.json), --csv [L132] # supported states: CA, TX, FL, NY [L133] [L134] # Normalize one company name [L135] npm run scrape -- normalize -n "Company Name" [L136] # required: -n|--name <name> [L137] [L138] # Enrich from public sources [L139] npm run scrape -- enrich -c "Company Name" -s CA --tier professional -o ./enriched-data.json [L140] # required: -c|--company <name>, -s|--state <code> [L141] # optional: -o|--output <file> (default: ./enriched-data.json), --tier <free|starter|professional>, --csv [L142] # supported states: CA, TX, FL, NY [L143] [L144] # Batch process CSV input [L145] npm run scrape -- batch -i ./companies.csv -o ./batch-results [L146] # required: -i|--input <file> (CSV header + rows company,state) [L147] # optional: -o|--output <dir> (default: ./batch-results) [L148] # max 1,000 rows; input file must be 5,242,880 bytes or smaller [L149] # note: --enrich is accepted by the parser but the batch loop only scrapes UCC filings [L150] [L151] # Export scored leads (database-backed) [L152] npm run scrape -- lead-export --min-score 70 --max-score 95 --state CA --limit 100 --offset 0 --output-dir ./lead-export [L153] # optional: -o|--output-dir <dir> (default: ./lead-export) [L154] # optional: --format <json|csv|both> (default: both) [L155] # optional: --min-score <0-100> (default: 70), --max-score <0-100> [L156] # optional: --state <CA|TX|FL|NY>, --industry <name>, --status <status> [L157] # optional: --limit <1-1000> (default: 100), --offset <integer> (default: 0) [L158] [L159] # List available states with configured UCC collectors [L160] npm run scrape -- list-states [L161] ``` [L162] [L163] The scheduled scraper is a separate script: [L164] [L165] ```bash [L166] npm run scrape:scheduled # Hard-coded sample companies for CA, TX, FL, NY, IL [L167] npm run scrape:scheduled -- --dry-run # Print the batch instead of writing output/ [L168] ``` [L169] [L170] It uses `SCRAPER_IMPLEMENTATION` when set (`mock`, `puppeteer`, or `api`); [L171] otherwise `scripts/scrapers/scraper-factory.ts` chooses a recommended [L172] implementation from the current environment. [L173] [L174] ### API entrypoints [L175] [L176] The Express server exposes Swagger UI at `/api/docs` and raw OpenAPI at [L177] `/api/docs/openapi.json` and `/api/docs/openapi.yaml`. [L178] [L179] The on-demand UCC API is mounted behind API-key-or-JWT auth: [L180] [L181] ```bash [L182] # Check whether a state scraper is available [L183] curl -H "Authorization: Bearer $TOKEN" http://localhost:3000/api/scrape/readiness/CA [L184] [L185] # Synchronous search — waits for results (suitable for small queries) [L186] curl -X POST http://localhost:3000/api/scrape/ucc \ [L187] -H "Authorization: Bearer $TOKEN" \ [L188] -H "Content-Type: application/json" \ [L189] -d '{"company_name":"Company Name","state":"CA","limit":100}' [L190] [L191] # Async search — returns 202 immediately with a jobId (use for large or slow queries) [L192] curl -X POST http://localhost:3000/api/scrape/jobs \ [L193] -H "Authorization: Bearer $TOKEN" \ [L194] -H "Content-Type: application/json" \ [L195] -d '{"company_name":"Company Name","state":"CA","limit":500}' [L196] # → {"data":{"jobId":"<uuid>","status":"pending","pollUrl":"/api/scrape/jobs/<uuid>"},...} [L197] [L198] # Poll until status is "completed" or "failed" [L199] curl -H "Authorization: Bearer $TOKEN" http://localhost:3000/api/scrape/jobs/<jobId> [L200] ``` [L201] [L202] ### Workspace exports [L203] [L204] Internal workspace packages expose source entrypoints for other workspace code: [L205] [L206] ```text [L207] @public-records/core [L208] . -> packages/core/src/index.ts [L209] ./database -> packages/core/src/database.ts [L210] ./identity -> packages/core/src/identity.ts [L211] ./types -> packages/core/src/types.ts [L212] ./enrichment -> packages/core/src/enrichment/index.ts [L213] [L214] @public-records/ui [L215] . and component subpaths such as ./button, ./card, ./dialog, ./table, [L216] ./tooltip, ./utils, and the other subpaths declared in packages/ui/package.json [L217] ``` [L218] [L219] --- [L220] [L221] ##
